Moed Katan
מועד קטן
William Davidson Edition - English
https://korenpub.com/collections/the-noe-edition-koren-talmud-bavli-1

Moed Katan



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One may irrigate a field that requires irrigation on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival as well as during the Sabbatical Year, both from a newly emerged spring</b> that began to flow only during the Festival, <b>and from a spring that did not just emerge</b> and that has been flowing for some time. <b>However, one may not irrigate</b> a field <b>with rainwater</b> collected in a cistern, a procedure that requires excessive exertion, <b>or with water drawn with a shadoof [<i>kilon</i>],</b> a lever used to raise water with a bucket from deep down in a well.
<b>And one may not construct circular ditches</b> around the bases of <b>grapevines</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: One may not construct a new</b> water <b>channel during</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival or during the Sabbatical Year. And the Rabbis say: One may construct a new</b> water <b>channel during the Sabbatical Year and one may repair damaged</b> water channels <b>during</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival.</b>
In addition to performing labor on one’s own property in order to avoid financial loss, it is also permitted to perform labor on the intermediate days of a Festival for the public welfare: <b>One may repair damaged water</b> cisterns <b>that are in the public domain, and clean them out</b> by removing the dirt and sediment that accumulated there; <b>one may repair roads, streets, and ritual baths; and one may tend to all</b> other <b>public needs.</b> So too, <b>one may mark graves</b> to inform the public of their ritual impurity, <b>and</b> inspectors <b>may even go out to</b> uproot <b>the</b> shoots of prohibited <b>diverse kinds</b> [<b><i>kilayim</i>]</b> that grew in the fields during the rainy season.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara begins by questioning the wording of the mishna: <b>Now</b> that <b>it has been said</b> that on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>one may irrigate</b> a field <b>from a newly emerged spring,</b> whose walls have not yet stabilized and are <b>likely to collapse,</b> necessitating laborious repairs, <b>is it necessary</b> to mention that one may irrigate a field <b>from a spring that did not just emerge,</b> whose walls have already stabilized <b>and are</b> therefore <b>not likely to collapse?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>They say</b> that <b>it was necessary</b> to mention the second case as well. For <b>had</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> us the <i>halakha</i> with regard to only <b>a newly emerged spring, I would have said</b> that <b>here,</b> in the case of <b>a field that requires irrigation, yes,</b> one is permitted to irrigate from such a spring, but in the case of <b>a field that</b> ordinarily <b>suffices with rainwater, no,</b> one is not permitted to do so, <b>because it is likely to collapse. But</b> with regard to <b>a spring that did not just emerge, that is not likely to collapse, I</b> might <b>say that</b> one may provide supplementary irrigation <b>even</b> in the case of <b>a field that</b> ordinarily <b>suffices with rainwater.</b>
Therefore, the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches us</b> that <b>a newly emerged spring is no different</b> from <b>a spring that did not just emerge.</b> In the case of <b>a field that requires irrigation, yes,</b> one may irrigate on the intermediate days of a Festival, while in the case of <b>a field that</b> ordinarily <b>suffices with rainwater, no,</b> one may not do so, even from an established spring.
The Gemara raises a question with regard to a linguistic issue: <b>And from where</b> may it <b>be inferred that this</b> term, <b><i>beit hashelaḥin</i>,</b> a field that requires irrigation, <b>is a term</b> denoting <b>thirst,</b> implying that supplementary watering is necessary? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “And you were faint and weary”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:18). The term faint is referring to the thirst of the Israelites in the desert. <b>And,</b> in the standard Aramaic translation, <b>we translate</b> the verse as: <b>And you were thirsty [<i>meshalhei</i>] and weary.</b> The letters <i>ḥet</i> and <i>heh</i> are sometimes interchanged, and therefore the term <i>beit hashelaḥin</i> connotes a thirsty field.
<b>And from where</b> may it <b>be inferred that this</b> term, <b><i>beit haba’al</i>,</b> a field that suffices with rainwater, <b>is a term denoting settlement,</b> i.e., an established field that does not require extensive upkeep? <b>As it is written: “For as a young man takes to himself [<i>yiv’al</i>] a virgin,</b> so shall your sons take you to themselves” (Isaiah 62:5). <b>And it is translated</b> in the Aramaic translation: <b>As a young man settles down with a virgin, so shall your sons become settled within you.</b> Similarly, <i>beit haba’al</i> is referring to a settled field that suffices with rainwater.
The Gemara begins to clarify the underlying principle of the mishna, asking: <b>Who is the</b> anonymous <b><i>tanna</i></b> of the mishna who maintains <b>that</b> labor performed to prevent a considerable <b>loss,</b> such as watering a field that requires irrigation, <b>yes,</b> it is permitted on the intermediate days of a Festival; but labor performed to increase one’s <b>profit,</b> such as watering a field that ordinarily suffices with rainwater, <b>no,</b> it is not permitted? Furthermore, <b>even in a case</b> involving <b>loss, one may not</b> excessively <b>exert</b> oneself, as the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna renders prohibited all cases of watering fields with collected rainwater or with water drawn with a shadoof, even in a field that requires irrigation.
<b>Rav Huna said: It is Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov, as we learned</b> in a mishna: <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says:</b> In a field filled with trees, <b>one may draw water</b> via channels <b>from tree to tree, provided that</b> in doing so <b>he does not water the entire field.</b> As this field ordinarily suffices with rainwater, it is prohibited to water the entire field. Therefore, it is evident that Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov renders prohibited work performed to increase profit on the intermediate days of a Festival.
The Gemara challenges this comparison: <b>Say that you heard</b> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> ben Ya’akov holds that labor performed only to increase <b>profit is not</b> permitted. But <b>did you hear him</b> prohibit excessive <b>exertion in a case of</b> considerable <b>loss?</b> This aspect of the mishna finds no expression in the words of Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov.
<b>Rather, Rav Pappa said:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>From a newly emerged spring one may irrigate even a field that</b> ordinarily <b>suffices with rainwater;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yehuda says: One may irrigate only a field requiring irrigation that dried up</b> and needs water. <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: Neither the one nor the other. Furthermore, Rabbi Yehuda said:</b> Owing to the exertion involved, <b>one may not divert a water channel</b> from its regular path in order <b>to water his garden or his ruin,</b> which is now being used for planting, <b>during the intermediate days of a Festival.</b>
The Gemara first clarifies the case of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>What</b> does Rabbi Yehuda mean when he speaks of a field that <b>is dried up? If we say</b> that the field is <b>literally dried up</b> and the plants are already parched, <b>why do I</b> need <b>to water it? Abaye said:</b> It means that <b>the one spring</b> from which the field had been irrigated until now <b>dried up, but</b> in the meantime <b>another spring emerged.</b> If the field is not irrigated from this spring, it will be ruined.
The Gemara explains the next clause of the <i>baraita</i>, which states: <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: Neither the one nor the other.</b> By this he means that <b>it is no different</b> whether <b>the</b> original <b>spring dried up or did not dry up.</b> The guiding principle is: From <b>a newly emerged spring</b> one may <b>not</b> irrigate even a field that requires irrigation. In any event, Rabbi Yehuda seems to maintain an opinion that is like that of the mishna, i.e., that only a field that requires irrigation may be watered, but not a field that suffices with rainwater. And even in the case of a field that requires irrigation, excessive exertion is prohibited. 
The Gemara challenges this understanding: <b>And from where</b> do you conclude that the mishna reflects the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda? <b>Perhaps Rabbi Yehuda stated</b> that <b>a field that requires irrigation, yes,</b> one may irrigate it on the intermediate days of a Festival, and <b>a field that suffices with rainwater, no,</b> one may not do so, <b>only</b> with regard to <b>a newly emerged spring,</b>

Daf 2b

<b>as it is likely to collapse. But</b> in the case of <b>a spring that did not just emerge, which is not likely to collapse</b> because its walls have already stabilized, <b>even a field that</b> ordinarily <b>suffices with rainwater</b> may be irrigated.
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> that were <b>so,</b> then <b>to whom,</b> i.e., to which <i>tanna</i>, <b>would you attribute</b> the mishna? According to this interpretation, the mishna does not correspond to any opinion, while according to the previous interpretation, it corresponds to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda. <b>Rather,</b> it must be that according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, a newly emerged spring is no different</b> from <b>a spring that did not just emerge. A field that requires irrigation, yes,</b> it may be irrigated, while <b>a field that suffices with rainwater, no,</b> it may not be irrigated. <b>And</b> the <i>baraita</i> opted <b>to teach</b> this dispute in the case of <b>a newly emerged spring</b> in order <b>to convey the far-reaching nature of</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Meir,</b> which states that <b>one may irrigate even from a newly emerged spring,</b> and <b>even a field that suffices with rainwater.</b>
§ <b>It was stated</b> that the <i>amora’im</i> disputed the following question: With regard to <b>one who weeds or one who waters seedlings on Shabbat, for what</b> prohibited labor <b>do we forewarn him?</b> Judicial punishment may be administered to a sinner only if he has been forewarned by two witnesses prior to the commission of his offense. This forewarning must include the specific transgression being violated, and on Shabbat it must include the specific category of prohibited labor that the action involves. <b>Rabba said:</b> It is <b>due to</b> the prohibition against <b>plowing. Rav Yosef said:</b> It is <b>due to</b> the prohibition against <b>sowing.</b>
<b>Rabba said: According to my opinion</b> it <b>is reasonable. Just as the</b> usual objective <b>of plowing is to loosen the earth, so too, this,</b> weeding or watering, <b>loosens the earth. Rav Yosef said: According to my</b> opinion, it <b>is reasonable. Just as the</b> usual objective <b>of sowing is to cause the fruit to grow, here too,</b> weeding or watering <b>causes the fruit to grow.</b>
<b>Abaye said to Rabba: According to your</b> opinion, it is <b>difficult, and according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Yosef,</b> it is <b>difficult.</b> Abaye explains: <b>According to your</b> opinion, it is <b>difficult:</b> Is it true that <b>for</b> the prohibition against <b>plowing, yes,</b> he is forewarned, but <b>for</b> the prohibition against <b>sowing, no,</b> he is not forewarned? Similarly, <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Yosef, it</b> is <b>difficult:</b> Can it be that <b>for</b> the prohibition against <b>sowing, yes,</b> he is forewarned, but <b>for</b> the prohibition against <b>plowing, no,</b> he is not forewarned? Everyone should agree that weeding and watering fall under the categories of both plowing and sowing.
<b>And if you say that anywhere that there are two</b> possible categories of prohibited labor into which a particular action might fall, <b>one is liable</b> to be punished <b>for only one</b> of them, <b>didn’t Rav Kahana say:</b> If <b>one prunes</b> the branches of a vine on Shabbat <b>and he needs the wood</b> for firewood or any other purpose, <b>he is liable</b> to bring <b>two</b> sin-offerings? He is liable to bring <b>one</b> sin-offering <b>for</b> violating the primary category of <b>sowing,</b> as pruning vines facilitates their growth, and so it is a sub-category of sowing. <b>And</b> he is liable to bring <b>one</b> sin-offering <b>for</b> violating the primary category of <b>reaping,</b> as the essence of reaping is detaching that which one needs from the ground. Since he needs the wood that he is detaching from the vine, his action is considered reaping. Consequently, one action that incorporates two prohibited labors causes liability for both.
The Gemara concludes: Indeed, it is <b>difficult</b> according to both opinions.
<b>Rav Yosef raised an objection to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabba</b> from what is taught in the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who weeds</b> in proximity to diverse kinds of seeds, <b>or covers</b> with earth the exposed roots of <b>diverse kinds, is flogged</b> for transgressing the prohibition against diverse kinds, i.e., tending different species of crops in one area of the same field, as it is stated: “You shall not sow your field with two kinds of seed” (Leviticus 19:19). <b>Rabbi Akiva says: Even one who maintains</b> in his field a mixture of species that he could have uprooted is flogged for violating the prohibition against diverse kinds of seeds.
Rav Yosef explains: <b>Granted, according to my</b> opinion, <b>that I say</b> that one who weeds on Shabbat is forewarned <b>for</b> the prohibited labor of <b>sowing, this is</b> the reason he is liable to be punished with flogging for weeding diverse seeds: <b>Sowing diverse kinds is prohibited. But according to you, who said</b> that one who weeds on Shabbat is forewarned <b>for plowing,</b> why is one liable to be flogged for weeding in proximity to diverse kinds? <b>Is plowing prohibited</b> in connection with <b>diverse kinds?</b> At the time of plowing, there is no mixture of different species of crops, so plowing cannot be prohibited in this case.
Rabba <b>said to him:</b> According to my opinion, one who weeds a field of diverse kinds is flogged not because he is guilty of plowing, but <b>for</b> violating the prohibition against <b>maintaining</b> a mixture of species in his field.
The Gemara objects: <b>However, from</b> the fact <b>that it teaches in the latter clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: Even one who maintains</b> in his field a mixture of species is liable to be flogged, <b>it may be inferred that</b> according to <b>the</b> anonymous <b>first <i>tanna</i>,</b> the liability for weeding is <b>not for maintaining</b> diverse kinds, but for performing some other prohibited labor.
The Gemara rejects this opinion: In fact, <b>the entire</b> <i>baraita</i> reflects the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, and it is stated</b> in the style of <b>what is the reason.</b> The <i>baraita</i> should be understood as follows: <b>What is the reason that one who weeds and one who covers</b> the exposed roots of <b>diverse kinds</b> with earth <b>is flogged?</b> He is flogged <b>for maintaining</b> diverse kinds in his field, <b>as Rabbi Akiva says: Even one who maintains</b> in his field a mixture of species is flogged.
With regard to Rabbi Akiva’s opinion, the Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for <b>Rabbi Akiva’s</b> opinion? How did he derive this prohibition from the verses? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“You shall not sow your field with mingled seed”</b> (Leviticus 19:19). <b>I have</b> derived from this verse that <b>only sowing</b> diverse kinds is prohibited. <b>From where</b> is it derived that <b>maintaining</b> diverse kinds, which does not involve any positive action, is also prohibited?
<b>The verse states: “Diverse kinds, you shall not</b> sow <b>your field</b> with mingled seed” (Leviticus 19:19). The prohibition against planting different species of a crop in one area of the same field is preceded in the verse by the prohibition against crossbreeding livestock: “You shall not let your cattle gender with a diverse kind.” A slight change in punctuation yields the phrase: Diverse kinds in your field, you shall not, indicating that merely preserving diverse kinds in one’s field is prohibited.
§ The Gemara returns to the original topic of discussion: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: <b>One may irrigate a field that requires irrigation on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival as well as during the Sabbatical Year.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> with regard to the intermediate days of <b>a Festival,</b> where the prohibition against irrigation is <b>due to</b> the mandate to avoid excessive <b>exertion</b> on the Festival, <b>in a case of</b> considerable <b>loss, the Sages permitted</b> one to exert himself. <b>However,</b> during the <b>Sabbatical Year, both according to</b> the <b>one who said</b> that one who waters is liable <b>due to</b> the prohibition against <b>sowing, and according to the one who said</b> that one is liable <b>due to</b> the prohibition against <b>plowing, are sowing and plowing permitted during the Sabbatical Year?</b> How can these actions be permitted when the Torah explicitly renders prohibited working the land during the Sabbatical Year?
<b>Abaye said:</b> The mishna is referring <b>to the Sabbatical Year in the present time,</b> when its prohibitions are only by rabbinic decree, <b>and it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> When the verse states: <b>“And this is the manner of the release,</b> every creditor <b>will release</b> that which he has lent to his neighbor” (Deuteronomy 15:2), <b>the verse speaks of two releases: One</b> is <b>the release of land,</b> that one must refrain from working the land, <b>and the other</b> is <b>the release of money,</b> that one must refrain from collecting debts.
This verse equates these two releases, indicating that <b>when you</b> are mandated by Torah law to <b>release land, you</b> must <b>release money, and when you</b> are <b>not</b> mandated to <b>release land, you need not release money.</b> This indicates that according to Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, at the present time, the release of land is not mandated by Torah law. Therefore, observance of the Sabbatical Year is mandated only by rabbinic law, and the Sages were lenient in a case of significant loss.
<b>Rava said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the mishna was taught in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi and maintain that the observance of the Sabbatical Year, even at the present time, is mandated by Torah law, one can understand the leniency in the mishna. The reason for the leniency with regard to irrigation is because only <b>primary categories of labor were prohibited by the Merciful One,</b> i.e., by Torah law,

Daf 3a

whereas <b>the subcategories</b> of labor that are derived from them, e.g., watering, <b>were not prohibited by the Merciful One,</b> i.e., by Torah law, but only by rabbinic law; and in a case of loss the Sages were lenient. The source for this distinction is <b>as it is written: “But in the seventh year shall be a sabbath of solemn rest for the land,</b> a sabbath for the Lord: <b>Your field you shall not sow,</b> and your vineyard you shall not prune. That which grows of its own accord of your harvest you shall not reap, nor gather the grapes of your undressed vines” (Leviticus 25:4–5).
<b>Since pruning is included in</b> the principal category of <b>sowing,</b> as its objective is to encourage the plant’s growth, <b>and picking</b> grapes <b>is included in</b> the principal category of <b>reaping</b> grain, as both involve removal of produce from a plant, <b>for</b> the purpose of teaching <b>what <i>halakha</i> did the Merciful One write them?</b> Why did the Torah explicitly prohibit pruning and picking grapes, rather than sufficing with the general prohibitions against sowing and reaping?
The Gemara answers that these were individually enumerated <b>to say</b> that only <b>for these subcategories</b> of labor <b>is one liable</b> to be flogged, while <b>for other</b> subcategories of labor, i.e., watering a field, <b>one is not liable.</b> Consequently, there are only four types of labor that are prohibited by Torah law during the Sabbatical Year: Sowing and its subcategory of pruning, as well as reaping and its subcategory of grape picking. All other subcategories of labor are prohibited only by rabbinic decree.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is there really <b>no</b> liability for other subcategories of labor? <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“Your field you shall not sow, and your vineyard you shall not prune”</b> (Leviticus 25:4). <b>I have</b> derived from here an explicit prohibition <b>only</b> against <b>sowing and pruning. From where</b> do I derive that <b>weeding,</b> i.e., uprooting weeds, <b>hoeing, and cutting weeds,</b> even without uprooting them, are also prohibited? <b>The verse states: “Your field</b> you shall <b>not</b> sow” and <b>“your vineyard</b> you shall <b>not</b> prune.” This wording indicates that <b>no labor</b> may be performed <b>in your field, and no labor</b> may be performed <b>in your vineyard.</b>
Similarly, <b>from where</b> is it derived that <b>one may not prune trees, and one may not trim dry branches</b> from trees, <b>and one</b> may <b>not cut large branches [<i>mefasegin</i>] from trees? The verse states: “Your field</b> you shall <b>not</b> sow” and <b>“your vineyard</b> you shall <b>not</b> prune,” thereby teaching that <b>no labor</b> may be performed <b>in your field, and no labor</b> may be performed <b>in your vineyard.</b>
Similarly, <b>from where</b> is it derived that <b>one</b> may <b>not fertilize</b> fields and vineyards, <b>and one may not remove stones</b> that surround the base of a tree and impede its growth, <b>and one</b> may <b>not</b> cover exposed roots with <b>dust, and one may not fumigate a tree</b> in order to exterminate worms? <b>The verse states: “Your field</b> you shall <b>not</b> sow” and <b>“your vineyard</b> you shall <b>not</b> prune,” thereby indicating that <b>no labor</b> may be performed <b>in your field, and no labor</b> may be performed <b>in your vineyard.</b>
The apparent conclusion from this is that all labor in a field or a vineyard is prohibited. One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one may</b> also <b>not hoe lightly under olive</b> trees <b>and one may not hoe under grapevines, and one may not fill cracks</b> in the ground <b>with water, and one may not construct circular ditches</b> around the bases of <b>grapevines</b> in order to collect rainwater. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Your field you shall not sow.”</b>
The Gemara explains the derivation: <b>Sowing was included</b> in the general prohibition against performing agricultural labors during the Sabbatical Year, as the verse states “But in the seventh year shall be a sabbath of solemn rest for the land” (Leviticus 25:4). <b>Why was</b> sowing <b>singled out</b> and mentioned explicitly? In order <b>to compare</b> other types of labor <b>to it</b> and to <b>say to you: Just as sowing is unique in that it is labor</b> performed both <b>in the field and in the vineyard, so too, any</b> other <b>labor</b> performed both <b>in the field and in the vineyard</b> is prohibited. However, labors performed only in the vineyard but not in the field, i.e., whose objective is merely to sustain the vines from year to year, such as the labors mentioned above, are permitted. In any case, this <i>baraita</i> indicates that many subcategories of labor are prohibited during the seventh year, and not just sowing and pruning, as Rava had stated.
The Gemara rejects this opinion: These labors, with the exceptions of sowing, pruning, grape picking, and reaping, are all prohibited only <b>by rabbinic law, and the verse</b> that is cited as a source from the Torah <b>is a mere support,</b> and not a bona fide source.
Incidental to this discussion, the Gemara asks: <b>Is</b> light <b>hoeing</b> under olive trees in fact <b>permitted during the Sabbatical Year? Isn’t it written: “But the seventh year you shall let it rest and lie fallow”</b> (Exodus 23:11), and it is taught with regard to this verse: <b>“You shall let it rest” from light hoeing, “and lie fallow” from clearing</b> the field of <b>stones.</b> This indicates that light hoeing is indeed prohibited during the Sabbatical Year.
<b>Rav Ukva bar Ḥama said: There are two</b> types of light <b>hoeing, one</b> whose objective is <b>to strengthen</b> the <b>trees, and another</b> that is intended <b>to seal cracks.</b> There is a practical halakhic difference between them: Hoeing performed in order <b>to strengthen the tree is prohibited,</b> as it is similar to plowing in that it enhances the tree’s growth. However, hoeing undertaken in order <b>to seal cracks is permitted,</b> as by doing so one prevents damage to the tree.
§ <b>It was stated</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Elazar</b> disagreed with regard to <b>one who plows during the Sabbatical Year. One said: He is flogged</b> for doing so, <b>while the</b> other <b>one said: He is not flogged.</b> The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say that they disagree with regard to</b> the principle that <b>Rabbi Avin</b> said that <b>Rabbi Ile’a said,</b> and one accepts this principle while the other does not. <b>As, Rabbi Avin said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ile’a said: Wherever a generalization is stated</b> in the Torah <b>as a positive</b> mitzva, <b>and a detail</b> relating to that generalization is stated <b>as a negative</b> mitzva, <b>one does not apply</b> the hermeneutical principle of <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization,</b> according to which the <i>halakha</i> under discussion is expanded to all cases that resemble the detail.
The Gemara explains: <b>The one who said</b> that one who plows during the Sabbatical Year <b>is flogged does not</b> accept the principle <b>that Rabbi Avin</b> said that <b>Rabbi Ile’a said.</b> Accordingly, he expounds the verses as follows: The verse “But in the seventh year shall be a sabbath of solemn rest for the land” (Leviticus 25:4) is a generalization stated as a positive mitzva. The continuation of the verse is “Your field you shall not sow,” which is a detail stated as a negative mitzva. This is followed by another generalization: “For it shall be a year of rest for the land” (Leviticus 25:5). By applying the principle of a generalization, a detail, and a generalization, one can derive that there is a prohibition against performing any agricultural task that is similar to sowing, including plowing.
<b>And the one who said</b> that <b>one is not flogged</b> for plowing <b>holds</b> in accordance with the ruling <b>that Rabbi Avin</b> stated in the name of Rabbi Ile’a. Therefore, the prohibition stated as a detail, i.e., the prohibition against sowing, is not extended to include any other labor. One who plows violates the positive command to let the land rest, but does not transgress a Torah law phrased as a negative mitzva, and therefore he is not flogged.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>No,</b> one can say that <b>everyone</b> holds that the <i>halakha</i> <b>is not</b> in accordance with the principle <b>that Rabbi Avin</b> said that <b>Rabbi Ile’a said.</b> According to <b>the one who said he is flogged,</b> all is <b>well,</b> as he applies the hermeneutical principle of a generalization, a detail, and a generalization to derive a prohibition for plowing.
<b>And the one who said</b> that <b>he is not flogged</b> could have <b>said to you: Since pruning is</b> included <b>in</b> the primary category of <b>sowing, and</b> grape <b>picking is</b> included in the primary category <b>of reaping, for</b> the purpose of teaching <b>what <i>halakha</i> did the Merciful One write them?</b> They were individually enumerated <b>to say</b> that only <b>for these</b> specific <b>subcategories</b> of labor <b>is one liable</b> to be flogged, but <b>for</b> performing <b>other subcategories</b> of labor <b>one is not liable.</b> According to this opinion, the verse comes to teach that one is not liable to be flogged for performing any labor not explicitly enumerated in the Torah, including plowing.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is there really <b>no</b> liability for other subcategories of labor? <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse <b>“Your field you shall not sow, and your vineyard you shall not prune”</b> that <b>I have</b> derived from here an explicit prohibition <b>only</b> against <b>sowing and pruning? From where</b> do I derive that <b>weeding, hoeing, and cutting weeds</b> are also prohibited? <b>The verse states: “Your field</b> you shall <b>not</b> sow and <b>your vineyard</b> you shall <b>not</b> prune” (Leviticus 25:4). By placing the word “not” immediately following the words “field” and “vineyard,” the verse indicates that one may <b>not</b> perform <b>any labor that</b> is generally performed <b>in your field, and</b> one may <b>not</b> perform <b>any labor that</b> is generally performed <b>in your vineyard.</b>
Similarly, <b>from where</b> is it derived that <b>one may not prune trees, and one may not trim dry branches</b> from trees, <b>and one</b> may <b>not cut large branches from trees? The verse states: “Your field</b> you shall <b>not</b> sow and <b>your vineyard</b> you shall <b>not</b> prune,” indicating that <b>any labor</b> that is generally performed <b>in your field may not</b> be performed during the Sabbatical Year, <b>and any labor</b> generally performed <b>in your vineyard may not</b> be performed during the Sabbatical Year.
<b>From where</b> is it derived that <b>one may not fertilize</b> fields and vineyards, <b>and one may not remove stones</b> from around the base of a tree that impede its growth, <b>and one may not fumigate a tree</b> in order to exterminate worms? <b>The verse states: “Your field</b> you shall <b>not</b> sow and <b>your vineyard</b> you shall <b>not</b> prune,” thereby indicating that <b>any labor</b> that is generally performed <b>in your field may not</b> be performed during the Sabbatical Year, <b>and any labor</b> generally performed <b>in your vineyard may not</b> be performed during the Sabbatical Year.
One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one may</b> also <b>not hoe</b> lightly <b>under olive</b> trees <b>and one may not hoe under grapevines, and one may not fill cracks</b> in the ground <b>with water, and one may not construct circular ditches</b> around the bases of <b>grapevines</b> in order to collect rainwater. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Your field you shall not sow.”</b>
<b>Sowing was included</b> in the general prohibition against performing agricultural labors during the Sabbatical Year. <b>Why was</b> sowing <b>singled out</b> and mentioned explicitly? In order <b>to compare</b> other types of labor <b>to</b> it and to <b>say to you: Just as sowing is unique in that it is labor</b> performed both <b>in the field and in the vineyard, so too, any other labor</b> performed both <b>in the field and in the vineyard</b> is prohibited. However, labor performed only in the vineyard, but not in the field, i.e., whose objective is merely to sustain the vines from year to year, is permitted. In any case, this <i>baraita</i> indicates that many labors are prohibited during the seventh year, and not just sowing and pruning.
The Gemara rejects this opinion: These labors, with the exceptions of sowing, pruning, grape picking, and reaping, are all prohibited only <b>by rabbinic law, and the verse</b> that is cited as source from the Torah <b>is a mere support,</b> and not a bona fide source.

Daf 3b

<b>When Rav Dimi came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, <b>he stated</b> a tradition he had heard from the Sages in Eretz Yisrael: One <b>might</b> have thought <b>that one would be flogged for the addition, but a teaching states an exemption</b> from lashes. Rav Dimi noted: <b>But I do not know what teaching or what addition</b> this tradition is referring to.
The Sages disputed the meaning of this tradition. <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> The addition in question is <b>plowing</b> during the Sabbatical Year, for which there is no explicit prohibition in the Torah, and so it may be regarded as an addition to the labors explicitly enumerated in the Torah. <b>And this is what it is saying:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one would be flogged for plowing</b> during the Sabbatical Year, <b>as it is derived by way</b> of the hermeneutical principle of <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization</b> that teaches that plowing is prohibited. <b>But a teaching states an exemption</b> from lashes for the labor of plowing.
This is logical, <b>because if</b> one is flogged for plowing, <b>why do I</b> need <b>all these details</b> that were enumerated in the verse, i.e., pruning and picking grapes? Rather, one must certainly conclude that these were singled out in order to teach that one is flogged only for these specific labors, but not for any other.
<b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> This addition is referring to <b>the</b> extra <b>days that the Sages added</b> to the prohibition against performing agricultural labor, <b>before Rosh HaShana</b> of the seventh year, when the Sabbatical Year formally begins. <b>And this is what it is saying:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one would be flogged for</b> working the land during <b>the additional</b> period prior to <b>Rosh HaShana</b> of the Sabbatical Year, <b>as</b> this prohibition <b>is derived</b> from the verse: <b>“In plowing and in reaping you shall rest”</b> (Exodus 34:21). This seemingly superfluous verse is understood as teaching that not only is working the land prohibited during the seventh year, but plowing a field during the sixth year to prepare the land for the seventh year, and reaping what grew in the seventh year during the eighth year are also prohibited. <b>But a teaching states an exemption</b> from lashes for these actions, <b>as we are about to state below.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: <b>What</b> are the extra <b>days before Rosh HaShana? As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 1:1): <b>Until when may</b> one <b>plow an orchard</b> on <b>the eve of the Sabbatical Year? Beit Shammai say:</b> One may plow <b>so long as</b> the plowing <b>is beneficial for the fruit</b> already on the trees. Once the plowing serves to benefit only the tree itself and the fruit it will produce the following year, it is prohibited. <b>And Beit Hillel say:</b> One may plow <b>until <i>Shavuot</i>.</b> The mishna notes: <b>And the statement of these,</b> Beit Shammai, <b>is close to being like the statement of these,</b> Beit Hillel; i.e., in practice, there is little difference between the dates established by the two opinions.
The mishna (see <i>Shevi’it</i> 2:1) additionally states: <b>And until when may</b> one <b>plow a white field,</b> i.e., a grain field, on <b>the eve of the Sabbatical Year?</b> One may plow <b>until the</b> residual <b>moisture</b> in the fields from the rain <b>ceases and so long as people</b> continue to <b>plow</b> their fields in order <b>to plant cucumbers and gourds,</b> which are planted at the end of the winter.
<b>Rabbi Shimon says: If it is so</b> that no set time was established, then <b>the Torah has given an</b> individual <b>measure</b> of time <b>into the hands of each and every individual.</b> One may plow until a self-determined time, as he can always claim that he is plowing in order to plant during the sixth year. <b>Rather,</b> a fixed time must be established: <b>In a white field</b> one may plow <b>until Passover, in an orchard</b> one may plow <b>until <i>Shavuot</i>, and Beit Hillel say: Until Passover.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Shimon ben Pazi said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said in the name of bar Kappara: Rabban Gamliel and his court</b> discussed and then <b>voted about the</b> prohibitions of <b>these two periods,</b> i.e., from Passover or <i>Shavuot</i> until Rosh HaShana, <b>and nullified them,</b> thereby permitting plowing until Rosh HaShana, the actual beginning of the Sabbatical Year.
<b>Rabbi Zeira said to Rabbi Abbahu, and some say</b> that it was <b>Reish Lakish</b> who said <b>to Rabbi Yoḥanan: How could Rabban Gamliel and his court nullify an ordinance</b> instituted by <b>Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel,</b> who were greater authorities than they were? <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 1:5): <b>A court cannot nullify the ruling of another</b> court <b>unless it surpasses it in wisdom and in number?</b>
Rabbi Abbahu <b>“was astonished for a while”</b> (Daniel 4:16), and then <b>said to him: Say</b> that when Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel established their decree, <b>they stipulated among themselves: Anyone who</b> later <b>wishes to nullify</b> this decree <b>may come and nullify</b> it.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is this</b> ordinance <b>theirs?</b> Did Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel institute the ordinance and as such have the authority to attach stipulations to it? <b>It is a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted <b>to Moses from Sinai. As Rabbi Asi said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said in the name of Rabbi Neḥunya from the valley of Beit Ḥortan:</b> The <i>halakha</i> of <b>ten saplings,</b> the <i>mitzva</i> of bringing <b>willow</b> branches to the Temple on <i>Sukkot</i> and standing them up around the altar, and the <i>halakha</i> of <b>water libation</b> on <i>Sukkot</i> are all <b><i>halakhot</i></b> transmitted <b>to Moses from Sinai.</b> Consequently, the prohibition against plowing on the eve of the seventh year is a not a rabbinic ordinance from the Second Temple period, but rather an oral tradition dating back to Moses at Sinai.
<b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said: When they learned</b> this <b><i>halakha</i></b> as a tradition dating back to Moses at Sinai, the prohibition applied from only <b>thirty days before Rosh HaShana.</b> Afterward, <b>these</b> Sages of Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel <b>came</b> and <b>instituted</b> lengthier periods of restriction, <b>from Passover and from <i>Shavuot</i>,</b> respectively, <b>but they stipulated among themselves: Anyone who</b> later <b>wishes to nullify</b> this decree <b>may come and nullify</b> it. Rabban Gamliel and his court were therefore able to nullify extended restrictions instituted by Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel.
The Gemara raises another question: <b>Are these</b> prohibitions of plowing before the Sabbatical Year really <b><i>halakhot</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai? <b>They are</b> actually prohibitions based on explicit <b>verses. As we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse <b>“In plowing and in reaping you shall rest”</b> (Exodus 34:21) that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: It is unnecessary</b> for the verse <b>to speak</b> about <b>plowing and reaping during the Sabbatical Year, as it was already stated:</b> “But in the seventh year shall be a sabbath of solemn rest for the land, a sabbath for the Lord; <b>your field you shall not sow, and your vineyard you shall not prune”</b> (Leviticus 25:4). This teaches that during the seventh year all agricultural labor is prohibited. <b>Rather,</b> the verse comes to prohibit <b>plowing on the eve of the Sabbatical Year</b>

Daf 4a

<b>that entered into the Sabbatical Year,</b> i.e., plowing in the sixth year that will benefit crops growing in the seventh year, <b>and reaping</b> the crops <b>of the Sabbatical Year that continued into the conclusion of the Sabbatical Year,</b> i.e., reaping seventh-year produce that continued to grow into the eighth year.
<b>Rabbi Yishmael says</b> that this verse is to be understood as referring to Shabbat and not to the Sabbatical Year, in accordance with the straightforward meaning of the verse. It teaches as follows: <b>Just as</b> only <b>optional plowing</b> is prohibited on Shabbat, as there is no instance where plowing fulfills a biblical mitzva, <b>so too,</b> only <b>optional reaping</b> is prohibited, <b>to the exclusion of the reaping of the <i>omer</i></b> offering, <b>which is a mitzva,</b> and consequently permitted on Shabbat. Nonetheless, the first opinion cited in the <i>baraita</i>, that of Rabbi Akiva, holds that the prohibition against plowing on the eve of the Sabbatical Year is derived from an explicit verse.
<b>Rather, Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: When we learned</b> this as <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai, <b>it was to permit</b> plowing in the case of <b>young</b> saplings until Rosh HaShana. In contrast, <b>the verses</b> that were cited come <b>to prohibit</b> plowing in the case of <b>mature</b> and well-rooted trees thirty days before Rosh HaShana of the Sabbatical Year.
The Gemara asks: <b>But since the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai comes <b>to permit</b> plowing in the case of <b>young</b> saplings until Rosh HaShana, <b>does it not automatically</b> follow that in the case of <b>mature</b> trees, plowing <b>is prohibited</b> before Rosh HaShana? Therefore, not only the allowance, but the prohibition as well was learned by tradition as a <i>halakha</i> transmitted to Moses from Sinai, and not from the verses.
<b>Rather,</b> the <b><i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai is the basis of the prohibition against plowing on the eve of the Sabbatical Year <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael,</b> who interprets the verse as referring to Shabbat, and not to the Sabbatical Year, whereas <b>the verses</b> are the basis of the prohibition <b>according to the</b> opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b>
The Gemara previously cited Rabbi Yitzḥak, who explained how Rabban Gamliel’s court nullified the extension to the prohibition against plowing before the Sabbatical Year that had been enacted by Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel. The Gemara now cites another opinion, which holds that Rabban Gamliel’s court abolished the prohibition against plowing before the Sabbatical Year entirely. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> that <b>Rabban Gamliel and his court nullified</b> the restrictions on working the land on the eve of the Sabbatical Year <b>based on</b> a source written <b>in the Torah.</b>
<b>What is the reason? He derives</b> it by means of a verbal analogy between the word <b>Shabbat</b> stated with regard to the Sabbatical Year in the verse: “But in the seventh year shall be a sabbath of solemn rest for the land” (Leviticus 25:4), <b>and</b> the word <b>Shabbat</b> stated with regard to the weekly Shabbat, which commemorates <b>the Shabbat of Creation. Just as there,</b> on Shabbat <b>itself it is prohibited</b> to perform labor, but <b>before and after</b> Shabbat <b>it is permitted, so too here,</b> in the case of the Sabbatical Year, during the Sabbatical Year <b>itself it is prohibited</b> to perform labor, but <b>before and after</b> the Sabbatical Year <b>it is permitted.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi strongly objects to this:</b> If Rabban Gamliel and his court nullified the restrictions based on a verbal analogy, then according to <b>the one who said</b> that the prohibition against plowing thirty days before Rosh HaShana of the Sabbatical Year is a <b><i>halakha</i></b> that was transmitted to Moses from Sinai, <b>can a verbal analogy come</b> and <b>uproot a <i>halakha</i></b> that was transmitted to Moses from Sinai? <b>And</b> similarly, according to <b>the one who said</b> that the prohibition against plowing is derived from <b>a verse, can a verbal analogy come</b> and <b>uproot a verse?</b>
<b>Rather, Rav Ashi said: Rabban Gamliel and his court held in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who said</b> that <b>they learned this</b> prohibition as <b>a <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai. <b>But they learned this <i>halakha</i></b> only with regard to the time period <b>when the Temple is standing.</b> This is evidenced by the fact that it is <b>similar to</b> the other <i>halakha</i> stated along with it, that of the <b>water libation,</b> which was part of the service in the Temple. <b>But when the Temple is not standing</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>does not</b> apply, and therefore Rabban Gamliel and his court nullified the prohibition after the destruction of the Temple.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>However,</b> one <b>may not</b> irrigate a field on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>with rainwater</b> collected in a cistern <b>or with water drawn with a shadoof.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> irrigating a field with <b>water drawn with a shadoof involves excessive effort,</b> and so it is prohibited on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>But what excessive effort is involved</b> in irrigating a field <b>with rainwater?</b> Rainwater collects on its own and one merely has to channel it to where it is needed.
<b>Rabbi Ile’a said that</b> Rabbi <b>Yoḥanan said:</b> A rabbinic <b>decree</b> was enacted with regard to <b>rainwater due to</b> its similarity <b>to water drawn with a shadoof. Rav Ashi said: Rainwater itself will come to be</b> like <b>water drawn with a shadoof.</b> Once the level of the collected rainwater drops, it will become necessary to draw it with a bucket, a procedure involving excessive effort.
The Gemara comments: Rabbi Ile’a and Rav Ashi <b>disagree with regard to</b> the ruling issued by <b>Rabbi Zeira, as Rabbi Zeira said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Yirmeya said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> With regard to <b>streams that draw water from pools</b> of collected water, <b>one is permitted to irrigate</b> his field <b>from them on the intermediate days of a Festival,</b> because the flow of water is steady.
One <b>Sage,</b> Rav Ashi, <b>is of</b> the opinion that the ruling is in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Zeira,</b> as he prohibits only irrigating with rainwater, because the supply might come to an end, but he does not prohibit watering from a source whose flow is steady. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan, <b>is not of</b> the opinion that the ruling is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Zeira,</b> as he prohibits irrigating with rainwater due to its similarity to water drawn with a shadoof. This applies regardless of whether the level of the rainwater will drop, and therefore Rabbi Yoḥanan would prohibit using collected water even if a stream flows through it and it will not dry up.
After mentioning the statement of Rabbi Zeira in the course of the previous discussion, the Gemara examines <b>the</b> matter <b>itself. Rabbi Zeira said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Yirmeya said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> With regard to <b>streams that draw water from pools</b> of water, <b>one is permitted to irrigate</b> his field <b>from them on the intermediate days of a Festival.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised an objection to Rabbi Zeira</b> from what is taught in the mishna: <b>However, one may not</b> irrigate a field on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>with rainwater</b> collected in cisterns <b>or with water drawn with a shadoof.</b> This indicates that whenever there is a concern that the water might run out, it is prohibited to irrigate from this water source. Consequently, Rabbi Yirmeya wanted to know why this concern did not exist in the case of the pools of water as well. Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him: Yirmeya, my son, these pools in Babylonia are like water that does not stop</b> flowing. Therefore, there is no concern that the water level in these pools might go down to such an extent that it will become necessary to draw the water with buckets.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>temporary pools and</b> regular <b>pools that were filled with water on the eve of a Festival, it is prohibited for one to irrigate</b> his field <b>from them on the intermediate days of the Festival,</b> lest they run out of water and he will come to exert himself and bring water from elsewhere. <b>But if a water channel passes between them</b> so that water flows from the one to the other, <b>it is permitted.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said: And this</b> allowance applies only <b>when the majority of that field can be irrigated from that water channel,</b> such that most of the field can be irrigated at the same time. In this case, there is no concern that when the water runs out, he will come to exert himself and irrigate the rest of the field from another source of water. <b>Rav Ashi said:</b> It applies <b>even though the majority of that field cannot be irrigated</b> from that water channel at the same time. <b>Since</b> the channel <b>continuously draws</b> water, constantly replenishing its supply, even if it does so at a slow rate, one will <b>say</b> to himself that even <b>if</b> the entire field <b>cannot be irrigated</b> from that water channel <b>on a single day, it can be irrigated</b> from it <b>over</b> the course of <b>two or three days.</b> Accordingly, he will not find it necessary to exert himself to quickly irrigate that portion of the field that did not already receive its water.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a pool</b> that receives <b>drips</b> of <b>water from this field that requires irrigation,</b> which itself receives water from a spring, <b>one is permitted to irrigate from</b> this pool <b>another field</b> situated below it <b>that requires irrigation.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t</b> the pool <b>likely to stop flowing,</b> which will force him to exert himself and draw water from somewhere else? <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> The case is where the upper field <b>is still trickling</b> water into the pool and does not stop. <b>Abaye said: And this</b> allowance applies only <b>when</b> the water from <b>the first spring</b> that irrigates the upper field <b>has not stopped</b> flowing. Only in that case can one rely on the water trickling from the upper field and consequently irrigating the lower field from the pool.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Menasya says:</b> With regard to <b>two garden beds</b> located <b>one above the other, one may not draw</b> water <b>from</b> the channel supplying <b>the lower</b> garden bed in order <b>to irrigate the upper</b> garden bed, due to the excessive exertion involved. <b>Furthermore, Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon said: Even</b> in the case of <b>a single garden bed, half of which is lower and half of which is higher, one may not draw</b> water <b>from the</b> channel supplying <b>the lower area to irrigate the upper area,</b> even though they are two parts of the same garden bed.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may draw</b> water and irrigate <b>vegetables in order to eat them</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>But if</b> he does this <b>in order</b> to improve their growth and <b>to enhance their</b> appearance <b>it is prohibited,</b> as he is considered to be unnecessarily exerting himself on the Festival.
The Gemara relates that <b>Ravina and Rabba Tosefa’a were</b> once <b>walking along the road</b> when <b>they saw a certain man that was drawing</b> water <b>with a bucket on the intermediate days of a Festival. Rabba Tosefa’a said to Ravina: Let the Master come and excommunicate him</b> for transgressing the words of the Sages. Ravina <b>said to him: But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may draw for vegetables in order to eat them,</b> and so he has not committed a transgression. Rabba Tosefa’a <b>said to him: Do you maintain</b> that <b>what is</b> meant by <b>one may draw</b> [<b><i>madlin</i></b>] <b>is</b> that <b>one may draw water</b> in order to irrigate the vegetables? This is not so. Rather, <b>what is</b> meant by

Daf 4b

<b>one may draw</b> is that <b>one may pull out</b> some of the vegetables that are growing densely together. The <i>baraita</i> comes to teach that one is permitted to thin out a garden bed on the intermediate days of a Festival in order to eat on the Festival those that he removes, but he is prohibited to do so in order to enhance the appearance of those that remain. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Pe’a</i> 7:5): <b>One who thins out [<i>meidel</i>] the vines</b> in his vineyard, <b>just as he may thin out his own</b> vines, <b>so too, he may thin out</b> the vines set aside for <b>the poor.</b> Since he is doing it for the sake of the vines, he may also thin out what he leaves for the poor; this is <b>the statemen</b>t <b>of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Meir</b> disagrees and <b>says: His own</b> vines <b>he is permitted</b> to thin out, <b>but he is not permitted</b> to thin out the vines set aside <b>for the poor.</b> This mishna indicates that the term <i>meidel</i> can be used to mean thinning out and does not refer only to drawing water.
Ravina <b>said to</b> Rabba Tosefa’a: <b>But wasn’t it</b> explicitly <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may draw water</b> to irrigate <b>vegetables in order to eat them?</b> Rabba Tosefa’a <b>said to him: If it is taught</b> explicitly in a <i>baraita</i>, the <i>halakha</i> is as <b>it is taught,</b> and I retract my statement.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>And one may not construct circular ditches [<i>ugiyyot</i>]</b> around the bases of <b>grapevines</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. The Gemara asks: <b>What are <i>ugiyyot</i>? Rav Yehuda said:</b> They are what are called in Aramaic <b><i>binkei</i>,</b> circular ditches around vines. The Gemara notes that <b>this is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>These are <i>ugiyyot</i>: <i>Bedidin</i>,</b> circular ditches <b>around the bases of olive trees and around the bases of grapevines.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so?</b> Is it prohibited to dig circular ditches on the intermediate days of the Festival? <b>Didn’t Rav Yehuda permit the family of bar Tzitai to construct circular ditches for their vineyards</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival? The Gemara answers: This is <b>not difficult. This</b> source, i.e., the mishna that renders the practice prohibited, is referring to digging <b>new</b> ditches. <b>That</b> other source, i.e., Rav Yehuda’s ruling that permits the digging of such ditches, is referring to <b>old</b> ones, which merely need to be cleared.
§ It was taught in the next clause of the mishna that <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: One may not construct a new</b> water <b>channel</b> during the intermediate days of a Festival or during the Sabbatical Year. The Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> it is prohibited for him to do so on the intermediate days of <b>a Festival, because</b> in so doing <b>he</b> excessively <b>exerts himself</b> on the Festival. <b>But what is the reason</b> that this is prohibited during <b>the Sabbatical Year,</b> when only labors that enhance the growth of plants are prohibited?
The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Zeira and Rabbi Abba bar Memel disagreed with regard to this</b> issue. <b>One</b> of them <b>said:</b> It is prohibited <b>because it appears</b> to others <b>as if he were hoeing</b> his field. As onlookers do not know that he is merely digging a water channel, they suspect him of working his land during the Sabbatical Year. <b>And</b> the other <b>one said:</b> It is prohibited <b>because he</b> thereby <b>prepares</b> the channel’s <b>banks for planting,</b> for when he digs out the channel, he piles the fresh soil that is fit for planting on its two banks.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the practical halakhic difference <b>between them?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>There is</b> a practical halakhic difference <b>between them</b> in a case where <b>water comes</b> into the channel immediately <b>after he</b> digs it out. According to <b>the one who said</b> that digging a water channel is prohibited <b>because he</b> thereby <b>prepares its banks for planting, there is</b> still a prohibition, as here too, he piles the fresh soil on the channel’s banks. <b>But</b> according to <b>the one who said</b> it is prohibited <b>because it appears as if he were hoeing, there is no</b> prohibition here, as the immediate entry of water makes it obvious that he is digging a water channel.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to the one who said</b> that digging a water channel is prohibited <b>because it appears as if he were hoeing, let him be concerned that</b> the digger thereby <b>prepares</b> the channel’s <b>banks for planting</b> and render digging prohibited in this case as well. <b>Rather,</b> the matter must be explained differently, such that <b>there is</b> a practical difference <b>between them</b> in a case <b>where he takes</b> the earth that he excavates <b>from</b> the channel <b>and throws</b> it a considerable distance <b>outside. According to the one who said</b> that it is prohibited <b>because he</b> thereby <b>prepares</b> the channel’s <b>banks for planting, there is no</b> prohibition here, as he does not prepare them for planting. But <b>according to the one who said</b> it is prohibited <b>because it appears as if he were hoeing, there is</b> a prohibition here, as here too, it appears as if he were hoeing.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to the one who said</b> that digging a water channel is prohibited <b>because he</b> thereby <b>prepares its banks for planting, let him be concerned that</b> the digger <b>appears as if he were hoeing.</b> The Gemara answers that this is not a concern, because it is <b>also</b> true of <b>one who hoes</b> that <b>when he takes</b> up a clump of earth, he <b>puts it down</b> again <b>in its place.</b> Consequently, since one throws the dug-up earth far away, it is immediately apparent that he is not engaged in hoeing but is rather digging a water channel.
<b>Ameimar would teach</b> this mishna as stating explicitly that Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says that one may not dig a new water channel during the Sabbatical Year <b>because it appears as if he were hoeing</b> his field, <b>and he</b> therefore found <b>a difficulty between</b> this statement of <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya and</b> another statement of <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya.</b> The difficulty is as follows: <b>Did Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya</b> actually <b>say</b> that <b>any</b> action <b>that</b> causes him to <b>appear as if he were hoeing is prohibited?</b>
One may <b>raise a contradiction</b> to this assertion from a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 3:3) that states: <b>A person</b> may pile <b>his manure</b> in his field during the Sabbatical Year so that it <b>becomes a storage</b> heap, and there is no cause for concern that it may appear as if he were fertilizing his field. <b>Rabbi Meir prohibits</b> this <b>unless he deepens</b> the storage area for the manure <b>three handbreadths</b> below the ground <b>or raises</b> it <b>three handbreadths</b> above the surface of the ground, so that it does not appear as if he were fertilizing his field. If <b>he</b> already <b>had a small amount</b> of manure in that heap from before the Sabbatical Year, <b>he may continue to add to it,</b> and there is no need for concern.
<b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya prohibits</b> piling his manure in his field <b>unless he deepens</b> the storage area for the manure <b>three</b> handbreadths below the surface of the ground, <b>or he raises</b> it <b>three</b> handbreadths <b>above</b> the surface, <b>or he places</b> it <b>on a rock.</b> In any event, it seems that according to Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya, it is permitted for him to dig a hole in the ground in order to deposit his manure there, even though he might appear to be hoeing the ground.
<b>Rabbi Zeira and Rabbi Abba bar Memel</b> both offered resolutions to this difficulty: <b>One</b> of them <b>said:</b> The case in this second mishna is one <b>where he</b> had already <b>deepened</b> the three-handbreadth pit during the sixth year. <b>And</b> the other <b>one said: His</b> pile of <b>manure is proof</b> that he intends merely to bury the manure and not to hoe the field.
§ It was taught in the mishna that the Rabbis say: <b>One may repair a damaged</b> water channel <b>during</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival. What is</b> meant by <b>a damaged</b> water channel? <b>Rabbi Abba said that if it was</b> now <b>a handbreadth deep</b> because it had become filled with sediment, he may dredge it out until <b>he sets it at</b> its original depth of <b>six handbreadths.</b>
Based on this ruling, the Gemara clarifies several practical issues: It is <b>obvious</b> that if the channel is <b>half a handbreadth</b> deep and he wants to restore it to a depth <b>of three handbreadths, since water does not flow</b> through a three-handbreadth-deep channel in sufficient quantity, <b>it is nothing</b> at all and it is certainly prohibited to exert oneself with work that provides insignificant benefit. So too, if the channel is <b>two handbreadths</b> deep and he wants to deepen it <b>to twelve handbreadths,</b> even though he preserves the same ratio as in Rabbi Abba’s case, <b>since it involves excessive exertion, no,</b> this is also not permitted.
However, in a case where the channel is <b>two handbreadths</b> deep and he wants to deepen it <b>to seven</b> handbreadths, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara explains the two sides of the question of whether this can be compared to the case in the mishna: <b>Here,</b> in the case of dredging a one-handbreadth-deep channel to restore its depth of six handbreadths, <b>he deepens</b> the channel by <b>five</b> handbreadths, <b>and</b> similarly <b>here,</b> in the case of deepening the channel from two to seven handbreadths, <b>he</b> likewise wants to <b>deepen</b> it by <b>five</b> handbreadths, and therefore it should be permitted. <b>Or perhaps, since there is an extra handbreadth</b> of depth, then bending over to dig that additional handbreadth <b>involves greater effort,</b> which is unnecessary, and so possibly it should be prohibited. The Gemara states that the dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
It was related that <b>Abaye permitted the people of Bar Hamdakh to remove</b> the branches of the trees from <b>the river</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya permitted the people of Sekhavta to dredge out a river that had become blocked. Rav Ashi permitted</b> his townsmen, <b>the people of Mata Meḥasya, to clean out the</b> nearby <b>Burnitz River. He said: Since the public drinks from it, it is considered like a public</b> need, <b>and we learned</b> in the mishna that <b>one may tend to all</b> other <b>public needs</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival.
§ It was taught in the mishna: During the intermediate days of a Festival <b>one may repair</b>

Daf 5a

<b>damaged water</b> cisterns <b>in the public domain and</b> clean them out by removing the dirt and sediment that has accumulated in them. The Gemara infers: <b>Cleaning</b> out the cisterns of dirt and sediment during the intermediate days of a Festival <b>is</b> indeed permitted, but <b>digging</b> a new cistern <b>is not</b> permitted.
<b>Rabbi Ya’akov said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: They taught</b> that it is prohibited to dig new cisterns <b>only when the public does not need them; but if the public needs them, even digging</b> new cisterns <b>is permitted.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And when the public needs them, is</b> digging really <b>permitted? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may clean</b> out <b>cisterns, ditches, and caves of an individual</b> during the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>and, needless to say,</b> one may clean out those <b>of the public. But one may not dig</b> new <b>cisterns, ditches, or caves of the public</b> during the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>and, needless to say,</b> one may not dig those <b>of an individual. What, is it not</b> so that this <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where the public needs them,</b> but nevertheless digging new cisterns, ditches, and caves is prohibited?
The Gemara rejects this opinion: <b>No,</b> this <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where the public does not need them.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: It would follow that <b>in the corresponding</b> situation <b>with regard to</b> the cisterns of <b>an individual,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case where <b>the individual does not need them.</b> But in that case, <b>is cleaning</b> them out really <b>permitted? Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may gather water into</b> the <b>cisterns, ditches, and caves of an individual, but one may not clean</b> them out <b>or plaster their cracks; but</b> for those <b>of the public, one may</b> indeed <b>clean</b> out <b>and plaster their cracks?</b> This indicates that on the intermediate days of a Festival, unneeded cisterns belonging to an individual may not even be cleaned out.
The Gemara rejects this difficulty: <b>Rather,</b> to <b>what</b> case does the first <i>baraita</i> refer? Is it referring to a case <b>where the individual needs</b> the cisterns? If so, then <b>in the corresponding</b> situation <b>with regard to</b> cisterns of <b>the public,</b> the <i>baraita</i> would be referring to a case where <b>the public needs them.</b> But in that case, <b>is digging</b> new cisterns really <b>prohibited? Isn’t it taught</b> in yet another <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may gather water into cisterns, ditches, and caves of an individual, and one may clean them</b> out, <b>but one may not plaster their cracks, clear</b> earth <b>into them</b> in order to fill in the cracks, or <b>lime them with lime</b> so that they hold water. <b>But</b> with regard to those <b>of the public, one may</b> even <b>dig them out and lime them with lime.</b> Therefore, in a case where the public needs them, it is permitted to dig out public cisterns.
<b>But</b> if so, <b>the first</b> <i>baraita</i>, which states that one may not dig new cisterns even for the public, is <b>difficult,</b> as it is contradicted by this last <i>baraita</i>. The Gemara explains: <b>Answer</b> the difficulty and explain the first <i>baraita</i> <b>as follows: One may clean</b> out <b>cisterns,</b> ditches, and caves <b>of an individual</b> during the intermediate days of a Festival <b>when the individual needs them; and needless to say,</b> one may clean out those <b>of the public when the public needs them, as even digging</b> new cisterns <b>is permitted</b> when the public needs them.
<b>But one may not dig cisterns, ditches and caves for the public when the public does not need them. And needless to say,</b> one may not dig them <b>for an individual, as when an individual does not need</b> the cisterns on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>even cleaning them</b> out <b>is prohibited.</b> In this way all of the seemingly contradictory sources can be reconciled.
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> The wording of <b>the mishna is also precise,</b> indicating that when there is a public need for such cisterns, they may be dug even on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>As it teaches: One may tend to all</b> other <b>public needs. What</b> does the word <b>all</b> come <b>to add</b> that was not stated explicitly? <b>Does it not</b> come <b>to add the digging</b> of cisterns, which is permitted?
The Gemara rejects this opinion: <b>No,</b> the word all comes <b>to add that which is taught</b> in the following <i>baraita</i>: On the intermediate days of a Festival, agents of the court <b>go out to clear thorns from the road, and to repair the</b> city <b>streets and highways [<i>isterata’ot</i>], and to measure the ritual baths</b> to ascertain that they have the requisite quantity of water. <b>And</b> if <b>any ritual bath does not contain forty <i>se’a</i>,</b> the minimal measure for ritual purification, <b>they direct [<i>margilin</i>]</b> a stream of water <b>into it,</b> such that it flows over the ground before entering the bath, so as not to disqualify the water as drawn water until it holds <b>forty <i>se’a</i></b> of water.
<b>And from where</b> is it derived <b>that if</b> agents of the court <b>did not go out and do all these</b> repairs, <b>that</b> with regard to <b>any blood that is shed there</b> on account of their negligence, <b>the verse ascribes to them</b> guilt <b>as if they had shed it? The verse states</b> with regard to the cities of refuge that offer protection to someone who committed inadvertent manslaughter: “That innocent blood be not shed in your land, which the Lord your God gives you for an inheritance, <b>and so blood be upon you”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:10). The Gemara maintains that the mishna uses the word all to allude to the cases mentioned in this <i>baraita</i>, and not to the digging of public cisterns.
The Gemara questions this: <b>But</b> these additional cases <b>are explicitly taught</b> in the mishna: <b>One may repair the roads, streets, and ritual baths, and one may tend to all</b> other <b>public needs. What</b> does this last phrase come <b>to add? Does it not</b> come to <b>add the digging</b> of cisterns needed by the public? The Gemara agrees: <b>Conclude from this</b> that the mishna means to permit the digging of new cisterns when they are needed by the public.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>One may mark graves</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival so that passersby will know to avoid them and not become ritually impure. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Pazi said: Where is there an allusion in the Torah to the marking of graves? The verse states:</b> “And when they that pass through shall pass through the land, <b>and any see a man’s bone, then shall he set up a sign by it”</b> (Ezekiel 39:15). Ezekiel prophesies that at some future time, the Jewish people will erect signs over the strewn remains of the dead so that others will know to avoid ritual impurity.
<b>Ravina said to Rav Ashi: Before</b> the prophet <b>Ezekiel came</b> and alluded to this obligation, <b>who said</b> that graves must be marked? Even before the time of Ezekiel, people were careful with regard to ritual impurity. Rav Ashi responded: <b>And according to your reasoning,</b> that Ezekiel was introducing a new <i>halakha</i>, the same question can be raised with regard to <b>this</b> statement <b>that Rav Ḥisda said.</b> As Rav Ḥisda said with regard to the <i>halakha</i> that one who is uncircumcised or an apostate may not serve in the Temple: <b>This matter we did not learn from the Torah of Moses our teacher,</b> but rather, <b>we learned</b> it <b>from the words of</b> the prophet <b>Ezekiel ben Buzi,</b> who said of such individuals: <b>“No stranger, uncircumcised in heart, or uncircumcised in flesh, shall enter into My Sanctuary to serve Me”</b> (Ezekiel 44:9).
Here too, one can ask: <b>Before Ezekiel came, who said</b> that such individuals cannot serve in the Temple? <b>Rather,</b> you must say that originally <b>they learned it as a tradition</b> and it was an accepted <i>halakha</i> for generations, <b>and</b> then <b>Ezekiel came and based it on a verse. Here too,</b> with regard to the obligation to mark graves, <b>they</b> originally <b>learned it as a tradition, and</b> then <b>Ezekiel came and based it on a verse.</b>
<b>Rabbi Abbahu said:</b> An allusion to the marking of graves may be derived <b>from here:</b> “And the leper in whom the plague is, his clothes shall be rent, and the hair of his head shall go loose, and he shall cover his upper lip, and <b>shall cry: Impure, impure”</b> (Leviticus 13:45). This verse teaches that <b>impurity cries out</b> to the passerby <b>and tells him: Remove yourself.</b> The leper must inform others of his status so that they know not to come into contact with him and thereby maintain their ritual purity. So too, in our case, graves must be marked so that others will know to avoid them and prevent contracting ritual impurity. <b>And similarly, Rabbi Uzziel, grandson of Rabbi Uzziel the Great, said: Impurity cries out</b> to the passerby <b>and tells him: Remove yourself.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> with regard to <b>this</b> verse, <b>does it come to teach this</b> idea? <b>That</b> verse <b>is needed for that which is taught</b> in the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>“And he shall cry: Impure, impure”;</b> this teaches that the leper <b>must inform the public of his distress, and the public will pray for mercy on his behalf.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>If it is so</b> that the verse comes to teach only one idea, <b>let it write: And he shall cry: Impure. What</b> is to be derived the repetition of <b>impure, impure? Learn from this</b> reiteration <b>two</b> ideas: First, that the leper must inform the public of his pain so that others will pray on his behalf, and second, that he must warn the public to stay away so that they avoid coming into contact with him and contracting ritual impurity.
<b>Abaye said:</b> An allusion to the marking of graves may be learned <b>from here,</b> as it is written: <b>“You shall not put a stumbling block before the blind”</b> (Leviticus 19:14). <b>Rav Pappa said</b> the obligation is alluded to in the verse: <b>“And He will say: Pave, pave, clear the way,</b> take up the stumbling block out of the way of My people” (Isaiah 57:14), which indicates that roads must be cleared of all obstacles and hazards.
<b>Rav Ḥinnana said:</b> This may be derived from the end of that very same verse: <b>“Take up the stumbling block from the way of My people”</b> (Isaiah 57:14). <b>Rabbi Yehoshua, son of Rav Idi, said:</b> This may be derived from the verse: <b>“And you shall show them the way in which they must walk”</b> (Exodus 18:20), i.e., you must properly repair the roads, which includes marking graves.
<b>Mar Zutra said</b> that an allusion to this obligation is found in the verse <b>“Thus you shall separate the children of Israel from their impurity”</b> (Leviticus 15:31), which indicates that people must be warned to stay away from that which could cause them to become ritually impure. <b>Rav Ashi said</b> it is derived from the verse: <b>“And you shall keep My charge”</b> (Leviticus 18:30), which means that you must <b>establish a safeguard for My charge,</b> i.e., protective measures must be enacted to prevent people from transgressing <i>halakha</i>, a task that includes distancing people from ritual impurity by marking off graves, so that they not come to convey ritual impurity to <i>teruma</i> or other consecrated items.
And finally, <b>Ravina said:</b> This obligation is alluded to by the verse <b>“And to him who orders his way, I will show the salvation of God”</b> (Psalms 50:23), meaning that one must mark the pathways that are ritually pure and upon which it is appropriate to walk.
With regard to the verse from Psalms cited above, <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: Whoever appraises his ways</b> in this world and contemplates how to act in the most appropriate way possible <b>merits seeing the salvation of the Holy One, Blessed be He, as it is stated: “And to him who orders his way.” Do not read</b> it as <b><i>vesam</i>,</b> who orders; <b>rather,</b> read it as <b><i>vesham</i>,</b> and appraises. With this reading, the verse indicates that one who appraises his ways, <b>him will I show the salvation of God.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yannai had a certain student who would raise difficulties</b> with his teachings <b>every day</b> as they were learning. <b>On Shabbat of a Festival,</b> when the broader public would come to hear the lesson, the student <b>would not raise</b> any <b>difficulties,</b> lest Rabbi Yannai lack an immediate answer and suffer embarrassment.

Daf 5b

Rabbi Yannai <b>read</b> this verse <b>about him: “And to him who orders his way, I will show the salvation of God”</b> (Psalms 50:23), for he considered his conduct and determined when it was inappropriate to challenge his master.
§ With regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of marking graves, <b>the Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: The courts <b>do not mark</b> the area <b>of an olive-bulk of a corpse; nor of a bone</b> that is the size of a <b>barleygrain-bulk; nor of</b> any <b>item that</b> imparts impurity only through physical contact but <b>does not impart ritual impurity by means of a tent</b> to an individual or object that it overshadows, or that is overshadowed by it, or that is found together with it under the same structure. <b>But they do mark</b> the area of <b>the spine</b> of a corpse, <b>the skull,</b> or the bones that comprise <b>the majority of the</b> skeletal <b>structure or the majority of the number</b> of bones in the body.
<b>And</b> furthermore, <b>they do not mark</b> the area <b>of certain</b> ritual impurity, i.e., a place that is known to all as ritually impure, <b>but they do mark</b> a place <b>of uncertain</b> ritual impurity. <b>And these are the</b> places of <b>uncertain</b> ritual impurity: <b>Overhanging boughs, protrusions, and a <i>beit haperas</i>. And they do not erect the marker</b> directly <b>over the site of the ritual impurity, so as not to cause a loss of ritually pure</b> food items, as one who is carrying such food might inadvertently walk up to the site of ritual impurity and only then notice the marker, after the food has already contracted impurity. <b>Similarly, they do not distance the marker from the</b> actual <b>site of ritual impurity, so as not to cause a loss of Eretz Yisrael,</b> i.e., so as not to increase the area into which individuals refrain from entering.
The Gemara begins to analyze this <i>baraita</i> by asking: <b>Is it</b> really <b>so that an olive-bulk of a corpse does not impart ritual impurity by means of a tent? But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Oholot</i> 2:1): <b>These are</b> the items <b>that impart ritual impurity by means of a tent,</b> and among other items this list includes <b>an olive-bulk of a corpse?</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said: Here, we are dealing with</b> a case <b>where</b> the piece of flesh <b>is exactly an olive-bulk, which,</b> as it decays, <b>will ultimately diminish</b> in size to less than an olive-bulk. Accordingly, <b>it is preferable that <i>teruma</i> and consecrated items be burned because of it for the time being,</b> in a case where one inadvertently encounters this impurity because it was not marked and consequently one must burn any <i>teruma</i> or consecrated items that became ritually impure, <b>and not be burned because of it forever</b> afterward. After some time the piece of flesh will be less than an olive-bulk, yet if the area is marked, people will continue to burn <i>teruma</i> or consecrated items because of it, as, due to the marking, they will assume that ritual impurity was imparted by means of a tent.
The Gemara continues to explicate the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And these are the</b> places of <b>uncertain</b> ritual impurity: <b>Overhanging boughs, and protrusions,</b> and a <i>beit haperas</i>.
The Gemara explains: <b>Overhanging boughs</b> is referring to <b>a tree that hangs over the ground</b> next to a cemetery, and under one of its branches there might be a corpse. If there is a corpse there, the branch overhanging it creates a tent and therefore imparts ritual impurity to anyone who passes underneath it.
