After Rabbi Shimon ben Ḥalafta repeated what Rav had said, his father <b>said to him: He blessed you with the blessing with which the Holy One, Blessed be He, blessed Israel and</b> then <b>repeated</b> the blessing, indicating that is a very great blessing, <b>as it is written: “And you shall eat in plenty, and be satisfied, and praise</b> the Name of the Lord your God, who has dealt wondrously with you; <b>and My people shall never be ashamed. And you shall know that I am in the midst of Israel,</b> and that I am the Lord your God, and there is none else; <b>and My people shall never be ashamed”</b> (Joel 2:26–27).
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>And a woman may engage in</b> all of <b>her</b> usual <b>cosmetic treatments</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>These are the cosmetic treatments of women</b> that are permitted: <b>She may paint her eyelids, she may remove</b> unwanted <b>hair [<i>pokeset</i>], and she may put rouge on her face. And some say: She may pass a comb over her lower face,</b> i.e., she may remove the hair from her pubic area.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Ḥisda’s wife would adorn herself</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>in the presence of her daughter-in-law,</b> i.e., when she already had a married son. <b>Rav Huna bar Ḥinnana sat before Rav Ḥisda, and he sat and said: They taught only</b> that a woman is permitted to engage in cosmetic treatments on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>only</b> with regard to <b>a young woman,</b> as such treatments bring her joy, <b>but</b> in the case of <b>an old woman, no,</b> the treatments are not permitted, as she does not need them.
Rav Ḥisda <b>said to him: By God! Even your mother, and even your mother’s mother, and even a woman</b> so old <b>that</b> she <b>is standing at</b> the edge of <b>her grave</b> are all permitted to adorn themselves. <b>As people say</b> in the popular adage: <b>A woman of sixty years, like one of six, runs at the sound of the timbrel [<i>tavla</i>],</b> implying that women of all ages are young in spirit; since they all take pleasure in their adornments, they are allowed to adorn themselves, regardless of age.
§ It was taught in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: She may not apply lime</b> to her skin <b>because it renders her</b> temporarily <b>unattractive</b> and she will be distressed during the Festival. <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: A woman may not apply lime</b> to her skin on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>because it</b> is temporarily <b>a disgrace to her,</b> as she appears unattractive until it is peeled off. <b>But Rabbi Yehuda concedes that with regard to lime that she can peel off during the Festival, she may apply it on</b> the intermediate days of <b>the Festival, as even though she is distressed now,</b> as the lime renders her appearance unattractive, <b>she will be happy afterward,</b> when the lime is removed and she sees the results.
The Gemara asks: <b>But does Rabbi Yehuda accept this reasoning</b> that it is permitted, despite the fact that it causes distress, because it will cause joy later? <b>Didn’t we learn</b> elsewhere in a mishna (<i>Avoda Zara</i> 2a): On the three days before the holidays of idolaters, it is prohibited to do business with them, so as not to cause them to thank their idols for their success. Similarly, it is prohibited on these days to lend to them, to borrow from them, to repay a debt to them, or even to collect a debt from them.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: One may collect</b> a debt <b>from them</b> during this period, <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> the gentile <b>is distressed</b> when he makes the payment. The Rabbis <b>said to him: Even though he is distressed now, he will be happy afterward,</b> as he is relieved of the debt, and therefore, it is prohibited to collect a debt from him during the three days before his holiday. In this mishna, Rabbi Yehuda considers only the immediate distress and does not consider the joy that will be experienced after some time.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: Leave</b> aside <b>the <i>halakhot</i> governing a Festival,</b> which cannot be compared to other areas, as <b>all</b> of the labors that are permitted on a Festival are permitted because even though <b>one is distressed</b> by them <b>now, he will be happy</b> about them <b>afterward.</b> For example, cooking and baking are permitted even though preparing food involves effort and trouble, because eating the food after it is prepared provides pleasure and enjoyment.
<b>Ravina said</b> a different explanation: According to Rabbi Yehuda, <b>with regard to repaying</b> a debt, <b>a gentile is always distressed,</b> even after he repays a debt. However, in general, even Rabbi Yehuda accepts the principle of taking into account the joy that will be experienced at a later time.
With regard to applying lime to one’s body, <b>Rav Yehuda said: The daughters of Israel who reached</b> physical <b>maturity, but had not</b> yet <b>reached the age</b> of majority, i.e., girls that were less than twelve years of age, and therefore embarrassed by their precocious physical development, would seek to remove the excess hair from their bodies. <b>The poor</b> among them would <b>smear their</b> skin <b>with lime</b> in order to remove the hair. <b>The wealthy ones</b> would <b>smear themselves with fine flour</b> for this purpose, <b>and the daughters of kings would smear themselves with <i>shemen hamor</i>. As it is stated</b> with regard to the women who were brought to King Ahasuerus: <b>“Six months with <i>shemen hamor</i>”</b> (Esther 2:12).
The Gemara asks: <b>What is <i>shemen hamor</i>? Rav Huna bar Ḥiyya said:</b> This is the perfume called <b><i>setaket</i>. Rav Yirmeya bar Ami said:</b> It is <b>olive oil</b> extracted <b>from</b> an olive <b>that</b> has <b>not</b> yet <b>reached one-third</b> of its growth; the acidic oil is effective as a depilatory.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> Wherever the Mishna mentions <b><i>anpikinon</i>,</b> it is referring to <b>olive oil</b> from an olive <b>that has not yet reached one-third</b> of its growth. <b>And why do</b> women <b>spread it</b> on the body? It is due to the fact <b>that it removes the hair and pampers the skin.</b>
It was related that <b>Rav Beivai had a daughter,</b> and <b>he spread</b> lime <b>on her limb</b> by <b>limb,</b> which caused her hair to fall out and her skin to whiten. She became so beautiful that when marrying her off, <b>he took four hundred dinars for her. There was a certain gentile in his neighborhood who</b> also <b>had a daughter</b> and wanted to do this as well. <b>He spread her</b> entire body with lime all <b>at once, and she died.</b> That gentile <b>said: Beivai killed my daughter. Rav Naḥman said: Rav Beivai, who</b> regularly <b>drinks beer, his daughters require</b> that they be <b>smeared</b> with lime, as beer causes hair growth, <b>but we who do not drink beer, our daughters do not require</b> that they be <b>smeared</b> with lime, as they have little hair even without this treatment.

Daf 10a

§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>A layman,</b> who is not a skilled tailor, may <b>sew in his</b> usual <b>manner</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> in which an individual is considered <b>a layman?</b> The Sages <b>of the school of Rabbi Yannai said: Anyone who cannot</b> deftly <b>pull a full needle</b> of stiches through the fabric <b>at the same time</b> is considered a layman. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina said: Anyone who cannot form a straight seam on the hem [<i>ḥefet</i>] of his shirt</b> is considered a layman.
§ It was further taught in the mishna: <b>A craftsman,</b> however, <b>may form</b> only <b>temporary stitches [<i>makhliv</i>].</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> meant by <b>forming temporary stitches? Rabbi Yoḥanan said: He</b> does not sew all the stitches that are necessary, but rather <b>he skips</b> certain stitches in the middle (Rabbeinu Hananel). <b>Rabba bar Shmuel said:</b> He sews stitches that are like <b>the teeth of a dog.</b> He does not sew all the stitches in a straight line, so that they resemble the teeth of a dog, which also are not straight.
§ It was also taught in the mishna: <b>One may interweave</b> the cords attached to the frame of <b>a bed.</b> Rabbi Yosei says: One may only tighten the cords. The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> meant by <b>interweaving, and what is</b> meant by <b>tightening? When Rav Dimi came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, <b>he said: Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rabbi Asi disagree about</b> the matter, <b>and both of them</b> reported their opinions <b>in the name of Ḥizkiyya and Rabbi Yoḥanan. One</b> Sage <b>said: Interweaving</b> involves placing and tying the cords of the bed both <b>lengthwise and widthwise,</b> whereas <b>tightening</b> means arranging the cords only <b>lengthwise and not widthwise. And one</b> Sage <b>said: Interweaving</b> involves arranging the cords only <b>lengthwise and not widthwise, while tightening</b> means <b>that if</b> a cord <b>became loose, he</b> may <b>tighten it</b> but he may not tie any new cords.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so? Didn’t Rav Taḥlifa bar Shaul teach</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>And</b> both Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei <b>agree that one may not braid cords anew</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival? <b>Granted, according to the one who said</b> that <b>interweaving</b> means tying the cords <b>lengthwise and widthwise, and tightening</b> means tying them only <b>lengthwise and not widthwise, this</b> explanation is consistent with <b>that which Rav Taḥlifa taught: And they</b> both <b>agree that one may not braid cords anew,</b> as even though one is permitted to arrange the cords of the bed, he is not permitted to produce new cords.
<b>However, according to the one who said</b> that <b>interweaving</b> means tying the cords only <b>lengthwise and not widthwise,</b> while <b>tightening</b> means <b>that if</b> a cord <b>became loose, he</b> may <b>tighten it,</b> there is a difficulty. <b>Now</b> that <b>you said</b> that tying the cords <b>lengthwise and widthwise, no,</b> it is not permitted even though the cords are readily available, <b>is</b> it <b>necessary</b> to say that braiding <b>cords anew</b> is prohibited? The Gemara comments: Indeed <b>it</b> is <b>difficult.</b>
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said to Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Avin: Is there one who says that interweaving</b> means tying the cords <b>lengthwise, but not widthwise? Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Kelim</i> 16:1) that <b>Rabbi Meir says: A bed</b> is considered a utensil and becomes susceptible to ritual impurity <b>from when one interweaves three</b> rows of <b>meshes in it,</b> i.e., three rows lengthwise and widthwise? It is clear from here that interweaving a bed involves tying its cords lengthwise and widthwise.
<b>Rather, when Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia <b>he stated</b> a different version of the tradition: <b>With regard to interweaving, everyone agrees</b> that it is referring to tying the cords of a bed <b>lengthwise and widthwise. When they disagree it is only about</b> the meaning of <b>tightening. One Sage holds</b> that <b>tightening</b> means arranging the cords only <b>lengthwise and not widthwise. And one Sage holds</b> that it means that <b>if</b> a cord <b>became loose, he may tighten it.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may interweave</b> the cords attached to the frame of <b>a bed</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>and needless to say one may tighten</b> those cords; <b>this is the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yosei says: One may</b> only <b>tighten</b> the ropes <b>but not interweave</b> them. <b>And some say: One may not tighten</b> them <b>at all.</b>
<b>Granted, according to the one who said</b> that <b>tightening</b> means tying the cords only <b>lengthwise and not widthwise, this is</b> why the opinion presented as: <b>Some say, comes to disagree</b> and says that one may not tie the cords at all, even only lengthwise. However, if the cords became loose, all agree that they may be tightened. <b>But according to the one who said</b> that tightening means <b>that if</b> a cord <b>became loose he</b> may <b>tighten it,</b> then <b>according to</b> the opinion presented here as: <b>Some say,</b> may <b>he not even tighten</b> the cords of his bed? How, then, would he be able to sleep on it?
The Gemara answers: <b>Yes,</b> indeed, according to this opinion, it is prohibited even to tighten the cords of one’s bed. <b>Since it is possible</b> for him <b>to fill</b> the depression in his bed <b>with clothing,</b> so that he can sleep there, <b>he</b> may <b>not exert</b> himself during the week of the Festival and fix the bed in a more permanent manner.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One may set up an oven, a stove, and a mill on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival. Rabbi Yehuda says: One may not chisel millstones for the first time</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> meant by <b>chiseling</b> millstones? <b>Rav Yehuda said: Cutting</b> grooves into <b>the millstones.</b> As the millstones wear down over time and become smooth, one must occasionally cut grooves into the stones so that they can grind properly. <b>Rav Yeḥiel said:</b> Cutting out <b>an eyehole</b> in the upper millstone, through which the grain can pass, so that it can be ground between the two millstones.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may set up an oven and a stove on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival, provided that he does not</b> fully <b>complete the labor;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. But the Rabbis say: He</b> may <b>even complete</b> the labor. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says in the name of</b> Rabbi Eliezer: <b>One may set up a new</b> millstone <b>and one may chisel an old</b> millstone. <b>And some say: One may not chisel</b> a millstone <b>at all.</b>
<b>Granted, according to the one who said</b> that <b>chiseling</b> a millstone means <b>cutting</b> grooves into <b>the millstones, this is</b> how <b>you</b> can <b>find</b> this activity relevant <b>to an old</b> mill. <b>But according to the one who said</b> it means cutting out <b>an eyehole, why does an old</b> millstone <b>need an eyehole</b> to be cut out? It must have one already. The Gemara answers: This is referring to a case <b>where he wants to widen</b> the hole <b>a little more.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Huna</b> once <b>heard a certain man cutting</b> grooves into <b>his millstone on the intermediate days of a Festival. He said: Who is this?</b> May <b>his body be desecrated, for he desecrates the intermediate days of a Festival.</b> The Gemara comments: Rav Huna <b>holds in accordance</b> with the statement cited as: <b>Some say,</b> that one is not permitted to roughen his millstones at all.
<b>Rav Ḥama taught</b> as follows: <b>One may cut</b> grooves into <b>millstones on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival. In the name of Rabbi Meir he said: Even</b> with regard to <b>the horse that</b> one <b>rides upon and the donkey that he rides upon, he is permitted to trim</b> their <b>hooves</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival so that they do not suffer when they walk.

Daf 10b

<b>However,</b> with regard to the hooves of <b>a donkey</b> that turns the <b>millstones, no,</b> they may not be trimmed. This is unnecessary on the intermediate days of a Festival, as one may only grind grain that one needs to use on the Festival itself, and the donkey can perform this task even if its hooves are not trimmed.
<b>Rav Yehuda permits</b> one <b>to trim the hooves of the donkey</b> that turns <b>a mill, to set</b> the stones of <b>a mill</b> in their place, <b>to build</b> the base of <b>a mill, to construct</b> a water <b>channel</b> to carry water that drives <b>a mill, and to build a stable</b> for horses on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rava permitted</b> one <b>to groom a horse, to build a trough, and to build a bench</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival.
<b>Rava</b> also <b>permitted</b> one <b>to let the blood of an animal</b> for medical purposes <b>on the intermediate days of a Festival. Abaye said to him:</b> A <i>baraita</i> <b>is taught that supports you: One may let blood from an animal, and one</b> does <b>not withhold</b> any <b>medical treatment from an animal on the intermediate days of a Festival.</b>
<b>Rava</b> further <b>permitted</b> one <b>to scrub garments</b> as part of the laundry process. <b>What is the reason? It is the work of a layman</b> and not the work of a craftsman, which is prohibited on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rav Yitzḥak bar Ami said that Rav Ḥisda said: Tying sleeves</b> to create pleats <b>is prohibited. What is the reason? It is the work of a craftsman</b> and is consequently prohibited on the intermediate days of a Festival.
§ <b>Rava said:</b> With regard to one <b>who clears the ground</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival, the following distinction applies: If <b>his intention</b> is to prepare <b>a threshing floor, it is permitted,</b> but if <b>his intention</b> is to prepare <b>the ground</b> for planting, <b>it is prohibited.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of each case? The Gemara explains: If he casts earth from one <b>mound [<i>mulya</i>] onto</b> another <b>mound, or</b> from <b>one furrow into</b> another <b>furrow [<i>natza</i>],</b> it is clear that he is doing this <b>with the intention of</b> preparing <b>a threshing floor,</b> as his sole interest is the level area in the middle and not the ground that surrounds it. However, if <b>he takes</b> earth <b>from a mound and casts it into a furrow,</b> he is presumably doing this <b>with the intention of</b> preparing <b>the ground</b> for planting, which is prohibited on the intermediate days of a Festival due to its similarity to the prohibited labor of plowing.
<b>And Rava said:</b> With regard to <b>one who clears</b> his field from <b>twigs</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival, the following distinction applies: If <b>his intention</b> is to use <b>the twigs it is permitted,</b> but if <b>his intention</b> is to prepare <b>the ground</b> for planting, <b>it is prohibited.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of each case? The Gemara explains: If <b>he takes the large</b> twigs <b>and leaves the small</b> ones, it is clear that he is doing this <b>with the intention of</b> using <b>the twigs.</b> But if <b>he takes</b> both <b>the large</b> twigs <b>and the small</b> ones, it appears as though that he is doing so <b>with the intention of</b> preparing <b>the ground,</b> and it is prohibited.
<b>And Rava</b> further <b>said:</b> With regard to <b>one who opens the</b> gates of <b>a water</b> channel running <b>through his land</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival, the following distinction applies: If <b>his intention</b> is to catch <b>the fish</b> that come along with the water, <b>it is permitted,</b> but if <b>his intention</b> is to water <b>the ground, it is prohibited.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of each case? The Gemara explains: If <b>he opens two gates</b> in the water channel, <b>one above</b> his field <b>and one below,</b> so the water that flows in also flows out, it is clear that he is doing this <b>with the intention of</b> catching <b>fish,</b> as the water will flow out and only the fish will remain in the indentations in his field. But if <b>he opens</b> only <b>one</b> gate, he is presumably doing this <b>with the intention of</b> watering <b>the ground,</b> as it is evident that he wants the channel to fill and the water to overflow into the field.
<b>And Rava</b> also <b>said:</b> With regard to <b>one who cuts</b> the branches of his <b>date palm</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival, the following distinction applies: If he does this <b>with the intention of</b> feeding <b>his animals, it is permitted,</b> but if he does it <b>with the intention of</b> enhancing the growth of his <b>date palm, it is prohibited.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of each case? The Gemara explains: If <b>he takes all</b> of the branches <b>from one side</b> of the tree, he is doing this <b>with the intention of</b> feeding the branches to <b>his animals,</b> as it is evident that he is not concerned with the damage to the date palm. However, if he takes the branches <b>from this side</b> of the tree <b>and</b> also <b>from the other side,</b> it appears as though he is doing so <b>with the intention of</b> enhancing the growth of <b>the date palm, and it is prohibited.</b>
<b>And Rava</b> further <b>said: With regard to these unripe dates, cutting them</b> into pieces in order to render them fit for eating <b>is permitted</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival, but <b>preserving them is prohibited,</b> as they will not be ready for use until after the Festival. <b>Rav Pappa said: Since they</b> are likely to <b>become infested with worms</b> if they are not properly attended to, <b>they are</b> treated <b>like merchandise that will be lost, and it is permitted</b> to preserve them on the intermediate days of a Festival.
§ <b>And Rava</b> also <b>said: Any commerce is prohibited</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Avin said: But</b> with regard to <b>a matter that,</b> if left unattended, will result in significant <b>loss, it is permitted.</b> The Gemara relates that <b>Ravina</b> once <b>had a</b> joint <b>business</b> venture <b>that could have been sold for six thousand</b> dinars, but <b>he delayed selling it</b> until <b>after the intermediate days of a Festival, and he sold it</b> then <b>for twelve thousand</b> dinars.
It is further related that <b>Ravina had lent money to the residents of the fortress of Shanvata. He came before Rav Ashi</b> and <b>said to him: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to going to them now,</b> on the intermediate days of the Festival, to collect my debt? Rav Ashi <b>said to him: Since it is now that you will find them,</b> whereas <b>on other days you will not find them,</b> as during the year these people would travel from place to place to earn their livelihood, <b>this is</b> treated <b>like merchandise that will be lost, and it is</b> therefore <b>permitted.</b>
The Gemara adds: <b>So too, it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>with regard to idol worship like this case: One may go</b>

Daf 11a

<b>to a fair of</b> pagan <b>gentiles</b> held on a day they have designated for idol worship <b>and buy animals, slaves, maidservants, houses, fields, and vineyards</b> from them. <b>And he may write</b> the necessary deeds <b>and confirm</b> them <b>in their courts [<i>arkaot</i>]</b> by verifying the signatures on the documents to prevent the sellers from appealing and retracting the sale. Although this behavior might be construed as a display of respect for the gentiles and their idols, it is nevertheless permitted <b>because it is as if he were rescuing</b> his property <b>from their hands,</b> as he would not have another opportunity to acquire these items. This indicates that time-sensitive transactions are considered like cases where one might suffer financial loss, and are permitted.
It was related that <b>Rav permitted Ḥiyya bar Ashi to weave</b> simple <b>traps [<i>oharei</i>] on the intermediate days of a Festival.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for this? It is because <b>it is the work of a layman. However,</b> complex <b>nets [<i>izlei</i>] are prohibited. What is the reason? It is the work of a craftsman,</b> which is prohibited on the intermediate days of a Festival.
It was further related that <b>Rav Yehuda permitted Ami the oven maker to construct ovens</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>and Rabba bar Ashabi to craft sieves.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Is that</b> really <b>so? Didn’t Rabba bar Shmuel teach</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>And they</b> all <b>agree that one may not construct a new oven</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. The Gemara answers that this is <b>not difficult: Here,</b> where Rav Yehuda permitted constructing an oven, he was referring <b>to the summer,</b> i.e., when the weather is hot and dry, so the oven can dry out and already be fit for use on the Festival; <b>there,</b> where the <i>baraita</i> prohibits constructing an oven, it is referring to <b>the rainy season,</b> i.e., when the weather is such that the oven will not be ready for use on the Festival.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One may construct a railing for a roof or a balcony</b> if it is done in a nonprofessional manner, as <b>the work of a layman, but not</b> if it is done skillfully, as <b>the work of a craftsman. One may plaster the cracks</b> in an oven <b>and roll over them with a roller,</b> a wooden tool used to smooth out clay, <b>with a hand or a foot, but not with a presser,</b> a tool that is specially designed for this task.
With regard to <b>the hinge</b> of a door, <b>and the cylinder</b> of the hinge, <b>and the</b> cross <b>beam</b> that holds the door, <b>and a lock and a key that broke, one may fix them on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival</b> as these items are essential for the Festival and their repair cannot be delayed. This is permitted, <b>provided that he does not intend</b> ahead of time <b>to do his labor on the Festival</b> and delay it until that time.
<b>And</b> with regard to <b>all preserved food from which one can eat on the Festival,</b> since they become ready to be eaten in a short amount of time, <b>he</b> may <b>preserve them</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> in which a railing is considered <b>the work of a layman? Rav Yosef said:</b> When it is made <b>with palm branches and</b> the branches of <b>bay trees,</b> which do not create a permanent structure. <b>It was taught in a <i>baraita</i></b> that <b>he fills in</b> the space <b>with stone but he does not plaster</b> the stones <b>with clay.</b>
It was taught in the mishna: <b>One may plaster the cracks</b> in an oven <b>and roll over them with a roller,</b> with a hand or a foot, but not with a presser. The Gemara asks: <b>Now</b> that <b>you said</b> that <b>it is permitted</b> to roll over the cracks <b>with a roller, is</b> it <b>necessary</b> to say that one may plaster the cracks <b>with a hand or a foot?</b> The Gemara answers that <b>this is what</b> the mishna <b>is saying: One may plaster the cracks</b> in an oven <b>and roll over them as if with a roller,</b> meaning <b>with a hand or a foot, but not with a presser,</b> which is a craftsman’s tool made especially for this.
It was taught in the mishna: With regard to <b>the hinge</b> of a door, <b>and the cylinder</b> of the hinge, <b>and the</b> cross <b>beam</b> that holds the door, <b>and a lock and a key that broke, one may fix them on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival.</b> The Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> to this from a different mishna (<i>Ma’aser Sheni</i> 5:15): <b>Until the days</b> of Yoḥanan the High Priest, workmen’s <b>hammers would beat in Jerusalem</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. One can deduce from this mishna that <b>until his days, yes,</b> the workmen’s hammers would beat, <b>but from then on, no,</b> because Yoḥanan the High Priest banned such work. This seems to contradict the mishna here that permits fixing certain items on the intermediate days of the Festival.
The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> is <b>not difficult: There,</b> when it is prohibited, it is referring to the work of <b>smiths,</b> which generates a great deal of noise; <b>here,</b> where it is permitted, it is referring to the work <b>of carpenters,</b> which is much quieter.
<b>Rav Ḥisda strongly objects to this:</b> If so, people <b>will say</b> that <b>a loud noise is prohibited,</b> but <b>a soft noise is permitted. Rather, Rav Ḥisda said</b> that the apparent contradiction is <b>not difficult</b> for a different reason: <b>This</b> mishna that permits labor is referring to <b>sickles,</b> as sickles emit no noise at all; whereas <b>that</b> mishna that prohibits labor is referring to <b>chisels. Rav Pappa said: Here</b> in the mishna it is referring to the period <b>before the decree</b> of Yoḥanan the High Priest; whereas <b>there</b> in the other mishna it is referring to the period <b>after the decree.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi said: That</b> mishna, which prohibits this labor, was taught in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda;</b> while <b>this</b> mishna was taught in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei. As Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Avdimi said: Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> who <b>taught</b> that all labor performed <b>on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival</b> must be done <b>with a change,</b> i.e., in an altered manner, even <b>with</b> regard to <b>a matter that will be lost</b> if timely action is not taken? It is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> as Rabbi Yosei permits doing labor that will prevent financial loss even when it is done in the usual manner.
<b>Ravina said: In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>do we now lift the lintel above the door</b> in order to drive in the nails that had loosened <b>on the intermediate days of a Festival</b> in the usual manner? <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this done? It is done <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> who permits labor that will prevent financial loss even when it is done in the usual manner.
§ It was taught in the mishna: With regard to all <b>preserved food from which he can eat on the Festival, he</b> may <b>preserve them</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. The Gemara relates that the <b>Bedita</b> River once <b>overflowed,</b> depositing large quantities of <b>fish</b> in small water deposits on the river bank on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Everyone went and trapped and brought</b> home <b>fish,</b> and <b>Rava permitted them to salt</b> some <b>of</b> the fish.
<b>Abaye said to him: Didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: With regard to <b>all preserved food from which he can eat on the Festival, he</b> may <b>preserve them</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival? In this case it was impossible to eat that much fish on the Festival, and therefore preserving them should have been prohibited. Rava <b>said to him: Since they initially brought</b> these fish <b>with the intention of eating</b> them on the Festival, and it only became clear afterward that they had brought too many, <b>and if one would leave them</b> without salting them <b>they would be ruined, they are</b> treated <b>like merchandise that will be lost, and</b> therefore preserving them <b>is permitted.</b>
<b>And some say</b> that it did not happen in this manner, rather, <b>Rava permitted them to trap</b> the fish, <b>to bring them</b> home, <b>and to salt</b> them. <b>Abaye said to him: Didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: With regard to <b>all preserved food from which he can eat on the Festival, he</b> may <b>preserve them</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival? These fish were heavily salted and consequently could not be eaten until they thoroughly dried out.
Rava <b>said to him: These</b> fish that were just salted <b>can also be eaten</b> now, <b>by pressing</b> out the salt and brine, and therefore, they too are fit to be eaten on the Festival, <b>as in that</b> case <b>of Shmuel,</b> where <b>they prepared</b> recently salted fish <b>for him by pressing</b> it <b>sixty times and he ate</b> the fish. The Gemara relates that <b>Rava happened</b> to come <b>to the house of the Exilarch</b> and <b>they prepared</b> freshly salted fish <b>for him by pressing</b> it <b>sixty times, and he ate</b> it.
Apropos eating fish in various ways, the Gemara relates that <b>Rav</b> once <b>happened</b> to come <b>to the house of Rav Shapir,</b> and <b>they brought before him a certain fish,</b> of which <b>one- third</b> was <b>cooked, one-third</b> was <b>salted</b> and made edible via pressing, and <b>one-third</b> was <b>broiled.</b>
<b>Rav said: Adda the fisherman told me</b> that <b>a fish</b> that has sat for some time and is <b>close to spoiling is</b> at its <b>best. And Rav</b> also <b>said: Adda the fisherman told me:</b> With regard to <b>a fish, broil it with its brother,</b> i.e., with salt, which, like fish, also comes from the sea; <b>place it in its father,</b> i.e., in water; and <b>eat it with its son,</b> i.e., with fish brine which comes from it; <b>and drink after it its father,</b> i.e., water.
<b>And</b> in addition <b>Rav said: Adda the fisherman told me:</b> After eating <b>fish, cress, and milk, let the body carry them and let not the bed carry them,</b> i.e., it is advisable to go for a walk after eating them, rather than immediately going to sleep. <b>And Rav said: Adda the fisherman told me:</b> After eating <b>fish, cress, and milk,</b> it is better to drink <b>water and not beer,</b> and if he has no water, he should drink <b>beer and not wine,</b> in order to avoid harming his body.

Daf 11b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The mishna discusses <b>one who had</b> already <b>turned over his olives</b> as part of the process of preparing them for pressing, <b>and mourning</b> for a close relative <b>befell him</b> and as a result he was prohibited from engaging in work, <b>or</b> some other <b>unavoidable accident</b> occurred, <b>or</b> his <b>workers misled him,</b> promising to come but failing to do so, so that he could not press his olives before the Festival. Under these circumstances, during the intermediate days of the Festival, <b>he may</b> place the olives in the press and <b>load the beam</b> with weights for the <b>initial</b> pressing of the olives and <b>leave it</b> this way <b>until after the Festival;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei says: He may press</b> the olives <b>and complete</b> the process <b>and</b> then <b>plug</b> each barrel of oil <b>in its</b> usual <b>manner.</b> Since delay can entail financial loss, the Sages did not require him to alter the normal process of extracting the oil.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: The <i>tanna</i> <b>opens</b> the mishna <b>with</b> a case of <b>mourning and ends</b> it <b>with</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of the intermediate days of <b>the Festival,</b> leaving the connection between them unclear. <b>Rav Sheisha, son of Rav Idi, said: That is to say: Actions that are permitted on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival are prohibited during the days of one’s mourning.</b> Therefore, if a mourning period befell one before the Festival, and he could not prepare properly for the Festival due to the prohibition against performance of labor by a mourner, he is permitted to perform labor during the intermediate days of the Festival to avoid financial loss.
<b>Rav Ashi said</b> that the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna <b>is speaking</b> utilizing the didactic style of: <b>It is not necessary,</b> and he is teaching two separate <i>halakhot</i>, one about mourning and the other about the Festival: <b>It is not necessary</b> to state that <b>during the days of his mourning, when</b> the prohibition against labor <b>is rabbinic, it is permitted</b> to perform labor that, if delayed, could cause serious loss; <b>but even on the</b> intermediate days of the <b>Festival, when the prohibition against</b> performing <b>labor is by Torah law,</b> performing such labor is permitted because <b>in a case of a loss the Sages permitted</b> it.
The Gemara comments: <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rav Sheisha, son of Rav Idi: These are actions that</b> others <b>perform for the mourner during the days of his mourning:</b> If <b>his olives have</b> already <b>been turned over, they may load</b> them <b>for him</b> into the olive press, <b>and</b> likewise if <b>his cask</b> needs <b>to be plugged, or</b> if <b>his flax</b> needs <b>to be lifted out of the soaking pool</b> where it will rot if not removed in time, <b>or</b> if <b>his wool</b> needs <b>to be lifted from the cauldron</b> into which it was placed for dyeing, they perform all these actions for him. <b>They</b> also <b>irrigate his field when his turn for water arrives,</b> as in many places, each field would receive water in turn according to a prearranged schedule. If one does not utilize the water when his turn arrives, he loses that water. This supports Rav Sheisha’s opinion that the prohibition against performing labor is waived in cases of loss only on the Festival. Under such circumstances, labor is prohibited to a mourner and in cases of loss may only be performed for him by others.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: They also sow for him a plowed field or a field set aside for flax,</b> where the time of sowing is critical. If the field is sown later, it will cause a loss. The Sages <b>said to him: If</b> that field <b>is not sown in the early</b> season, <b>it can be sown in the later</b> season; and similarly, <b>if it is not sown with flax, it can be sown with another species.</b> Therefore, this is not considered a great loss, and the Sages did not permit it.
<b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says:</b> If <b>his olives have</b> already <b>been turned over and there is no skilled worker</b> available who knows how to press them properly <b>but him,</b> or <b>his cask</b> needs <b>to be plugged and there is no skilled worker</b> available <b>but him,</b> or <b>his flax</b> needs <b>to be lifted out of the soaking pool, or his wool</b> needs <b>to be lifted from the cauldron, and there is no skilled worker</b> available <b>but him, he may perform</b> all of these actions himself <b>in private.</b>
<b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel said further: If</b> this mourner <b>was a craftsman</b> who serves <b>the public,</b> providing a necessary service, <b>or a barber or a bath attendant for the public, and the time of the Festival arrived,</b> i.e., it was the eve of a Festival, <b>and there is no</b> other <b>skilled worker</b> available <b>but him,</b> then <b>he may perform</b> the labor even in public, as the needs of the public takes precedence over the mourning of an individual.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Tenant farmers,</b> who farm others’ land in exchange for a certain percentage of the crop, <b>and leasers,</b> who work the land in exchange for a fixed amount of the crop, <b>and contractors,</b> who work the land in exchange for a set amount of money, are included amongst <b>those for whom others may perform</b> their labor when they are in mourning. In contrast, <b>donkey drivers, camel drivers, and sailors</b> are <b>those</b> for whom others <b>may not perform</b> their work during their mourning periods. <b>However, if they</b> or their animals <b>were leased or hired out to others, then they may perform</b> work, because they were hired personally and cannot be replaced without loss. <b>A day laborer, even</b> if he is <b>in a different city</b> where his mourning status is not public knowledge, <b>may not perform</b> his work. If <b>a commission for others was his responsibility, even if it was by contract</b> and he was paid not by the day but upon its completion, <b>he may not perform</b> that labor.
The statement in the <i>baraita</i> that one may not perform a task for others <b>even if it was by contract</b> implies that <b>it is not necessary</b> to state that if it was <b>not by contract</b> the work is prohibited to a mourner. The Gemara asks: <b>On the contrary,</b> when he works <b>by contract it is considered as</b> though it were <b>his</b> own work, since he simply receives payment for the completed task. Non-contracted labor, where he is paid for the time he works, is not viewed as his own and it is conceivable that it would be permitted during his mourning period. The Gemara concludes: <b>Rather,</b> emend the text and <b>say</b> that the <i>baraita</i> should read: If a commission for others was his responsibility, <b>whether by contract or not by contract, he may not perform</b> it.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If <b>he commissioned others</b> to perform <b>his work,</b> then if it was <b>in his house they may not perform</b> the work, but if it was <b>in another house they may perform</b> it.
The Gemara relates that <b>Maryon, son of Ravin, and Mar, son of Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, held a team of</b> two <b>oxen</b> in partnership <b>between them,</b> one ox apiece. <b>An event,</b> i.e., a death in the family, <b>befell Mar, son of Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, and he detached his</b> ox <b>from the team,</b> preventing any work from being performed by the team, because he did not want work performed with his animal during his days of mourning. <b>Rav Ashi said: A distinguished man like Mar, son of Rav Aḥa, is behaving this</b> way? <b>Granted, he is not concerned about his own loss,</b> but <b>is he not concerned about the loss of others,</b> i.e., his partner? <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> cited above: <b>If they</b> or their animals <b>were hired out or leased to others, then they may perform</b> their work? In that case, why not allow the partner to work with the animal of Mar, son of Rav Aḥa?
The Gemara explains: <b>But</b> Mar, son of Rav Aḥa, <b>maintains: An important person is different.</b> Even if strictly speaking something is permitted, an important person must be more rigorous with himself, so that people learn not to take the <i>halakha</i> lightly.

Daf 12a

§ <b>Shmuel said:</b> With regard to gentile <b>contractors,</b> who are paid for the completed job rather than by the day, <b>it is prohibited</b> for them to work for a Jew <b>within</b> the boundaries of <b>the</b> Shabbat <b>limit</b> on a day that it is prohibited for the Jew to work. It looks like the Jew assigned work for them to do on that day, even though they in fact make their own schedule. <b>Outside the</b> Shabbat <b>limit,</b> where Jews will not see the work being done, <b>it is permitted.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said: And even outside the</b> Shabbat <b>limit, we said only</b> that it is permissible to let them work <b>when there is no</b> other <b>city in close proximity to there, but if there is</b> another <b>city in close proximity to there, it is prohibited,</b> since the work is likely to be seen by a Jew.
<b>Rav Mesharshiyya said: And even when there is no</b> other <b>city in close proximity, we said</b> that it is permissible for gentile contractors to work for a Jew <b>only on <i>Shabbatot</i> and Festivals, when</b> Jewish <b>people do not routinely go there,</b> i.e., to the place where the work is being performed. <b>However, on the intermediate days of a Festival, when</b> Jewish <b>people routinely come and go from there</b> and see the work being done, <b>it is prohibited.</b>
The Gemara relates about <b>Mar Zutra, son of Rav Naḥman,</b> that gentile <b>contractors built a mansion [<i>apadana</i>] for him outside the</b> Shabbat <b>limit. Rav Safra and Rav Huna bar Ḥinnana happened</b> to come there, <b>but they did not enter it,</b> because it had been built on Shabbat in a prohibited manner. <b>And there are</b> those <b>who say: Even he,</b> Mar Zutra himself, <b>did not enter it.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t Shmuel say:</b> With regard to gentile <b>contractors, within</b> the boundaries of <b>the</b> Shabbat <b>limit, it is prohibited</b> to let them work on Shabbat, but <b>outside the</b> Shabbat <b>limit it is permitted?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>An important person is different</b> and must behave in a stricter manner. <b>And there are</b> those <b>who say: He provided them with assistance with straw</b> for making the bricks. Consequently, they were not fully independent contractors, and therefore it was prohibited for them to work on Shabbat, even outside the Shabbat limit.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Ḥama permitted the bath attendants [<i>abunagrei</i>] of the Exilarch’s house to work for them,</b> i.e., the Exilarch and his household, <b>on the intermediate days of a Festival. He said</b> in explanation of this leniency: <b>Since they do not take payment</b> for working during the week of the Festival, they <b>are</b> merely <b>conferring a favor</b> on the Exilarch. Therefore, it is not viewed as actual labor, <b>and we have no</b> problem <b>with it.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Gentiles <b>may receive contract work on</b> the intermediate days of a <b>Festival</b> in order <b>to execute it after the Festival, but it is prohibited</b> to execute it <b>during the Festival</b> week itself. <b>The principle of the matter</b> is: <b>Any</b> action <b>that</b> a Jew <b>may perform</b> himself, <b>he may tell a gentile to perform, and any</b> action <b>that he may not perform</b> himself, <b>he may not tell a gentile to perform. It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: A gentile <b>may receive contract work on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival</b> in order <b>to execute it after the Festival, provided that he does not measure, weigh, or count,</b> in order to plan the work, <b>in the</b> same <b>manner as he does on an</b> ordinary <b>weekday.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may not breed an animal,</b> i.e., coax or compel a male to mate with a female, <b>on the intermediate days of a Festival,</b> as this is similar to prohibited labor. <b>Similarly, one may not breed a firstborn</b> animal, <b>nor an unfit consecrated animal.</b> It is prohibited to ever work these animals because of their sanctified status, and breeding them resembles working them.
<b>It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may not breed an animal on the intermediate days of a Festival. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> If <b>a she-donkey yearns</b> for a mate because she is in heat, <b>one may breed her with a male, lest she cool off,</b> i.e., lest her estrus cycle pass without her mating, which will cause a significant loss. With regard to <b>all other animals</b> that are in heat, <b>they are</b> merely <b>brought into corrals</b> in which there are males, there being no need for further human intervention.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may not enclose</b> livestock in particular areas of a field, in order to fertilize that area with their manure, <b>on <i>Shabbatot</i>, or on Festivals, or on the intermediate days of a Festival. But if</b> the animals <b>came on their own</b> to the field, <b>it is permitted,</b> and he need not remove them although he benefits from their manure.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And one may not assist</b> gentile shepherds in pasturing their sheep in his field so that it will be fertilized, <b>nor may one provide them with a shepherd to</b> help <b>herd their sheep</b> all around the field so that it is completely fertilized. If the gentile shepherds <b>were hired by the week, by the month, by the year,</b> or <b>by the septennial</b> period, i.e., the seven years from one Sabbatical Year to the next, <b>one may assist them and provide them with a shepherd to herd their sheep.</b> Since they were hired for an extended period of time, it is not as though they were hired specifically for Shabbat or the Festival. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: On Shabbat</b> one may have the gentile pasture the sheep in one’s field <b>as a favor,</b> i.e. in order to earn one’s good graces, but not for pay; <b>on a Festival,</b> it is permitted even in exchange <b>for food;</b> and <b>on the</b> intermediate days of <b>the Festival,</b> it is permitted <b>for pay. Rav Yosef said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>And similarly,</b> with regard to <b>one whose wine was</b> already <b>in the</b> collection <b>pit</b> beside the wine press and needed to be removed lest it sour, <b>and mourning</b> for some close relative <b>or</b> some other <b>unavoidable accident befell him, or</b> his workers <b>misled him,</b> promising to come but failing to do so, during the intermediate days of the Festival <b>he may draw off</b> the wine <b>and complete</b> the process, <b>and</b> then <b>plug</b> each barrel of wine <b>in its</b> usual <b>manner;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei. Rabbi Yehuda says: He should construct a wooden panel</b> to cover the collection pit <b>so that</b> the wine <b>will not sour.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> both this mishna and the previous one (11b), about the olive press, are <b>necessary, as, if</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches us</b> only <b>the first</b> mishna, one might say that only <b>in that</b> case <b>does Rabbi Yosei say</b> that one is permitted to complete the process, <b>because</b> in the case of <b>oil, the</b> potential <b>loss is considerable. However,</b> in the case of <b>wine, where the loss is not</b> as <b>considerable,</b> one might say <b>that he concedes to Rabbi Yehuda</b> that it must be done in an altered manner. <b>And if he teaches us</b> only <b>the last</b> mishna, one might say that only <b>in that</b> case involving wine <b>did Rabbi Yehuda say</b> that one must be stringent, <b>but in the other</b> case involving oil one might <b>say that he concedes to Rabbi Yosei</b> and is lenient. Therefore, it is <b>necessary</b> to teach both cases.
<b>Rav Yitzḥak bar Abba said: Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> who teaches that labor performed <b>during the intermediate days of the Festival</b> must be performed in <b>an altered fashion</b> even <b>with regard to a matter that,</b> if unattended, will result in significant <b>loss?</b> That ruling is <b>not</b> in accordance with <b>Rabbi Yosei’s</b> opinion, as Rabbi Yosei permits one to perform labor that will prevent such a loss even in the usual manner. <b>Rav Yosef said:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> and it is permitted in cases of loss to perform labor in the usual manner.
The students <b>asked of Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to sealing a barrel of beer during the intermediate days of the Festival?</b> If it is not properly sealed, the beer will leak out through the sides of the barrel. <b>He said to them: Sinai,</b> i.e., Rav Yosef, <b>said:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei.</b> They asked: <b>Say that Rabbi Yosei said</b> that one may be lenient <b>about wine,</b> but <b>about beer does he say</b> the same thing? Beer is much cheaper than wine, and the loss would be much less significant.
The Gemara explains why no distinction should be made: With regard to <b>wine, what is the reason</b> that Rabbi Yosei was lenient? It is <b>because the loss</b> involved <b>is considerable;</b> with <b>beer as well, there is a loss</b> if the barrel is not properly sealed, <b>as Abaye said:</b> My foster <b>mother told me:</b> It is better to have a barrel <b>of six <i>se’a</i> that is sealed than</b> a barrel <b>of eight</b> <i>se’a</i> <b>that is not sealed,</b> as in the end more beer will remain in the smaller sealed barrel than in the larger unsealed one.
§ <b>Rav Ḥama bar Gurya said</b> that <b>Rav said: The <i>halakhot</i> relating to</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival resemble the <i>halakhot</i> relating to the Samaritans in the <i>halakha</i>.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what</b> aspect of <b>the <i>halakha</i></b> are they similar? <b>Rav Daniel bar Ketina said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> He meant <b>to convey that they are sterile, and one may not learn</b> further <i>halakhot</i> by extrapolation <b>one from one another</b> in both of these areas of <i>halakha</i>.
The Gemara offers an example: <b>For</b> instance, <b>Shmuel said: One may seal a jug with pitch, but one may not seal a barrel with pitch</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rav Dimi from Neharde’a said: One may seal a barrel with pitch, but one may not seal</b> a small <b>jug with pitch.</b> Their reasoning is as follows: <b>This Sage,</b> i.e., Rav Dimi, <b>was</b> more <b>concerned about</b> possible financial <b>loss,</b> and so he permitted sealing the larger vessel, for which the potential for loss is great, but not the smaller one. <b>And this Sage,</b> i.e., Shmuel <b>was concerned about</b> unnecessary <b>exertion</b> on the Festival, and therefore he permitted sealing the smaller vessel, which requires less effort.
<b>Abaye stated</b> a different principle: <b>We have a tradition</b> that <b>the <i>halakhot</i> of</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival are like the <i>halakhot</i> of Shabbat:</b>

Daf 12b

<b>Some of</b> the actions for which one is <b>exempt</b> by Torah law <b>are</b> nevertheless <b>prohibited, and some of them</b> are <b>permitted <i>ab initio</i>.</b>
§ The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Huna had his crop harvested for him on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival. Rabba bar Rav Huna raised an objection to</b> his father <b>Rav Huna</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may grind flour on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival for the sake of the Festival, but</b> if it is <b>not for the sake of the Festival,</b> it is <b>prohibited.</b> With regard to <b>a matter that,</b> if unattended, will result in significant <b>loss on the Festival, it is permitted to perform</b> labor to prevent that loss <b>on</b> the intermediate days of <b>the Festival.</b> For <b>a matter that</b> will not result <b>in loss</b> if unattended during the intermediate days of <b>the Festival, it is prohibited</b> to perform labor.
<b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said? In</b> the case of crops that are already <b>detached from the ground, but</b> in the case of crops that are still <b>attached to the ground, even if it all will be lost,</b> labor <b>is prohibited</b> during the intermediate days of the Festival. <b>And if one does not have anything to eat,</b> then <b>he may reap and bind sheaves, and thresh, and winnow</b> the grain in the wind, <b>and separate</b> the grain from the chaff, <b>and grind</b> the grain into flour, <b>provided that he does not thresh with cows,</b> the way that threshing is performed on an ordinary weekday. Why, then, did Rav Huna, who certainly had enough to eat, allow his workers to reap the field during the Festival week?
Rav Huna <b>said to</b> his son: The opinion expressed in this unattributed <i>baraita</i> <b>is an individual</b> opinion, <b>and we do not hold in accordance with it; as it is taught</b> in a different <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel stated a principle in the name of Rabbi Yosei:</b> In the case of <b>an item that is</b> already <b>detached from the ground, even if</b> only <b>a portion of it will be lost</b> by not working during the Festival, <b>it is permitted</b> to perform labor to prevent the loss, <b>whereas</b> for crops <b>that are</b> still <b>attached to the ground, even if all of them will be lost, it is prohibited</b> to perform labor for them. The opinion that all labor is prohibited for crops that have not yet been harvested is the opinion of an individual authority, Rabbi Yosei, with whom the Rabbis disagree.
The Gemara asks: <b>But if</b> the unattributed <i>baraita</i> reflects the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> and it is permitted to work when one has nothing to eat, <b>let him thresh even with cows.</b> Didn’t <b>Rav Yitzḥak bar Abba say: Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> that labor performed <b>on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival</b> must be done in <b>an altered manner</b> even <b>with regard to a matter that</b> will involve <b>a loss</b> if unattended? It is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> as Rabbi Yosei does not require altering the manner in which one performs the labor. Why, then, does the <i>baraita</i> say that the threshing must be done in an altered manner and not with cows?
The Gemara rejects this argument: Rabbi Yosei could have <b>said to you: Here, too, since one does not always thresh with cows,</b> threshing without them <b>now</b> on the intermediate days of the Festival <b>is also not</b> considered threshing in <b>an altered manner.</b> The prohibition against threshing with cows is not because one must perform that labor in an altered manner but in order to avoid attracting public attention to the fact that one is working during the Festival week.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may grind</b> flour <b>on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival for the sake of the Festival, but</b> if it is <b>not for the sake of the Festival,</b> it is <b>prohibited. And if he ground and left</b> some flour <b>over, it is permitted</b> for use after the Festival. Similarly, <b>one may cut down trees on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival for the sake of the Festival, but</b> if it is <b>not for the sake of the Festival</b> it is <b>prohibited. And if he cut down</b> trees for the Festival <b>and left</b> some wood <b>over, it is permitted</b> for use after the Festival.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>One may brew beer on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival for the sake of the Festival, but</b> if it is <b>not for the sake of the Festival,</b> it is <b>prohibited. And if he brewed</b> beer for the sake of the Festival <b>and left</b> some <b>over, it is permitted</b> for use after the Festival <b>provided that he does not employ an artifice,</b> exploiting the allowance to work on a Festival by knowingly making more than was needed.
The Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may brew beer on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival for the sake of the Festival, but</b> if it is <b>not for the sake of the Festival,</b> it is <b>prohibited.</b> This applies <b>both</b> to <b>date beer and</b> to <b>barley beer. And even though he has old</b> beer, <b>he may employ an artifice and drink from the new</b> beer, i.e., he may say he prefers new beer and brew a new batch, although he has enough old beer for the Festival. This statement contradicts the previous <i>baraita</i> that prohibited circumventing the prohibition against labor through artifice. The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> matter <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may not employ an artifice in this regard,</b> i.e., to circumvent the prohibition against labor during the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda says: One may employ an artifice.</b>
§ The Gemara relates that <b>Rav had his crop harvested on the intermediate days of a Festival. Shmuel heard</b> about this and <b>was dismayed.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Shall we say</b> that <b>Shmuel holds in accordance with the individual</b> opinion of Rabbi Yosei, who prohibits all labor for crops that are attached to the ground, even if inattention will result in significant loss? The Gemara answers: <b>No, it was a crop of wheat, which would not have been ruined</b> had the harvest been pushed off a week, and therefore Shmuel was offended.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> that <b>Rav acted in this manner?</b> The Gemara answers: Rav is in the category of <b>one who does not have anything to eat,</b> and that is why it was permitted to harvest his crops. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> why, then, did <b>Shmuel</b> disapprove? The Gemara explains: The people who reported Rav’s actions to Shmuel <b>did not complete</b> the story <b>for him. Alternatively, an important person</b> such as Rav <b>is different,</b> and Shmuel thought Rav should have been stringent even in his difficult circumstances.
It was further related that <b>Rabbi Yehuda Nesia</b> once <b>went out</b> on Shabbat <b>with a coral seal [<i>ḥumreta</i>]</b> on his ring <b>and drank water heated by a gentile cook. Rabbi Ami heard</b> about this and <b>was dismayed</b> about both things. <b>Rav Yosef said: What is the reason</b> that <b>he was dismayed? If</b> it was <b>because of the coral seal, isn’t</b> it <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Bracelets, nose rings, and rings are like all</b> other <b>utensils that may be carried in a courtyard?</b> Therefore, Rabbi Yehuda Nesia’s action conformed to <i>halakha</i>.
And <b>if</b> Rabbi Ami was dismayed <b>because</b> Rabbi Yehuda Nesia <b>drank water heated by a gentile cook [<i>kapeila</i>], didn’t Shmuel bar Yitzḥak say</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> With regard to <b>anything that is eaten raw,</b> like water, even if it was cooked by a gentile, <b>it is not subject to</b> the prohibition against eating <b>food cooked by gentiles?</b> The Gemara resolves both difficulties: <b>An important person is different,</b> and Rabbi Ami believed that Rabbi Yehuda Nesia should have been more stringent.
<b>Rav Ḥananel said</b> that <b>Rav said: A person may chop down a palm tree on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival, although he needs only its sawdust.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>Abaye cursed it,</b> i.e., the behavior of one who exerts such effort on the Festival for so small a need.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Ashi had a forest [<i>ava</i>] in Shelaniya. He went to cut it down on the intermediate days of the Festival. Rav Sheila from Shelaniya said to Rav Ashi: What is your opinion</b> about what you are doing? Do you rely on <b>that</b> which <b>Rav Ḥananel said</b> that <b>Rav said: A person may chop down a palm tree on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival even though he needs only its sawdust? Didn’t Abaye curse</b> such behavior? Rav Ashi <b>said to him: I did not hear</b> that Abaye said that; <b>that is to say,</b> in a delicate way, <b>I do not hold</b> in accordance with his opinion. <b>The</b> iron of Rav Ashi’s <b>ax</b> then <b>slipped</b> and <b>was about to cut off his leg. He left it,</b> ceasing his chopping, <b>and came back again</b> to chop down the forest only after the Festival.
The Gemara relates that <b>Rav Yehuda permitted uprooting flax, cutting hops, and uprooting sesame plants</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival. <b>Abaye said to Rav Yosef: Granted,</b> this is reasonable in the case of <b>flax,</b> which is <b>suitable for</b> use as <b>a cover [<i>ḥafifa</i>]</b> for various objects and therefore has a use even during the Festival, and so too <b>hops,</b> which are <b>suitable for</b> making <b>beer, but for what are sesame plants suitable,</b> since the sesame seeds require a long drying period before oil can be extracted from them? Rav Yosef said to him: <b>They are suitable for the seeds [<i>nazyei</i>] on them,</b> which can be consumed immediately.
It was further mentioned that <b>Rabbi Yannai had an orchard whose time</b> for harvesting the fruit <b>arrived during the intermediate days of the Festival. He harvested it</b> during the Festival, as the fruit would otherwise have spoiled. <b>The following year, everyone delayed</b> the harvest of <b>their orchards until the intermediate days of the Festival. Rabbi Yannai declared</b> the earnings from <b>his orchard ownerless that year,</b> since his harvesting of his fruit provided a misleading example for the public, causing many people to adopt an inappropriate leniency.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A person may bring his fruit in</b> from the field on the intermediate days of the Festival <b>because</b> he is concerned <b>about thieves, and he may draw his flax out of the soaking pool so that it is not ruined</b> from soaking too long in the water, <b>provided that he does not plan</b> from the outset to perform <b>his work on</b> the intermediate days of <b>the Festival. And</b> with regard to <b>all</b> of these cases, <b>if one planned</b> from the outset to perform <b>his labor on</b> the intermediate days of <b>the Festival,</b> the fruit of that labor <b>must be lost</b> and no benefit may be derived from it.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that one may bring in crops out of concern for thieves, <b>provided that he brings them into his house discreetly.</b>
The Gemara relates: <b>Rav Yosef had</b> wooden <b>beams,</b> which <b>he brought into</b> his house <b>during the day</b> on one of the intermediate days of a Festival.
<b>Abaye said to him: But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: One may bring in items <b>provided that he brings them into his house discreetly?</b> Rav Yosef <b>said to him: Discretion</b> with respect <b>to these</b> beams <b>is</b> moving them during <b>the day, since at night more people are required, and</b> also <b>torchbearers [<i>medokhrei denura</i>] are needed,</b> and <b>the matter</b> would be more <b>noticeable.</b>
The mishna states: <b>And he may draw his flax out of the soaking pool</b> so that it is not ruined from soaking too long in the water, provided that he does not plan from the outset to do his work on the intermediate days of the Festival. And with regard to anyone, if he planned from the outset to perform his labor on the intermediate days of the Festival, the fruit of that labor must be destroyed. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya asked Rabbi Zeira:</b> If <b>one planned</b> from the outset to perform <b>his labor on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival, and he died</b> after doing so, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Should his children be penalized after him</b> and have to lose the products of their father’s labor?
Even <b>if you might say</b> that

Daf 13a

in the case of <b>one who slit the ear of a firstborn</b> animal, the Sages <b>penalized his son after him,</b> that is <b>because the prohibition is by Torah law.</b> A firstborn animal is disqualified from sacrifice if it becomes blemished, rendering it permitted to be eaten as non-sacred meat. It is prohibited to deliberately engender a blemish in a firstborn animal, and one who does so is prohibited from eating the meat.
<b>And if you might say</b> that in the case of <b>one who sold his slave to a gentile and</b> then <b>died</b> the Sages <b>penalized his son after him,</b> that is <b>because every day</b> the slave <b>is in the gentile’s possession precludes him from</b> performing mitzvot. A Canaanite slave is obligated in the same mitzvot as a Jewish woman. If he is illicitly sold to a gentile and escapes, requiring the seller to compensate the gentile, the seller may not then re-enslave the slave, who is regarded as emancipated. The Sages extended this penalty to the owner’s heirs as well.
<b>What,</b> then, is the <i>halakha</i> <b>here,</b> where one planned from the outset to perform his work on the intermediate days of a Festival and then after performing the work he died? Should one say that <b>the Sages penalized the man</b> himself <b>and he is no longer</b> alive, <b>or perhaps the Sages imposed a penalty on the money,</b> such that no benefit may be derived from it, <b>and it</b> still <b>exists</b> in the hands of his heirs?
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yirmeya: <b>You</b> already <b>learned</b> the answer to your question in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 4:2): <b>A field whose thorns were removed during the Sabbatical Year may be sown in the eighth year,</b> as removing thorns is not full-fledged labor that renders the field prohibited; but <b>if it had been improved</b> with fertilizer, <b>or if it had been enclosed</b> so that animals therein would fertilize it with their manure, <b>it may not be sown in the eighth year.</b> The Sages imposed a penalty that one not benefit from prohibited labor. <b>And Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina said: We have a tradition</b> that if one <b>improved</b> his field in a prohibited manner <b>and</b> then <b>died, his son may sow it. Apparently, the Sages penalized</b> only <b>him,</b> the one who acted wrongly, but <b>the Sages did not penalize his son. Here, too,</b> with regard to work performed on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>the Sages penalized him,</b> but <b>the Sages did not penalize his son.</b>
<b>Abaye said: We have a tradition</b> that if one <b>rendered impure another’s ritually pure items and died</b> before paying, the Sages <b>did not penalize his son after him</b> and require him to pay for the damage. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? It is that <b>damage that is not evident,</b> i.e., that does not involve any physical change visible to the eye, <b>is not considered damage</b> by Torah law; nevertheless, the injured party suffers a loss, and <b>the Sages penalized</b> only him, but <b>the Sages did not penalize his son.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One may not purchase houses, slaves, and cattle</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>unless</b> it is <b>for the sake of the Festival, or</b> to provide <b>for the needs of the seller who does not have anything to eat.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rava asked Rav Naḥman: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>wages</b> for a worker <b>who does not have anything to eat?</b> Is it permitted to provide a poor worker with work on the intermediate days of the Festival? Rav Naḥman <b>said to him: We learned</b> this in the mishna: <b>Or</b> to provide <b>for the needs of the seller, if he has nothing to eat. What is added by</b> mentioning the condition that he has nothing to eat? Does it not <b>add</b> the case of <b>wages</b> and not only the case of one selling items he owns? Rava <b>said to him: No,</b> it <b>is</b> merely <b>explaining</b> what is meant by the needs of the seller.
<b>Abaye raised an objection</b> from a mishna (18b): <b>Promissory notes may not be written on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival, but if</b> the lender <b>does not trust</b> the borrower and is concerned that without such a note he will be unable to collect, <b>or if he has nothing to eat, he may write</b> a promissory note. The Gemara elaborates: <b>What is added</b> here by the words: <b>If he has nothing to eat?</b> Are they not meant to add that a promissory note may be written for the sake of <b>the wages</b> of the scribe who draws up the document? <b>Conclude from it</b> that it is permitted to hire a worker who is in need of a livelihood even for work that would otherwise be prohibited on the intermediate days of a Festival.
<b>Rav Sheshet raised an objection</b> from a mishna (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 55a) that addresses the prohibition against performing labor on the eve of Passover: <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> The practitioners of only <b>three crafts</b> are permitted to <b>perform labor until midday on Passover eve,</b> and they are <b>the tailors, the barbers, and the launderers,</b> whose work is needed for the Festival. The <i>baraita</i> elaborates: <b>The tailors</b> may perform labor on Passover eve, <b>as a layperson</b> is permitted to <b>sew in his</b> usual <b>manner during the intermediate days of the Festival.</b> Since similar labor is permitted during the Festival, one may be lenient on Passover eve as well. <b>The barbers and the launderers</b> are permitted to work, <b>as those who arrive from overseas</b> or <b>one who leaves prison</b> are permitted to <b>cut</b> their <b>hair and wash</b> their clothes <b>on the intermediate days of the Festival</b> because they had no opportunity to do so prior to the Festival.
<b>And if it enters your mind</b> that paying <b>wages</b> to a worker <b>who does not have anything to eat is permitted</b> on the intermediate days of the Festival, then <b>all</b> other types of <b>labor should also be permitted</b> on the eve of Passover, <b>as there is</b> something similar permitted on the intermediate days of the Festival, namely, <b>the wages</b> paid to a worker <b>who does not have anything to eat.</b> Tailors are permitted to work on the eve of Passover because similar work is permitted during the intermediate days of the Festival. The payment of wages to a worker who does not have anything to eat on the Festival must be prohibited, as otherwise similar work, i.e., any work involving the payment of wages, would be permitted on the eve of Passover, which is not the case.
<b>Rav Pappa strongly objects to this: If that is so,</b> that one can derive <i>halakhot</i> in this manner, then <b>building</b> as well <b>should be permitted</b> on the eve of Passover, <b>as a wall that tilts toward the public domain</b> and is likely to fall <b>may be demolished and rebuilt in the usual manner</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>due to the danger</b> posed to passersby. Since building under such circumstances is permitted on the intermediate days of a Festival, it should be permitted in general on the eve of Passover.
<b>Ravina</b> also <b>strongly objects to this: If that is so, a scribe [<i>lavlar</i>]</b> who draws up documents <b>should be permitted</b> to do so on the eve of Passover, <b>as one may write betrothal deeds, bills of divorce, and receipts</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival.
<b>Rather, Rav Ashi said</b> that Rav Sheshet’s original objection has no basis: <b>Are you raising a contradiction</b> between the <i>halakhot</i> of the intermediate days of <b>a Festival and</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the fourteenth</b> day of Nisan, i.e., the eve of Passover? They are not comparable, as the prohibition against labor on each is for a different reason: Labor is prohibited on the intermediate days of <b>a Festival because it involves</b> excessive <b>exertion, and in a situation of</b> significant <b>loss the Sages permitted</b> it. In contrast, the prohibition against labor on <b>the fourteenth</b> of Nisan <b>is due to the need</b> to prepare <b>for the Festival,</b> and especially the Paschal lamb. <b>Whatever is needed for the Festival,</b> e.g., the work of tailors and barbers, <b>the Sages permitted,</b> but <b>whatever is not needed for the Festival, the Sages did not permit.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One may not move</b> his possessions <b>from house to house</b> during the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>but he may move</b> them <b>to his courtyard</b> if that is necessary. <b>One may not bring</b> home <b>utensils from the house of a craftsman</b> after he has completed his work, but <b>if one is concerned about them</b> that if he leaves them in the craftsman’s house they are likely to be stolen, <b>he may move them to another courtyard.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t you say in the first clause</b> of the mishna that <b>one may not move</b> one’s possessions <b>at all,</b> even from one house to another? How is it that the mishna then permits moving objects from one courtyard to another in the second clause? <b>Abaye said</b> in explanation: <b>In the latter clause we come to</b> the case of moving objects from <b>the house in his courtyard</b> to that same courtyard.
The mishna states: <b>And one may not bring</b> home <b>utensils from the house of a craftsman. Rav Pappa said: Rava tested us</b> with the following question: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna that <b>one may not bring</b> home <b>utensils from the house of a craftsman. And this raises a contradiction to</b> another mishna (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 55b): <b>One may take utensils to and bring them from the house of a craftsman, even if they are not</b> needed <b>for the sake of the Festival.</b>
<b>And we answered him: Here,</b> the mishna that permits the practice is referring <b>to the fourteenth</b> of Nisan, the eve of Passover, and <b>here,</b> the mishna that prohibits it, is referring <b>to the intermediate days of a Festival.</b> And <b>if you wish, say</b> another explanation: Both <b>this and that</b> refer <b>to the intermediate days of a Festival,</b> but <b>here,</b> the mishna that prohibits the practice is referring to a case <b>where he trusts</b> the craftsman and is not concerned about his utensils being lost or stolen, whereas <b>here,</b> the mishna that permits taking utensils to and from the craftsman is referring to a case <b>where he does not trust</b> the craftsman.

Daf 13b

<b>And</b> so <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may bring</b> home <b>utensils from the house of a craftsman; for example, a jug from the jug-maker's house and a cup from the glass-blower’s house,</b> as they will be used on the Festival itself. <b>But</b> he may <b>not</b> bring home <b>wool from the dyer’s house or utensils from the craftsman’s house,</b> as they are not needed on the Festival. <b>But if</b> the craftsman <b>has nothing to eat, he may give him his wages</b> during the Festival week <b>and leave</b> the utensil <b>with him. And if he does not trust</b> the craftsman to properly safeguard his utensil, <b>he may</b> take the utensil from the craftsman and <b>leave it in a house near him. And if he is concerned about</b> his utensils <b>lest they be stolen, he may move them to another courtyard. And if he does not trust</b> the owner of the other courtyard, <b>he may bring them into his house discreetly.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>You have</b> adequately <b>explained</b> the contradiction between the <i>mishnayot</i> about whether <b>one may bring</b> home utensils from the house of a craftsman. The mishna that permits it can be understood as referring to a case where the craftsman is not to be trusted. But the mishna in <i>Pesaḥim</i> also states that <b>one may take</b> utensils to the house of a craftsman, and this is <b>difficult, as</b> the mishna above <b>teaches: One may not bring</b> home utensils from the house of a craftsman. <b>And</b> this indicates that <b>all the more so, one may not take</b> utensils from his house to that of the craftsman. <b>Rather, it is clear as we answered initially</b> that one mishna is referring to the eve of Passover and the other to the intermediate days of a Festival.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> On the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>figs</b> that were spread out to dry <b>may be covered with straw</b> to protect them from rain and dew. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: One may even condense</b> the figs. <b>Those who sell produce, clothing, and utensils may sell</b> them <b>in private, for the sake of the Festival. Fishermen, and groats makers [<i>dashoshot</i>], and</b> bean <b>pounders,</b> who pulverize the beans, <b>may ply</b> their trades <b>in private for the sake of the Festival. Rabbi Yosei says: They were stringent with themselves</b> to refrain from this work even with respect to what was needed for the Festival.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rabbi Asi disagree about</b> the following issue, <b>and both of them</b> assert their opinions <b>in the name of Ḥizkiyya and Rabbi Yoḥanan. One said: Cover</b> the figs means to <b>lightly</b> cover them with straw, and <b>condense</b> means to spread straw over them <b>densely. And</b> the other <b>one said: Cover</b> means to spread straw over them <b>whether lightly or densely,</b> and <b>condense</b> means to amass the figs into a pile, <b>making it as if</b> it were <b>a pile</b> of grain. <b>This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may condense,</b> i.e., amass the figs into a pile, <b>making it as if</b> it were <b>a pile</b> of grain. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
§ The mishna states: On the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>those who sell produce, clothing, and utensils may sell</b> them <b>in private,</b> for the sake of the Festival.
