The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> this verse <b>saying?</b> What does it mean? <b>Rabbi Abbahu said: This is what</b> the verse <b>is saying: The God of Israel said, the Rock of Israel spoke to me:</b> Although <b>I rule over man, who rules over Me?</b> It is <b>a righteous person.</b> How is it possible to say that a righteous person rules over God, as it were? <b>As I,</b> God, <b>issue a decree</b> and the righteous person <b>nullifies it.</b>
Similarly, the verse states there: <b>“These are the names of David’s warriors; Josheb-Basshebeth</b> a Tahchemonite, chief of the captains; the same was Adino the Eznite; he raised his spear against eight hundred, whom he slew at one time” (II Samuel 23:8). The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> this verse <b>saying? Rabbi Abbahu said: This is what</b> the verse <b>is saying: These are the names of the mighty actions of David.</b> These expressions should not be read as names of people but instead as descriptions of David’s good deeds.
<b>Josheb-Basshebeth [<i>yoshev bashevet</i>]</b> indicates that <b>when</b> David <b>would sit [<i>yoshev</i>] in the study hall, he would not sit upon pillows and cushions,</b> as an important person ordinarily would. <b>Rather,</b> he would sit <b>on the ground</b> like one of the students. <b>For as long as</b> David’s <b>teacher, Ira the Jairite, was alive,</b> Ira <b>would teach the Sages</b> while sitting <b>on pillows and cushions. When</b> Ira <b>passed away, David would teach the Sages, and he</b> did this while <b>sitting on the ground. They said to him: Master,</b> you <b>should sit upon pillows and blankets. He did not accept</b> their suggestions, since in his humility he did not wish to appear as the teacher of the Jewish people.
In this verse, David is described as <b>“a Tahchemonite [<i>taḥkemoni</i>].” Rav said: The Holy One, Blessed be He, said to him: Since you have humbled yourself, be you</b> now <b>like Me [<i>tehe kamoni</i>].</b> How so? <b>As I issue a decree, and you,</b> owing to your righteousness, <b>may nullify it.</b>
David is also described here as <b>“chief of the captains [<i>rosh hashalishim</i>]”</b> because God said to him: <b>You will be the head [<i>rosh</i>] of the three [<i>sheloshet</i>] Patriarchs. “The same was Adino the Eznite”;</b> this alludes to the fact that <b>when</b> David <b>would sit and occupy himself with Torah, he would make himself soft [<i>me’aden</i>] as a worm, and when he would go out to war, he would make himself hard</b> and strong <b>as a tree [<i>etz</i>].</b>
The expression: <b>“Against eight hundred people, which he slew at one time,”</b> means <b>that he would throw an arrow</b> in the air <b>and</b> with it <b>kill eight hundred people at one time. And</b> David <b>would sigh over the two hundred</b> who were missing from fulfillment of the Torah’s promise, <b>as it is written: “How should one man chase a thousand”</b> (Deuteronomy 32:30).
<b>A Divine Voice issued forth and said</b> by way of explanation as to why the promise was not entirely fulfilled: “Because David did that which was right in the eyes of the Lord, and turned not aside from anything that He commanded him all the days of his life, <b>save only the matter of Uriah the Hittite”</b> (I Kings 15:5). Had David not committed this sin, then all of the promises mentioned in the Torah would have been fulfilled in their entirety through him.
The Gemara returns to the <i>halakhot</i> of ostracism and mentions that <b>Rabbi Tanḥum, son of Rabbi Ḥiyya, of the village of Akko, said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa said</b> that <b>Rabbi Simlai said, and</b> some <b>say</b> that this tradition was transmitted in the following manner: <b>Rabbi Tanḥum said</b> that <b>Rav Huna said, and</b> others <b>say</b> that <b>Rav Huna himself</b> made this statement without the chain of transmission:

Daf 17a

If <b>a student ostracized</b> someone else <b>due to</b> an insult to <b>his dignity,</b> and not because the ostracized person was guilty of some transgression, <b>his</b> decree of <b>ostracism is valid, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who is ostracized by the teacher is</b> considered <b>ostracized with regard to the student.</b> However, <b>one who is ostracized by the student is not</b> considered <b>ostracized with regard to the teacher.</b> The Gemara attempts to draw an inference from a careful reading of this <i>baraita</i>: <b>He is not</b> considered <b>ostracized with regard to the teacher,</b> which implies <b>that he is</b> considered <b>ostracized with regard to everyone</b> else.
The Gemara clarifies the case: <b>For what</b> reason was he ostracized? <b>If</b> it was <b>for a matter relating to Heaven,</b> i.e., because he sinned, then why, if he was ostracized by the student, should he not be considered ostracized with regard to the teacher? Doesn’t the verse state: <b>“There is no wisdom or understanding or counsel against the Lord”</b> (Proverbs 21:30)? This means that when a sin is committed and God’s name is desecrated, all other considerations are ignored, even the eminence and knowledge of the teacher, and therefore he too must treat the offender as ostracized. <b>Rather, is it not</b> that the Gemara is referring to a case where the student ostracized the other person due to an insult <b>to his</b> own <b>dignity?</b> Therefore, it is apparent that his decree of ostracism is valid and binding upon all, with the exception of his teacher.
<b>Rav Yosef said: A Torah scholar may execute judgment for himself with regard to a matter about which he is certain,</b> and he is not required to first go to court and have the case decided for him. The same applies when another person behaves in a disrespectful manner toward him; he is permitted to go ahead on his own and ostracize him.
<b>There was a certain Torah scholar who gained a bad reputation</b> due to rumors about his conduct. <b>Rav Yehuda said: What should be done? To excommunicate him</b> is not an option. <b>The Sages need him,</b> as he is a great Torah authority. <b>Not to excommunicate him</b> is also not an option, as then <b>the name of Heaven would be desecrated.</b>
Rav Yehuda <b>said to Rabba bar bar Ḥana:</b> Have <b>you heard anything with regard to this</b> issue? <b>He said to him: Rabbi Yoḥanan said as follows: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written: “For the priest’s lips should keep knowledge, and they should seek Torah at his mouth; for he is a messenger [<i>malakh</i>] of the Lord of hosts”</b> (Malachi 2:7)? This verse teaches: <b>If the teacher is similar to an angel [<i>malakh</i>] of the Lord,</b> then <b>seek Torah from his mouth,</b> but if he is <b>not</b> pure and upright, then <b>do not seek Torah from his mouth;</b> even if he is knowledgeable about Torah, do not learn from him.
Based on this statement, <b>Rav Yehuda ostracized</b> that Torah scholar. <b>In the end,</b> after some time had passed, <b>Rav Yehuda took ill</b> and was on the verge of death. <b>The Sages came to inquire about his well-being, and</b> the ostracized scholar <b>came</b> along <b>with them as well. When Rav Yehuda saw him,</b> that scholar, <b>he laughed.</b>
The ostracized scholar <b>said to him: Was it not enough that you excommunicated that man,</b> i.e., me, <b>but</b> now <b>you even laugh at me?</b> Rav Yehuda <b>said to him: I was not laughing at you; rather, I am happy as I go to that</b> other <b>world that I did not flatter even</b> a great <b>man like you,</b> but instead I treated you fairly in accordance with the <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Rav Yehuda died.</b> The ostracized scholar <b>came to the study hall and said</b> to the Sages: <b>Release me</b> from the decree of ostracism. <b>The Sages said to him: There is no man here as eminent as Rav Yehuda who can release you</b> from the ostracism. <b>Rather, go to Rabbi Yehuda Nesia</b> in Eretz Yisrael, <b>as</b> only <b>he can release you.</b> That scholar <b>came before</b> Rabbi Yehuda Nesia. Rabbi Yehuda Nesia <b>said to Rabbi Ami: Go and examine his case. If it is necessary to release him</b> from his decree of ostracism, <b>release him</b> on my behalf.
<b>Rabbi Ami examined his case</b> and <b>thought</b> at first <b>to release him</b> from his ostracism. But <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani rose up on his feet and said: If the maidservant in the house of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi once ostracized another person, and <b>the Sages did not relate frivolously to her</b> decree of <b>ostracism</b> and did not revoke it until <b>three years</b> had passed, <b>all the more so, with regard to</b> a decree of ostracism placed by <b>Yehuda our colleague,</b> we must take it seriously and not release this scholar.
<b>Rabbi Zeira said: What</b> caused <b>this Elder,</b> Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani, <b>to come before us in the study hall</b> today <b>though for many years he did not come,</b> and now he comes precisely during this discussion. <b>Learn from this</b> that <b>it is not necessary to release him</b> from his decree of ostracism, as this combination of events is certainly not a coincidence. Rather, it should be viewed as an instructive sign from Heaven. Consequently, Rabbi Ami <b>did not release him</b> from the ostracism, and the ostracized scholar <b>left in tears.</b>
<b>A wasp came and stung</b> the ostracized scholar <b>on his penis and he died.</b> Because he was a great Torah scholar, <b>they took him into the caves in which the pious</b> are interred in order to bury him there, <b>but</b> the caves <b>did not accept him.</b> A snake stood at the entrance of the caves and did not let them pass. <b>They</b> then <b>took him into the caves of the judges, and they accepted him.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> that he was accepted there? The Gemara answers: Even though he sinned, <b>he</b> still <b>acted in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ilai, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Ilai says: If a person sees that his</b> evil <b>inclination is gaining control over him</b> and he cannot overcome it, then <b>he should go to a place where he is not known. He should wear black, and he should wrap his</b> head <b>in black,</b> as if he were a mourner. Perhaps these changes will influence him, so that he not sin. Even if these actions do not help, <b>he should</b> at least <b>do as his heart desires</b> in private <b>and not desecrate the name of Heaven in public.</b> Although this person had sinned, he did so in private and in a manner that did not publicly desecrate God’s name, and therefore it was fitting that he be given an honorable burial.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the story mentioned by Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥmani involving <b>the maidservant in the house of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? It was related <b>that the maidservant in Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi’s <b>house saw a certain man who was striking his adult son. She said: Let that man be excommunicated, due to</b> the fact that <b>he has transgressed</b> the injunction: <b>“You shall not place a stumbling block before the blind”</b> (Leviticus 19:14), <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse states: <b>“You shall not place a stumbling block before the blind,”</b> and <b>the verse speaks</b> here <b>of one who strikes his adult son,</b> as the son is likely to become angry and strike his father back, thereby transgressing the severe prohibition against hitting one’s parent.
Similarly, it was related that <b>Reish Lakish was guarding an orchard</b> for payment when <b>a certain man came and ate</b> some <b>figs</b> that were growing there. Reish Lakish <b>raised his voice</b> and yelled at him, <b>but</b> this man <b>paid no attention to him</b> and kept eating. Reish Lakish <b>said: Let that man be</b> in a state of <b>excommunication.</b> The man eating the figs <b>said to him: On the contrary, let that man,</b> i.e., Reish Lakish, <b>be</b> in a state of <b>excommunication,</b> for even <b>if I have become liable to you for payment,</b> as I have eaten of the figs without permission, <b>have I become liable to you for excommunication?</b> With that statement, the man left.
Reish Lakish <b>went to the study hall</b> to inquire about the <i>halakha</i> with regard to this man. The other Sages <b>said to him: His</b> decree of <b>ostracism is</b> valid, but <b>your</b> decree of <b>ostracism is not.</b> In other words, that man was correct and Reish Lakish should not have ostracized him in response to his actions.
Reish Lakish then asked: If so, <b>what is the remedy</b> for this decree of ostracism? The Sages answered him: <b>Go to him so that he may release you</b> from it. Reish Lakish replied: <b>I do not know him. They said to him: Go</b> then <b>to the <i>Nasi</i>, so that he may release you</b> from the ban, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one <b>was ostracized, but he does not know who ostracized him, he should go to the <i>Nasi</i>, and</b> the <i>Nasi</i> <b>may release</b> him from <b>his</b> decree of <b>ostracism.</b>
The Gemara continues: <b>Rav Huna said</b> that <b>in Usha it was enacted:</b> If <b>the president of the court sinned, he is not ostracized.</b> Although this would be the appropriate punishment, he is not ostracized, so as not to cause a desecration of God’s name. <b>Rather,</b> they <b>say to him</b> the words of the verse: <b>“Keep your honor and stay at home”</b> (II Kings 14:10). That is to say, to preserve your dignity, it would be best if you were to stay at home, resign your position, and refrain from further public appearances. If <b>he sins again, he is ostracized, due to the desecration of God’s name</b> that would ensue were people to think that he was spared his rightful punishment due to his high position.
The Gemara comments: This opinion <b>disagrees with</b> that of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> for <b>Reish Lakish said:</b> If <b>a Torah scholar sins, he is not ostracized</b> at all <b>in public, as it is stated: “Therefore, shall you fall in the day, and the prophet also shall fall with you in the night”</b> (Hosea 4:5). This is explained to mean: If a prophet or any other important person sins, his offense <b>should be concealed like the night</b> and not punished in public.
With regard to this issue, it was related that <b>when a Torah scholar would become liable</b> to be punished with <b>excommunication before Mar Zutra the Pious,</b> Mar Zutra <b>would first excommunicate himself</b> as a mark of respect for the Torah scholar, <b>and afterward he would ostracize</b> the Torah scholar. This self-imposed excommunication was meant only as a show of honor to the other Torah scholar, and therefore <b>when</b> Mar Zutra <b>would enter his house</b> of lodging, <b>he would release his</b> own excommunication, <b>and afterward he would release</b> the other’s excommunication.
<b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said: A Torah scholar may ostracize himself, and he may</b> similarly <b>release himself</b> from self-imposed ostracism. <b>Rav Pappa said: I have</b> good <b>coming to me,</b> for I am praiseworthy, <b>as I have never ostracized a Torah scholar.</b> The Gemara asks about this: <b>If so, when a Torah scholar was liable to be ostracized, what would he do?</b> The Gemara answers: He did <b>as they do in the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, where <b>they appoint</b> a court <b>to give a Torah scholar lashes, but they do not appoint</b> a court <b>to ostracize him.</b> That is to say, lashes were preferred over ostracism.
The Gemara proceeds with a discussion that explains the severity of the punishment of excommunication: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the word <b>excommunication [<i>shamta</i>]? Rav said:</b> This word is a contraction of the expression <b>there is death [<i>sham mita</i>],</b> alluding to the deathly aspect of excommunication. <b>And Shmuel said:</b> <i>Shamta</i> means that <b>he will be a desolation [<i>shemama yiheyeh</i>], and it is effective upon him like fat</b> smeared <b>on an oven.</b> Just as some of the fat will always remain absorbed in the walls of the oven, so too some aspect of the curse contained in the excommunication will continue to adhere to him even after the excommunication has been nullified.
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> this opinion <b>disagrees with</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> for <b>Reish Lakish said: Just as</b> ostracism <b>enters the two hundred and forty-eight organs</b> of one’s body when it is first pronounced, <b>so too when it leaves, it leaves from</b> his <b>two hundred and forty-eight organs.</b>
The following allusion is offered in support of the opinion of Reish Lakish: <b>When it enters,</b> it enters all the organs, <b>as it is written: “And the city shall be a curse [<i>ḥerem</i>]”</b> (Joshua 6:17), <b>and the numerical value [<i>gimatriyya</i>]</b> of the word <b><i>ḥerem</i>,</b> a concept similar to ostracism, <b>is two hundred and forty-eight.</b> Therefore, the verse alludes to the fact that a decree of ostracism penetrates one’s two hundred and forty-eight organs. <b>When it leaves,</b> it leaves all the organs, <b>as it is written: “In wrath remember mercy [<i>raḥem</i>]”</b> (Habakkuk 3:2), <b>and the numerical value</b> of the word <b><i>raḥem</i> is also</b> two hundred and forty-eight, thereby teaching that when the decree of ostracism is revoked, it disappears entirely.
The Gemara continues discussing the power of a ban. <b>Rav Yosef said: Cast an excommunication on the tail of a dog and it,</b> the excommunication, <b>will do its work</b> and harm the dog. It was related that there was <b>a certain dog that would eat the shoes of the Sages, and they did not know who it was</b> causing this damage. They thought that it was a person, <b>and</b> so <b>they excommunicated</b> whoever was doing it. Soon thereafter, the dog’s <b>tail caught fire and got burnt.</b> This shows that excommunication can have a harmful effect even on a dog.
It was further related that there was <b>a violent person who</b> caused <b>suffering to a certain Torah scholar.</b> This Torah scholar <b>came before Rav Yosef</b> to ask what he should do. Rav Yosef <b>said to him: Go and ostracize him.</b> This Torah scholar <b>said to him: I am afraid of him,</b> that he will harass me even more.
Rav Yosef <b>said to him: Take</b> out, i.e., publish <b>a written</b> ostracism <b>against him.</b> The Torah scholar said to him: <b>All the more so I am fearful of him,</b> for if I publicize the matter he will certainly come after me. Rav Yosef <b>said to him</b> to do as follows: <b>Take</b> the written ostracism <b>and place it in a jug,</b>

Daf 17b

<b>and set it down in a cemetery,</b> where nobody is found, <b>and sound a thousand,</b> i.e., many, <b><i>shofar</i> blasts over the course of forty days.</b> That man <b>went and did this. The jug burst and the violent man died.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the reason that <b><i>shofarot</i></b> are sounded when a decree of ostracism is pronounced? The <i>shofarot</i> allude to the fact <b>that they extract punishment [<i>shenifra’in</i>] from</b> the excommunicated person.
The Gemara asks further: <b>What</b> is the reason that <b>broken</b> blasts are sounded on the <i>shofar</i> when the excommunication is pronounced? <b>Rav Yitzḥak, son of Rav Yehuda, said: It breaks tall buildings,</b> i.e., a decree of ostracism can harm and break even the high and mighty, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel said: Wherever</b> it says that <b>the Sages set their eyes</b> in anger upon a particular person, it causes <b>either death or poverty.</b>
§ It is taught in the mishna: <b>And the nazirite</b> whose term of naziriteship ended on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>and the leper</b> who needs to purify himself on the intermediate days and must shave his entire body in order to leave <b>his</b> state of ritual <b>impurity</b> and regain <b>his</b> ritual <b>purity,</b> these people are permitted to cut their hair on the intermediate days of the Festival because they were not able to do so on the eve of the Festival. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised a question before Rabbi Zeira:</b> Is this allowance limited to the case <b>where they did not have the time</b> to cut their hair before the Festival, as it was prohibited for them to do so beforehand? <b>Or perhaps</b> they may cut their hair <b>even</b> in a case <b>where they had the time</b> to do so beforehand.
<b>He said to him: We</b> already <b>learned</b> this in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>all those</b> about whom <b>the</b> Sages <b>said</b> that they <b>are permitted to cut their hair on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival,</b> they may do so only <b>if they did not have time</b> to cut their hair before the Festival. <b>But</b> if <b>they had time</b> before the Festival began, then <b>they are prohibited</b> from doing so.
However, <b>a nazirite and a leper, even if they had the time</b> to do so before the Festival, <b>they are permitted</b> to cut their hair. Why are they granted this special allowance? So <b>that they will not delay</b> bringing <b>their offerings.</b> Both a nazirite and a leper must cut their hair before sacrificing their offerings in completion of their purification process. Therefore, if they are not permitted to cut their hair, they will not be able to sacrifice their offerings at the proper time.
In was <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A priest and a mourner are permitted to cut their hair</b> during the intermediate days of a Festival. The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> when the <b>mourner</b> is permitted to do so? <b>If we say that the eighth</b> day of <b>his</b> mourning <b>occurred on the eve of the Festival,</b> then <b>he should have cut his hair on the eve of the Festival,</b> as the more stringent restrictions of his mourning no longer applied. <b>Rather,</b> it must be that we are dealing with a case <b>where the eighth</b> day of <b>his</b> mourning <b>occurred on a Shabbat that was the eve of the Festival,</b> and so he could not have cut his hair on the Festival eve.
But if this is the case, <b>he should have cut his hair on Friday, for Rav Ḥisda said</b> that <b>Ravina bar Sheila said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul.</b> If the deceased was buried seven days before the Festival, then not only has the mourner completed the seven-day mourning period, but he is even regarded as having begun the thirty-day mourning period, and so the Festival cancels this thirty-day period of mourning. <b>And the Rabbis concede</b> to the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul when the eighth</b> day of <b>his</b> mourning <b>occurs on a Shabbat that is the eve of the Festival,</b> and they maintain <b>that he is permitted to cut his hair on Friday.</b> Since he is unavoidably prevented from cutting his hair on the eighth day, the Rabbis allowed him to cut it already on the seventh day.
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> the ruling of the <i>baraita</i> is <b>necessary</b> in the case <b>where the seventh</b> day of <b>his</b> mourning <b>occurs on a Shabbat that is the eve of the Festival.</b> In that case, he certainly cannot cut his hair on Friday because it is only the sixth day of his mourning, and so he is permitted to cut his hair on the intermediate days of the Festival.
The Gemara comments: <b>The <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i>,</b> who permits a mourner to cut his hair during the intermediate days of the Festival, <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul, who said:</b> The legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that of <b>an entire</b> day, <b>and</b> therefore <b>the seventh</b> day <b>counts as both</b> the final day of the seven-day mourning period and the first day of the thirty-day mourning period. From the perspective of the <i>halakhot</i> of mourning, one would be permitted to cut his hair; however <b>since</b> this day <b>is Shabbat, he is unavoidably prevented</b> from doing so, and therefore he is permitted to cut his hair during the intermediate days of the Festival.
<b>The <i>tanna</i></b> of <b>our</b> mishna, on the other hand, who does not mention that a mourner is permitted to cut his hair on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of the <b>Rabbis, who say: We do not say</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that of <b>an entire</b> day for a mourner. Therefore, <b>he has not yet completed</b> his <b>seven-</b>day period of <b>mourning,</b> and consequently he will not be permitted to cut his hair even after the Festival, until the completion of the thirty-day mourning period.
The Gemara continues and asks: The case of <b>this priest,</b> who is permitted to cut his hair during the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>what are the circumstances? If we say that he completed his watch on the eve of the Festival,</b> then <b>he should have cut his hair on the eve of the Festival.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> the ruling of the <i>baraita</i> is <b>necessary</b> in the case <b>where one completed his watch during the Festival</b> week itself. <b>The <i>tanna</i></b> of <b>our</b> mishna <b>holds</b> that <b>since we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Sukka</i> 55b): <b>At the year,</b> i.e., the three pilgrimage Festivals, <b>all of the priestly watches share equally in the Festival offerings and in the division of the shewbread</b> among the priests on Shabbat that occurs on the Festival, therefore, <b>he is</b> considered <b>like one whose watch was not completed during the Festival,</b> and he may not cut his hair until after the Festival. <b>And the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> holds that although</b> he <b>belongs</b> also <b>to those</b> other <b>watches</b> serving during the Festival, <b>nevertheless, his own watch was completed</b> before the Festival, and so he is permitted to cut his hair.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>All those about whom it was said</b> that they <b>are permitted to cut their hair on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival</b> because they were unable to do so beforehand, <b>they may</b> similarly <b>cut their hair during the period of their mourning</b> if they had been unable to do so beforehand.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: <b>They are</b> nevertheless <b>prohibited</b> from cutting their hair during the period of their mourning? <b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that <b>Rav Sheila said: When it is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> that <b>they are permitted</b> to cut their hair, it is referring to one <b>for whom</b> two periods of <b>mourning applied in succession.</b> In other words, this person was required to practice one period of mourning immediately following another period of mourning, and he is unable to endure the prescribed restrictions for such an extended period.
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> this is referring to a case <b>where his mourning applied in succession,</b> then <b>why specifically</b> does the <i>baraita</i> discuss <b>all those about whom it was said</b> that they may cut their hair? The allowance should apply <b>even to everyone,</b> not only to those who had been unavoidably prevented from cutting their hair in the preceding period, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one’s mourning</b> periods <b>applied in succession, one after the other,</b> and <b>his hair</b> grew long and <b>became heavy, he may lighten</b> it by cutting his hair <b>with a razor, and he may wash his garment in water,</b> so that he does not suffer for such a long time without cutting his hair or washing his clothes.
The Gemara answers: <b>But wasn’t it said with regard to that</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rav Ḥisda said:</b> When it states that one may cut his hair, it means that he may do so <b>with a razor, but not with scissors?</b> Similarly, when it states that one may wash his clothes, it means that he may launder them <b>with water, but not with <i>neter</i> or soap.</b> Therefore, the <i>baraita</i> teaches that if one had been unavoidably prevented from performing these actions beforehand and then had to observe a double mourning period, he may cut his hair in an ordinary manner, without performing these actions in an altered way. <b>Rav Ḥisda said: That is to say</b> that, generally speaking, <b>a mourner is prohibited from laundering</b> his clothes.
<b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>Just as</b> the Sages <b>said</b> that <b>it is prohibited to cut one’s hair during the</b> intermediate days of <b>a Festival, so too it is prohibited to cut one’s nails during the</b> intermediate days of <b>a Festival;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda, whereas Rabbi Yosei permits</b> cutting nails.
<b>And just as</b> the Sages <b>said</b> that <b>a mourner is prohibited from cutting his hair during the period of his mourning, so too he is prohibited from cutting his nails during the period of his mourning;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda, whereas Rabbi Yosei permits</b> a mourner to cut his nails.
<b>Ulla said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda with regard to mourning, but the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei with regard to the</b> intermediate days of the <b>Festival. Shmuel said:</b>

Daf 18a

<b>The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei with regard to</b> the intermediate days of <b>the Festival and with regard to mourning, as Shmuel said</b> this general principle: <b>The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with the statement of the</b> more <b>lenient</b> authority <b>in</b> matters relating to <b>mourning.</b>
It was related that <b>something</b> unpleasant <b>happened to Pineḥas, brother of Mar Shmuel,</b> that is to say, one of his close relatives died. <b>Shmuel entered to ask him the reason,</b> i.e., to console him. <b>He saw that</b> Pineḥas’s <b>nails were long,</b> and <b>said to him: Why do you not cut them?</b> Pineḥas <b>replied: If it were your</b> relative who died, and you were in mourning, <b>would you treat</b> the matter <b>so lightly</b> and cut your nails?
Pineḥas’s words <b>were: “Like an error that proceeds from a ruler”</b> (Ecclesiastes 10:5). As soon as he uttered them they come true, even though he did not intend them. Shortly after Pineḥas made his comment, <b>something</b> unpleasant <b>happened to Shmuel,</b> and one of his close relatives died. <b>Pineḥas, his brother, entered to ask him the reason,</b> i.e., to offer words of comfort. Shmuel <b>took his nails and cast them in</b> Pineḥas’s face. Shmuel then <b>said to him:</b> Do <b>you not</b> know the principle that <b>a covenant is made with the lips?</b> In other words, do you not know that what one says influences future events?
This is <b>as Rabbi Yoḥanan said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that a covenant is made with the lips,</b> and that one’s speech has the power to change events? <b>For it is stated: “And Abraham said to his young men: Stay here with the donkey, and I and the lad will go onward; and we will worship, and we will come back to you”</b> (Genesis 22:5). Abraham said this even though he thought that he was going to sacrifice his son as an offering and that Isaac would not be returning, <b>yet this had an influence and they both came back.</b>
With regard to this <i>halakha</i> pertaining to a mourner cutting his nails: They initially <b>concluded from this:</b> With regard to the nails on his <b>hand, yes,</b> a mourner may cut them; but as for the nails on his <b>foot, no,</b> he may not cut them, because long toenails are less repulsive. <b>Rav Anan bar Taḥlifa said: It was explained to me by Shmuel</b> himself: <b>It is not different</b> if it is the nails on the <b>hand and it is not different</b> if it is the nails on the <b>foot,</b> as in both cases cutting the nails is permitted.
<b>Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi said</b> that <b>Rav said: But with scissors [<i>genustera</i>]</b> specifically for nail cutting <b>it is prohibited,</b> i.e., the mourner should cut his nails in an alternate manner. <b>Rav Shemen bar Abba said: I</b> once <b>stood before Rabbi Yoḥanan in the study hall during the intermediate days of a Festival, and he cut his nails with his teeth and threw them</b> down.
The Gemara comments: <b>Learn from this</b> incident of Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>three</b> <i>halakhot</i>: <b>Learn from this</b> that <b>it is permitted to cut one’s nails on the intermediate days of a Festival. And learn from this</b> that nails <b>have no</b> prohibition <b>due to</b> the fact that they are <b>repulsive,</b> i.e., there is no prohibition against biting them on that basis. Inasmuch as one is prohibited from placing something repulsive in his mouth, this incident teaches that nails do not fall into this category. <b>And</b> also <b>learn from this</b> that <b>it is permitted</b> to throw <b>nails</b> away.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Three things were said about nails: One who buries them</b> in the ground <b>is</b> deemed <b>righteous. One who burns them</b> is even better, as he <b>is</b> considered <b>pious. One who</b> merely <b>throws them</b> away <b>is</b> regarded as <b>wicked.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> that it is prohibited to throw away nail clippings? This is prohibited <b>lest a pregnant women pass over them and miscarry,</b> for the Sages had a tradition that it is dangerous for a pregnant woman to walk over fingernails.
The Gemara answers: <b>A woman is not usually</b> found <b>in the study hall,</b> and therefore Rabbi Yoḥanan was not concerned about throwing his nail clippings there. <b>If you say</b> that <b>sometimes</b> the nails <b>are gathered</b> together when the floor is swept <b>and</b> then <b>thrown outside</b> where a pregnant woman may walk over them, this is not a problem. <b>Once</b> their place <b>has changed</b> the nails themselves <b>change</b> and are no longer harmful.
<b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: A pair</b> of Sages from <b>Ḥamatan came before Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>And Mar Zutra taught</b> it without the names of Rav Yehuda and Rav, simply as: <b>A pair</b> of Sages from <b>Ḥamatan came before Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>And they asked him</b> whether or not a mourner is permitted to cut his <b>nails, and he permitted</b> it <b>to them. And had they asked him</b> whether or not a mourner may trim his <b>mustache, he would</b> also <b>have permitted</b> it <b>to them. And Shmuel said: They also asked him</b> about trimming <b>a mustache,</b> and <b>he permitted</b> it <b>to them.</b>
<b>Avitul the scribe said in the name of Rav Pappa: A mustache</b> may be trimmed <b>from</b> one <b>corner to</b> the other <b>corner</b> of the mouth. <b>Rabbi Ami said:</b> One may trim only the portion of the <b>mustache that interferes</b> with normal eating, but one may not trim the mustache for beautification. <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: For me,</b> my entire mustache <b>is</b> considered <b>like a mustache that interferes</b> with normal eating, as I am particularly sensitive, and so I may trim my entire mustache.
§ Having mentioned Avitul the scribe, the Gemara records other statements of his: <b>And Avitul the scribe said in the name of Rav Pappa:</b> The <b>Pharaoh who lived in the days of Moses was a cubit</b> tall, his <b>beard was a cubit</b> long, <b>and his penis</b> [<b><i>parmashtako</i></b>] was <b>a cubit and a span,</b> i.e., a cubit and the distance between the thumb and the little finger, in length, <b>in order to fulfill what is stated: “And He sets up over it the lowest of men”</b> (Daniel 4:14), which teaches that Pharaoh was extremely short and lowly.
<b>Avitul the scribe</b> also <b>said in the name of Rav Pappa:</b> The <b>Pharaoh who lived in the days of Moses was a sorcerer</b> [<b><i>amgushi</i></b>], <b>as it is stated: “Behold, he goes out to the water”</b> (Exodus 7:15). Pharaoh would regularly go out to the water in order to engage in witchcraft.
§ The mishna taught: <b>And these</b> people <b>may launder</b> their clothes <b>on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival: One who comes from a country overseas. Rav Asi said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Anyone who has only one shirt is permitted to launder it on the intermediate days of a Festival.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised an objection</b> from what was taught in the mishna: <b>And these</b> people <b>may launder</b> their clothes <b>on the</b> intermediate days of <b>a Festival: One who comes from a country overseas,</b> and one who is released from a house of captivity, and one who comes out of prison, and one who had been ostracized and the Rabbis released him from his decree of ostracism, <b>etc.</b> It may be inferred: <b>Those</b> who are mentioned in the mishna, <b>yes,</b> they may launder their clothes during the intermediate days of the Festival, but <b>one who has only one shirt</b> may <b>not</b> launder it.
<b>Rabbi Ya’akov said to Rabbi Yirmeya: I will explain it to you. The mishna</b> is referring to those cases where one is permitted to launder his clothes even if <b>he has two</b> changes of garments <b>and</b> they are <b>dirty.</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan speaks about one who has only one garment, and he rules that he may launder it in all circumstances.
<b>Rav Yitzḥak bar Ya’akov bar Giyorei sent</b> a message <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> With regard to <b>linen garments, it is permitted to launder them during the intermediate days of the Festival</b> because they are easily soiled. <b>Rava raised an objection</b> from what is taught in the mishna: <b>Hand towels, the towels</b>

Daf 18b

<b>of barbers</b> that are used to cover a person having a haircut, and body-drying towels may all be laundered on the intermediate days of a Festival. This statement implies that <b>these</b> garments, <b>yes,</b> they may be laundered, but other types of <b>linen garments, no,</b> they may not be laundered.
<b>Abaye said to him:</b> This is not a contradiction: <b>The mishna</b> is referring to garments made <b>even of other types</b> of materials; linen garments, however, may be laundered even when they serve other purposes. <b>Bar Hedya said: I myself saw the Sea of Tiberias,</b> the Sea of Galilee, <b>to which basins</b> full <b>of linen garments were brought out</b> to be laundered <b>during the intermediate days of a Festival.</b>
<b>Abaye strongly objects to this:</b> This report cannot be adduced as proof for the <i>halakha</i>, for <b>who says to us</b> that <b>they did</b> this <b>in accordance with the will of the Sages? Perhaps they did</b> it <b>without the will of the Sages.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>And these</b> are the documents that <b>may be written on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival:</b> Documents of <b>betrothal of wives,</b> through which bridegrooms betroth their brides; <b>bills of divorce; receipts</b> for the repayment of debts; <b>wills [<i>deyateiki</i>];</b> deeds of <b>gift; <i>perozbolin</i>,</b> documents through which lenders authorize the courts to collect their loans on their behalf, thereby preventing the Sabbatical year from canceling their debts; <b>letters of valuation,</b> which were drawn up by the court when they valuated property and transferred it to the lender; and <b>letters of sustenance,</b> which were drawn up when one accepted upon himself to maintain another, e.g., his step-daughter.
The list continues: <b>Documents of the ritual through which the brother-in-law frees the <i>yevama</i> of her levirate bonds [<i>ḥalitza</i>],</b> thereby freeing her from the obligation to marry one of her deceased husband’s brothers; documents in which the court records the <b>refusal</b> of a girl upon reaching majority to remain married to the man to whom her mother or brothers married her as a minor after the death of her father; <b>documents of arbitration,</b> in which the court summarizes a conflict that had been resolved through arbitration; <b>court rulings; and</b> the official <b>correspondence of the</b> ruling <b>authorities.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Shmuel said: It is permitted to betroth</b> a woman <b>on the intermediate days of a Festival, lest another</b> come and betroth her <b>first.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Let us say that</b> the mishna <b>supports</b> Shmuel, who said: <b>And these</b> are the documents that <b>may be drawn up on the</b> intermediate days of <b>a Festival: Documents of betrothal.</b>
<b>What, is</b> the mishna <b>not</b> referring to <b>actual documents of betrothal,</b> through which one would actually betroth a woman? The Gemara rejects this: <b>No,</b> the mishna is referring to <b>documents of stipulation</b> recording the amounts that the parents agree to pay as the dowry of their respective son or daughter, <b>in accordance with</b> what <b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said.</b>
<b>For Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> When two families negotiate the terms of marriage for their respective children, and one side says to the other: <b>How much do you give to your son</b> as a dowry? And the second side says: I give <b>such and such</b> amount; <b>how much do you give to your daughter?</b> And the first side responds: <b>Such and such</b> amount, then, once the bride and groom <b>arose and</b> pronounced <b>the betrothal</b> formula, then all of these obligations are <b>acquired</b> and therefore binding. <b>These are</b> among <b>the things that are acquired through words</b> alone. In other words, there is no need to perform an additional act of acquisition in order to confirm the agreement, and the mishna is referring to a document recording such an agreement. Although such a document may be drawn up even on the intermediate days of a Festival, this does not mean that one may actually betroth a woman during this period.
The Gemara asks: <b>Let us say</b> that the following mishna <b>supports</b> Shmuel: <b>One may not marry a woman on the</b> intermediate days of <b>a Festival, neither a virgin nor a widow; nor may one</b> then <b>perform levirate marriage</b> with his sister-in-law, if his brother died childless, <b>because that</b> would be <b>a joyous</b> occasion <b>for him.</b> This statement implies that it is only marrying that is prohibited, <b>but betrothing is permitted.</b>
The Gemara rejects this argument: This is not the correct way to understand the mishna, as <b>it is speaking in the style of: Needless to say.</b> It is <b>needless to say</b> that <b>betrothal</b> is not permitted, <b>because</b> the groom <b>does not perform a mitzva</b> through betrothal. <b>Rather,</b> the same is true <b>even of marriage, through which one performs a mitzva,</b> as marriage is preparation for fulfilling the mitzva of procreation. It is still <b>not permitted.</b>
The Gemara offers another support for Shmuel: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear that which</b> a Sage of <b>the school of Shmuel taught</b> in the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may betroth</b> a woman on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>but he may not marry</b> her, <b>nor may he make a betrothal feast, nor may he perform levirate marriage, because that</b> would be <b>a joyous</b> occasion <b>for him,</b> and one may not mix the joy of a wedding with the joy of the Festival. The Gemara concludes: <b>Learn from this</b> a support for Shmuel’s opinion.
The Gemara raises a question about the ruling itself: <b>And did Shmuel</b> actually <b>say</b> that we are concerned that <b>perhaps another</b> man will come and betroth the woman <b>first? But didn’t Rav Yehuda say that Shmuel said: Every day a Divine Voice issues forth and says: The daughter of so-and-so</b> is destined to be the wife of <b>so-and-so; the field of so-and-so</b> will belong <b>to so-and-so?</b> If this is the case, why should one be concerned lest another betroth her first? It is predestined that he will marry his designated mate.
<b>Rather,</b> Shmuel’s statement should be understood as follows: <b>Perhaps another</b> man will come and betroth her <b>first by means of</b> praying for divine <b>mercy.</b> In other words, Shmuel is concerned that the rival may beseech God to cancel the decree of the Divine Voice, and therefore the first man needs to hurry and betroth the woman before the other one has a chance to pray that he should take her from him.
This is <b>like this</b> incident, in <b>which Rava heard a certain man asking for mercy,</b> i.e., praying, <b>who said: Grant me so-and-so</b> as a wife. Rava <b>said to him: Do not</b> pray and <b>ask for mercy</b> in <b>this</b> way. <b>If</b> she <b>is fit for you,</b> and it has been decreed that she will be your wife, <b>she will not go away from you. And if</b> she is <b>not</b> destined to be your wife, <b>you will</b> come to <b>deny the Lord</b> when you see that your prayer is not answered. <b>After</b> the man married this woman, Rava <b>heard him say</b> in prayer: Please <b>either let him die before her or let her die before him.</b> He was speaking about himself and his wife because he had grown to hate her so much. Rava <b>said to him: Did I not say to you not to pray for this matter?</b>
<b>Rav said in the name of Rabbi Reuven ben Itzterobili as follows: From the Torah, and from the Prophets, and from the Writings;</b> it implies that the decree that a specific <b>woman</b> is destined to be married <b>to</b> a specific <b>man</b> is <b>from God.</b> From where is this derived? It is <b>from the Torah, as it is written: “Then Laban and Bethuel answered and said: The thing comes from the Lord,</b> we cannot speak to you either bad or good” (Genesis 24:50). <b>From the Prophets, as it is written: “But his father and his mother knew not that it was of the Lord”</b> (Judges 14:4). <b>From the Writings, as it is written: “House and riches are the inheritance of fathers; but a prudent woman is from the Lord”</b> (Proverbs 19:14).
§ Apropos a teaching of Rabbi Reuven ben Itzterobili, the Gemara states that <b>Rav said in the name of Rabbi Reuven ben Itzterobili, and some say</b> that <b>it was taught in a <i>baraita</i></b> that <b>Rabbi Reuven ben Itzterobili said: A man is suspected of</b> having done <b>something</b> wrong <b>only if he has</b> indeed <b>done so. And if he did not do it wholly,</b> then probably <b>he did it partly. And if he did not do it</b> even <b>partly,</b> then probably <b>he thought in his heart to do it. And if he did not</b> even <b>think to himself to do it,</b> then certainly <b>he saw others doing it and was happy.</b> Suspicions do not arbitrarily arise about a person; therefore there is certainly some basis for them.
<b>Rabbi Ya’akov raised an objection:</b> Does the verse not say: <b>“And the children of Israel fabricated matters that were not right against the Lord their God”</b> (II Kings 17:9), which indicates that it is possible to make up stories about someone else even though they are entirely baseless. The Gemara answers: <b>There they did it in order to anger</b> God, but they did not actually think that what they were saying was true.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a challenge from a different source: The verse states: <b>“And they were jealous of Moses in the camp, of Aaron the Lord’s holy one”</b> (Psalms 106:16). <b>Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak said:</b> This verse <b>teaches that every man warned his wife</b> against seclusion <b>with Moses</b> because he was jealous. This implies that every man thought that his wife had secluded herself with Moses and sinned, although this was certainly not the case. This demonstrates that it is possible to suspect an absolutely innocent person. The Gemara answers: <b>There they did it out of hatred</b> for Moses. They did not actually suspect him of wrongdoing. Instead, their goal was to degrade him by leveling these false accusations against him.
The Gemara raises another challenge, based on yet another source: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear that which Rabbi Yosei said: May my portion</b> in the future world <b>be with one who is suspected</b> of a certain wrongdoing <b>but is innocent,</b> as the pain that such a person experiences atones for his sins. This statement also appears to imply that it is possible to suspect an absolutely innocent person. <b>And Rav Pappa said: They suspected me</b> of a certain wrongdoing <b>but I was not</b> guilty.
The Gemara answers: <b>It</b> is <b>not difficult. This</b> is referring <b>to a rumor that stops,</b> and therefore it is possible that it is groundless, whereas <b>that</b> is referring <b>to a rumor that does not stop,</b> and in that case there must be a factual basis for the suspicion. The Gemara asks: To be considered <b>a rumor that does not stop, for how long</b> must it persist? <b>Abaye said: My nurse told me: Local gossip lasts for a day and a half,</b> and then it is deemed to be a rumor that does not stop.
The Gemara comments: <b>This applies only</b> if the rumor <b>did not stop in between,</b> during the day and a half, <b>but</b> if <b>it stopped in between</b> then <b>we have no</b> problem <b>with it,</b> and it is not a persistent rumor. <b>And if</b> the rumor <b>stopped in between, we said</b> that it is considered baseless <b>only</b> if it <b>stopped</b> of its own accord and <b>not out of fear,</b> i.e., because the suspect is violent and therefore people are afraid to speak badly about him. But if the rumor <b>stopped out of fear,</b> then this dispensation does <b>not</b> apply, and it is still assumed that there must be some basis to the rumor.
<b>And we said</b> that a rumor that stopped is assumed to be baseless <b>only if it did not arise again. But</b> if <b>it arose again,</b> then this does <b>not</b> apply. <b>And we said</b> that a rumor that does not stop must be taken seriously <b>only if</b> the slandered person <b>has no enemies. But</b> if <b>he has</b> known <b>enemies,</b> then it can be assumed that <b>it was the enemies who disseminated the rumor.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One may not write bills of debt on</b> the intermediate days of <b>a Festival. But if</b> the lender <b>does not trust</b> the borrower, and he is concerned that the borrower will later deny the loan, <b>or</b> if the scribe <b>has nothing to eat,</b> then <b>he may write</b> a bill of debt during the Festival week.
<b>One may not write</b> Torah <b>scrolls, phylacteries, or <i>mezuzot</i> on the</b> intermediate days of <b>a Festival, nor may one correct a single letter, even in the</b> Torah <b>scroll of Ezra,</b> which was kept in the Temple and upon which all the Jewish communities relied. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: One may write phylacteries and <i>mezuzot</i> for himself</b> on the intermediate days of a Festival if he needs them.

Daf 19a

<b>And one may spin sky-blue</b> wool <b>for</b> his <b>ritual fringes on his thigh,</b> but not in the ordinary manner with a spindle, as this procedure must be performed in an altered manner on the intermediate days of a Festival.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>A person may write phylacteries and <i>mezuzot</i> for himself and spin sky-blue</b> wool <b>for his fringes on his thigh. And for others</b> he may do these things <b>as a favor,</b> but not for payment. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> If he initially made them for himself, <b>he may employ artifice, sell his own and</b> then <b>go back and write</b> new ones <b>for himself. Rabbi Yosei says: He may write and sell</b> them <b>in his</b> usual <b>manner,</b> in the amount that is <b>enough</b> to provide <b>for his livelihood.</b>
<b>Rav ruled for Rav Ḥananel, and some say</b> it was <b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana</b> who ruled <b>for Rav Ḥananel: The <i>halakha</i> is</b> that <b>one may write and sell</b> them <b>in his</b> usual <b>manner,</b> in the amount that is <b>enough</b> to provide <b>for his livelihood.</b>
§ The mishna taught: <b>And one may spin sky-blue</b> wool <b>for</b> his <b>ritual fringes on his thigh. The Sages taught</b> a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A person may spin sky-blue</b> wool <b>for his ritual fringes on his thigh, but not with a stone,</b> which can be used to form a small spindle and ease the spinning process; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. But the Rabbis say:</b> One may spin the sky-blue wool <b>even with a stone. Rabbi Yehuda said in</b> the name of Rabbi Eliezer: It is permitted <b>with a stone, but not with a spindle. And the Rabbis say:</b> One may spin this wool <b>with either a stone or a spindle.</b>
<b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said, and similarly Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The <i>halakha</i> is that one may spin the sky-blue wool for fringes on the intermediate days of a Festival, <b>both with a stone and with a spindle,</b> owing to the importance of the mitzva of ritual fringes. <b>And</b> similarly <b>the <i>halakha</i> is: One may write</b> phylacteries and <i>mezuzot</i> on the intermediate days of a Festival <b>in his</b> usual <b>manner and sell enough</b> to provide <b>for his livelihood.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who buries his deceased</b> relative <b>three days before a pilgrimage Festival</b> has <b>the decree of</b> the <b>seven-</b>day period of mourning, i.e., the <i>halakhot</i> and prohibitions associated with that period, <b>nullified for him</b> by the Festival. He is not required to complete this seven-day mourning period after the Festival. If one buries his deceased relative <b>eight</b> days before a pilgrimage Festival, then <b>the decree of thirty</b> days <b>is nullified for him.</b> The restrictions that ordinarily apply during this thirty-day mourning period no longer apply after the Festival.
This is <b>because</b> the Sages <b>said</b> a principle with regard to this issue: <b>Shabbat counts</b> as one of the days of mourning, although one may not mourn on it <b>and it does not interrupt</b> the mourning period, which continues after Shabbat. The <b>pilgrimage Festivals,</b> on the other hand, <b>interrupt</b> the mourning period, so that if one began mourning before such a Festival, then the mourning period is canceled by the Festival. <b>They do not,</b> however, <b>count.</b> If one did not begin mourning before the Festival, or if his relative died during the Festival, then he is required to complete his mourning period afterward, as the days of the Festival do not count toward the requisite days of mourning.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer says: From the time that the Temple was destroyed, <i>Shavuot</i> is like Shabbat,</b> because nowadays the days following <i>Shavuot</i> are not treated like Festival days. When the Temple stood, many of the Festival’s offerings that could not be sacrificed on <i>Shavuot</i> itself would be sacrificed during the six days following the Festival. Nowadays, however, when offerings are no longer sacrificed, <i>Shavuot</i> lasts for only one day in Eretz Yisrael, and therefore it is treated like Shabbat with regard to mourning: It counts as one of the days of mourning, but does not interrupt the period of mourning.
<b>Rabban Gamliel says:</b> Even <b>Rosh HaShana and Yom Kippur</b> are considered <b>like</b> the <b>pilgrimage Festivals,</b> in that they interrupt the mourning period but are not counted toward the days of mourning. <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> The <i>halakha</i> <b>is neither in accordance with the statement of</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>nor in accordance with the statement of </b> Rabban Gamliel. <b>Rather,</b> with regard to mourning, <b><i>Shavuot</i></b> is treated <b>like the</b> other <b>pilgrimage Festivals,</b> whereas <b>Rosh HaShana and Yom Kippur</b> are treated <b>like Shabbat.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the mishna’s statement that the seven- and thirty-day periods of mourning are nullified, <b>Rav said:</b> The <b>decree,</b> meaning the main prohibitions of the period, <b>was nullified,</b> but <b>the days</b> of mourning themselves <b>were not</b> entirely <b>nullified.</b> Instead, these periods of mourning remain to some degree. <b>And so said Rav Huna: The decree was nullified,</b> but <b>the days</b> of mourning themselves <b>were not nullified. And Rav Sheshet said: Even the days</b> of mourning <b>were also nullified.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason that the days</b> themselves <b>were not nullified?</b> The Gemara explains: It is so <b>that if</b> one observed eight days of mourning before the Festival, so that the prohibition against hair cutting was nullified before the Festival, but for whatever reason <b>he did not cut his hair on the eve of the Festival, he is prohibited from cutting his hair after the Festival</b> until the end of the thirty-day period of mourning. In other words, the mourning period was not entirely nullified, and since he did not take advantage of the allowance to cut his hair before the Festival, he must observe the prohibitions applying during the thirty-day period of mourning after the Festival as well.

Daf 19b

<b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who buries his dead</b> relative <b>three days before a pilgrimage Festival, the decree of</b> the <b>seven-</b>day period of mourning is <b>nullified for him.</b> If one buries his dead relative <b>eight</b> days before a Festival, <b>the decree of thirty</b> days <b>is nullified for him,</b> and therefore he may <b>cut his hair on the eve of the Festival. If,</b> however, <b>he did not cut his hair on the eve of the Festival, he is prohibited from cutting his hair after the Festival</b> until the end of the thirty-day period of mourning.
<b>Abba Shaul says:</b> Even if he failed to cut his hair on the eve of the Festival, <b>he is permitted to cut his hair after the Festival, for just as</b> his observance of <b>the mitzva of three</b> days of mourning prior to the Festival <b>cancels the decree of</b> the <b>seven-</b>day period of mourning, and so if the deceased was buried three days before the Festival, the mourner is no longer required to continue this period of mourning after the Festival, <b>so too,</b> his observance of <b>the mitzva of seven</b> days of mourning before the Festival <b>cancels the decree of</b> the <b>thirty-</b>day period of mourning.
The Gemara asks: Why does Abba Shaul speak of the observance of <b>seven</b> days of mourning? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna that it requires <b>eight</b> days? The Gemara explains: <b>Abba Shaul maintains</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that of <b>an entire day,</b> and therefore <b>the seventh day counts as both</b> the final day of the seven-day period of mourning and the first day of the thirty-day period of mourning. <b>Rav Ḥisda said</b> that <b>Ravina bar Sheila said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul.</b>
<b>And</b> furthermore, even <b>the Rabbis concede to Abba Shaul that when one’s eighth</b> day of mourning <b>occurs on a Shabbat,</b> which is also <b>the eve of a Festival, he is permitted to cut his hair on Friday,</b> which is the seventh day. Since he is unavoidably prevented from cutting his hair on the eighth day, as it is prohibited for him to cut his hair on Shabbat, he may do so already on Friday, although it is only his seventh day of mourning.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which Rav Amram said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> With regard to <b>a mourner, once the comforters have stood up</b> to leave <b>his</b> house on the seventh day of his mourning, <b>he is immediately permitted to bathe? In accordance with whose</b> opinion? <b>In accordance</b> with the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul.</b> He maintains that a part of the seventh day is treated as an entire day with regard to this issue.
<b>Abaye said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul with regard to the seventh</b> day of mourning, <b>and the Rabbis concede to Abba Shaul with regard to the thirtieth day,</b> for with regard to the thirtieth day <b>we</b> certainly <b>say</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that of <b>an entire</b> day, and so a mourner may already cut his hair on the morning of the thirtieth day.
<b>Rava said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul with regard to the thirtieth day</b> of mourning, <b>but the <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with Abba Shaul with regard to the seventh</b> day. <b>And</b> the Sages of <b>Neharde’a say: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul with regard to both</b> the seventh day and the thirtieth day, for <b>Shmuel said: The <i>halakha</i> follows the statement of the</b> more <b>lenient</b> authority <b>in</b> matters relating to <b>mourning.</b>
§ The Gemara asks now about the thirty-day mourning period: <b>From where do we</b> derive the <b>thirty-day</b> period of mourning? The Gemara answers: <b>It is learned</b> by way of a verbal analogy <b>between</b> one instance of the word <b><i>pera</i></b> and a different instance of the word <b><i>pera</i></b> stated with regard to <b>a nazirite. Here,</b> in the instructions given to Aaron not to mourn the deaths of his sons, <b>it is written: “Let the hair of your heads not grow loose [<i>tifra’u</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 10:6), which indicates that ordinary mourners are required to grow their hair long. <b>And there,</b> with regard to a nazirite, <b>it is written: “He shall let the hair of his head grow long [<i>pera</i>]”</b> (Numbers 6:5). <b>Just as there,</b> in the case of the nazirite, he must grow his hair for <b>thirty</b> days, <b>so too, here</b> a mourner must grow his hair for <b>thirty</b> days.
The Gemara asks: <b>And there,</b> with regard to a nazirite, <b>from where do we</b> derive that he must grow his hair for thirty days? Nowhere is this explicitly stated with regard to a nazirite. The Gemara answers: <b>Rav Mattana said: An unspecified naziriteship,</b> when one takes a vow of naziriteship without specifying for how long, extends for <b>thirty days. What is the reason</b> for this? <b>The verse states: “He shall be sacred,</b> and he shall let the hair of his head grow” (Numbers 6:5), and <b>“He shall be [<i>yihye</i>],”</b> which is written <i>yod</i>, <i>heh</i>, <i>yod</i>, <i>heh</i>, <b>has a numerical value [<i>gimatriyya</i>] of thirty,</b> as <i>yod</i> has a numerical value of ten and <i>heh</i> has a numerical value of five. This implies that an unspecified naziriteship extends for thirty days, and by way of the verbal analogy, this is applied to mourning as well.
<b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, said: Everyone,</b> including Abba Shaul, who says that part of a day is treated like a full day, <b>agrees</b> that <b>when his third</b> day of mourning <b>occurs on the eve of the Festival,</b> the mourner <b>is prohibited from bathing</b> his body <b>until the evening.</b> In this case, the principle that the legal status of part of the day is like that of an entire day does not apply. Rather, one must observe three full days of mourning. He must therefore wait until the evening and wash himself with cold water, or wait until the intermediate days of the Festival and bathe in hot water.
<b>Rav Neḥemya, son of Rav Yehoshua, said: I</b> once <b>found Rav Pappi and Rav Pappa sitting</b> together <b>and saying: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua. There are</b> those <b>who say</b> a different version of this tradition: <b>Rav Neḥemya, son of Rav Yosef, said: I</b> once <b>found Rav Pappi, Rav Pappa, and Rav Huna, son of Yehoshua, sitting</b> together <b>and saying: Everyone concedes</b> that <b>when the third</b> day of mourning <b>occurs on the eve of a Festival,</b> the mourner <b>is prohibited from bathing</b> his body <b>until the evening.</b>
<b>Abaye inquired of Rava:</b> If <b>one buried</b> his dead relative <b>on the Festival</b> itself, <b>does the Festival count toward his thirty-</b>day period of mourning, <b>or does the Festival not count toward his thirty-</b>day period of mourning? Abaye elaborated on his question: <b>I do not ask</b> whether or not the Festival counts toward his <b>seven-</b>day period of mourning because <b>the obligation</b> to observe <b>seven</b> days of mourning <b>does not apply</b> at all <b>during the Festival,</b> and therefore he must certainly observe the seven-day mourning period, beginning from after the Festival. <b>What I am asking is</b> with regard to the <b>thirty-</b>day period of mourning, because certain aspects of the <b>mitzva of</b> the <b>thirty-</b>day mourning period <b>do</b> in fact <b>apply during the Festival,</b> e.g., the prohibitions to launder clothes and cut hair. <b>What,</b> then, is the <i>halakha</i>: Do the days of the Festival count toward the thirty days or not?
Rava <b>said to him:</b> The Festival <b>does not count</b> toward the thirty days. Abaye <b>raised an objection to</b> Rava’s opinion from the following <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one buries his dead</b> relative <b>two days before a Festival, he must count five days</b> of mourning <b>after the Festival, and</b> during this period <b>his work is performed</b> for him <b>by others. And his menservants and maidservants do</b> this work <b>in private inside his house, and the public need not occupy themselves with him</b> by coming to console him,

Daf 20a

<b>for they already occupied themselves with him</b> when they came to console him <b>during the Festival. The</b> general <b>principle with regard to the matter</b> is as follows: <b>Any</b> activity <b>that</b> is prohibited to the mourner <b>because it is</b> an expression of <b>mourning is interrupted by the Festival</b> and remains prohibited afterward. <b>And anything that involves the public’s occupation</b> with the mourner, e.g., coming to the mourner to offer him comfort and condolence, <b>is not interrupted by the Festival,</b> for people console the mourner during the Festival as well.
If <b>he</b> buries his relative <b>three days</b> before <b>the end of the Festival,</b> the mourner <b>must count seven</b> days of mourning <b>after the Festival</b> because his mourning never began. On the <b>first four days</b> of his mourning, <b>the public must occupy themselves with him</b> and come to console him. On <b>the three last days,</b> however, <b>the public need not occupy themselves with him, as they already occupied themselves with him during the Festival.</b> That is to say, they certainly came to console him during the Festival, although the period of mourning did not actually begin until afterward. <b>And the Festival counts for him</b> toward the thirty-day mourning period.
With regard to the statement of the <i>baraita</i> that the Festival counts toward the thirty-day period of mourning: <b>What, is it not</b> referring to <b>the latter clause</b> of that <i>baraita</i>, which speaks about a case where the burial was performed during the Festival itself? If this is the case, then a refutation of Rava’s opinion is stated explicitly, that the Festival counts toward the thirty-day mourning period, even if the deceased was buried on the Festival itself. Rava rejects this argument: <b>No,</b> it is referring to <b>the first clause,</b> which discusses a case where the burial was performed before the Festival, and since rites of mourning were observed already before the Festival, the Festival is counted toward the thirty-day period of mourning. Therefore, it is not a refutation of Rava’s <i>halakha</i>.
Abaye <b>raised an objection to</b> Rava’s opinion from another <i>baraita</i>, in which it was taught: <b>A pilgrimage Festival counts toward the thirty-</b>day period of mourning. <b>How so?</b> If one <b>buried</b> his dead relative <b>at the beginning of a pilgrimage Festival, he must count seven</b> days of mourning <b>after the Festival, and</b> during this period <b>his work is performed by others. And his menservants and maidservants perform</b> this work <b>in private inside his house, and the public need not occupy themselves with him</b> and come to console him, <b>for they already occupied themselves with him during the Festival. And the Festival counts for him.</b> Here it is explicitly stated that the Festival counts toward the thirty-day period of mourning, even when the deceased was buried during the Festival itself. The Gemara concludes: This is indeed <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of Rava’s opinion.
<b>When Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, <b>he said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Even</b> if <b>one buried</b> his dead relative <b>during the Festival,</b> the Festival counts toward his thirty-day period of mourning. <b>And, similarly, Rabbi Elazar ruled for Rabbi Pedat, his son: Even if one buried</b> his dead relative <b>during the Festival</b> it counts towards his thirty days.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one fulfilled the</b> obligation of <b>overturning the bed for three days before the pilgrimage Festival, he is no</b> longer <b>required to overturn it after the Festival;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. But the Rabbis say: Even</b> if one overturned his bed for only <b>one day, or even for</b> only <b>one hour,</b> he is not required to overturn it after the Festival.
<b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, said: This is the statement of Beit Shammai,</b> and <b>that is the statement of Beit Hillel.</b> In other words, Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis disagreed about a matter that was the subject of an earlier dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel, <b>for Beit Shammai say: Three days, and Beit Hillel say: Even one day.</b>
<b>Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said, and some say</b> a different version of this line of transmission: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said to Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and to Rav Huna: Even one day, even one hour.</b> The Gemara cites <b>Rava,</b> who <b>said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the <i>tanna</i></b> of <b>our</b> mishna, who <b>said three</b> days. Based on this, we rule that the mourning rites are not canceled after the Festival unless they were observed for at least three days before the Festival.
<b>Ravina happened</b> to come to the city of <b>Sura on the</b> banks of the <b>Euphrates</b> River. <b>Rav Ḥaviva said to Ravina: What is</b> the <b><i>halakha</i></b> with regard to this issue? <b>He said to him: Even one day, and even one hour.</b>
§ It was related that <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, Rabbi Ami, and Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa were</b> once <b>sitting in the pavilion of Rabbi Yitzḥak ben Elazar</b> and were conversing. <b>A matter emerged from among them: From where</b> is it derived that the rites of <b>mourning</b> are observed for <b>seven</b> days? <b>As it is written: “And I will turn your Festivals into mourning”</b> (Amos 8:10). <b>Just as a Festival</b> lasts for <b>seven</b> days, <b>so too mourning</b> lasts for <b>seven</b> days.
The Gemara asks: <b>And say</b> that perhaps mourning is like <b><i>Shavuot</i>, which is</b> only <b>one day.</b> The Gemara rejects this argument: <b>That</b> derivation, from the one day of <i>Shavuot</i>, <b>is required for</b> what was stated by <b>Reish Lakish, as Reish Lakish said in the name of Rabbi Yehuda Nesia: From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> mourning in the case of <b>distant tidings,</b> i.e., when one hears that one of his relatives died a long time ago, <b>applies for only one day? As it is written: “And I will turn your Festivals into mourning,” and we find</b> with regard to <b><i>Shavuot</i> that one day is</b> also <b>called a Festival.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>recent tidings</b> of a relative’s death, mourning <b>applies for seven- and thirty-</b>day periods. In the case of <b>distant tidings, it applies only for one day. What are</b> considered <b>recent tidings and what are</b> considered <b>distant tidings? Recent</b> tidings are news that arrives <b>within thirty</b> days of the person’s death. <b>Distant</b> tidings are news that arrives <b>after thirty</b> days; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. And the Rabbis say: Both</b> in the case of <b>recent tidings and</b> in the case of <b>distant tidings,</b> mourning <b>applies for seven- and thirty-</b>day periods.
<b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Wherever you find an individual being lenient and the majority being stringent, the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with the majority, except for this</b> case, <b>for even though Rabbi Akiva is lenient and the Rabbis are stringent, the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b> This is in keeping with the principle <b>stated by Shmuel: The <i>halakha</i> follows the statement of the</b> more <b>lenient</b> authority <b>in</b> matters relating to <b>mourning.</b>
It was related that <b>Rav Ḥanina received</b> distant <b>tidings of his father’s</b> death <b>from Bei Ḥozai. He came before Rav Ḥisda</b> to ask what he should do. Rav Ḥisda <b>said to him:</b> In the case of <b>distant tidings,</b> mourning <b>applies for only one day.</b> It was similarly related that <b>Rav Natan bar Ami received a report about his mother’s</b> death <b>from Bei Ḥozai. He came before Rava,</b> and Rava <b>said to him: They said</b> that in the case of <b>distant tidings,</b> mourning <b>applies for only one day.</b>
Rav Natan <b>raised an objection to</b> Rava’s opinion based on what was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement</b> that mourning applies for only one day <b>said?</b> In the case of <b>the</b> other <b>five close relatives,</b> over whose <b>death</b> it is <b>a mitzva</b> to mourn, i.e., son, daughter, brother, sister, and spouse. <b>But for one’s father or mother,</b> one is required to mourn for <b>seven and thirty</b> days, even when the report of the parent’s death is received some time after the event.
Rava <b>said to him:</b> This <b>is an individual</b> opinion, <b>and we do not hold in accordance with his</b> opinion, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: There was <b>an incident and the father of Rabbi Tzadok died in</b> the city of <b>Ginzak, and they informed him</b> of his father’s passing only <b>after three years. He came and asked Elisha ben Avuya and the Elders with him</b> what he should do. <b>And</b> Elisha ben Avuya <b>said</b> to him: <b>Observe</b> the rites of mourning for <b>seven and thirty</b> days. <b>And when the son of Rabbi Aḥiyya died in the Diaspora,</b> and Rabbi Aḥiyya was informed about his passing a long time afterward, he <b>sat</b> in mourning for <b>seven and thirty</b> days. Therefore, it is clear that this opinion was held only by a few individual Sages, but it was not generally accepted.
The Gemara asks: <b>Is that so,</b> that this is Rabbi Aḥiyya’s opinion? <b>But Rav was the son of Rabbi Ḥiyya’s brother and</b> also <b>the son of Rabbi Ḥiyya’s sister,</b> for Rav’s father, Aivu, was Rabbi Ḥiyya’s paternal half brother, and Rav’s mother was Rabbi Ḥiyya’s maternal half sister. <b>When</b> Rav <b>went there,</b> Eretz Yisrael, his uncle Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to him: Is</b> your <b>father,</b> Aivu, still <b>alive?</b>

Daf 20b

Not wishing to deliver unfortunate news, Rav <b>said to him: Is</b> my <b>mother</b> still <b>alive?</b> In other words, why do you not ask me about my mother, who is your sister, whether she is still among the living? Rabbi Ḥiyya <b>said to him: Is</b> your <b>mother</b> still <b>alive?</b> Rav <b>said to him: Is</b> my <b>father</b> still <b>alive?</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya understood from Rav’s failure to provide him with straight answers that both his brother and his sister had passed away. Rabbi Ḥiyya immediately <b>said to his servant: Remove my shoes</b> as a sign of mourning, and afterward <b>carry my clothes behind me,</b> as I am going <b>to the bathhouse.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>Learn three</b> <i>halakhot</i> <b>from this</b> incident with Rabbi Ḥiyya. <b>Learn from this</b> that <b>a mourner is prohibited from wearing shoes,</b> and therefore Rabbi Ḥiyya removed his shoes upon hearing of the deaths of his brother and sister. <b>And learn from this</b> that in the case of <b>distant tidings</b> of a relative’s death, the mourning rites <b>apply for only one day</b> and no more. <b>And learn from this</b> that the legal status of <b>part of the day is like</b> that <b>of an entire</b> day, as immediately following the removal of his shoes, Rabbi Ḥiyya went to the bathhouse.
In any case, Rabbi Ḥiyya observed only one day of mourning after receiving a belated report of his siblings’ passing, in contrast to Rabbi Aḥiyya, who observed seven and thirty days. The Gemara’s question assumes that Rabbi Ḥiyya and Rabbi Aḥiyya are the same person. The Gemara answers: This is not difficult, for <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya is a discrete</b> person <b>and Rabbi Aḥiyya is a discrete</b> person; despite the similarity between their names, these are two different people.
§ <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Avin said:</b> If <b>one received recent tidings</b> of a relative’s death <b>during a Festival,</b> when it is prohibited to mourn, <b>and after the Festival they became distant</b> tidings, as after the Festival thirty days had already elapsed since the relative’s passing, the Festival <b>counts for him</b> toward the number of days that make it a belated report. <b>And,</b> consequently, after the Festival <b>he observes only one day</b> of mourning. Similarly, <b>Rabbi Adda of Caesarea taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> If <b>one received recent tidings</b> of a relative’s passing <b>on Shabbat, and at the conclusion of Shabbat it became distant tidings, he observes only one day</b> of mourning.
§ The Gemara asks: Upon receiving a belated report of a close relative’s passing, <b>does one rend</b> his garment <b>or does he not rend</b> it? <b>Rabbi Mani said: He does not rend</b> it, whereas <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said:</b> He does <b>rend</b> it. <b>Rabbi Mani said to Rabbi Ḥanina: Granted,</b> it makes sense <b>that according to my</b> position this is consistent, <b>as I say</b> that <b>he does not rend</b> his garment, and <b>this is because there is no seven-</b>day period of <b>mourning. But according to you, who says</b> that <b>he does rend</b> his garment, <b>is there rending without a seven-</b>day period of <b>mourning</b> afterward?
The Gemara asks: Is there <b>not? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> reported by <b>Isi, father of Rabbi Zeira, and some say</b> it was taught by <b>the brother of Rabbi Zeira before Rabbi Zeira:</b> If at first <b>one did not have</b> his own <b>garment to rend, and he acquired</b> one <b>during the seven-</b>day period of mourning, he should <b>rend</b> it then. But if he acquired it only <b>after the seven-</b>day period of mourning, <b>he does not rend</b> it.
<b>Rabbi Zeira explained</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>after him,</b> filling in a missing element: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement</b> that one does not rend his garment after the seven-day period of mourning <b>said?</b> In the case of <b>the</b> other <b>five close relatives</b> over whose <b>death</b> it is <b>a mitzva</b> to mourn, i.e., son, daughter, brother, sister, and spouse. <b>But for one’s father or mother, one continues</b> to be obligated <b>to rend</b> his garment if he receives the garment after the seven-day mourning period as well.
