This apparently contradicts Rabbi Mani’s statement that there is no rending without seven days of mourning.
The Gemara answers: <b>When that</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is taught,</b> it is referring to a symbolic act meant <b>to honor his father or mother.</b> It is for one’s father or mother that he must rend his garment, even if it becomes available only after the seven-day period of mourning. Essentially, however, the obligation to rend one’s garments applies only when it is followed by a seven-day period of mourning.
§ <b>The Sages taught:</b> With regard to <b>all</b> of the relatives <b>mentioned in the</b> Torah in the <b>passage</b> referring to <b>priests, for which a priest becomes impure, a mourner must mourn for them. And they are: His wife, his father, and his mother, his brother and his</b> unmarried <b>sister</b> from the same father, <b>his son, and his daughter.</b> The Sages <b>added</b> other relatives <b>to</b> this list: <b>His</b> maternal <b>brother and his unmarried sister from the same mother, and his married sister, whether from the same father or from the same mother.</b> One mourns for these relatives, <b>although</b> a priest would not become impure for them.
<b>Just as one mourns for them, so too he mourns for their</b> relatives’ relatives, who are his <b>second-degree relatives.</b> That is to say, just as one is required to mourn over his close relatives, so too he is required to mourn over his relatives’ close relatives, which are known as second-degree relatives. For example, if his father’s father, his son’s son, his brother’s son, or the like passed away, he must join his relatives in their mourning; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: He mourns only over his son’s son and his father’s father,</b> but not over the other relatives of his relatives. <b>And the Rabbis say: Any</b> relative <b>over whom one would mourn</b> if that person died, <b>one mourns with him</b> when he is in mourning.
The Gemara asks: The statement of <b>the Rabbis is</b> identical to the statement of the <b>first <i>tanna</i>,</b> Rabbi Akiva. What does their statement add? The Gemara answers: The practical difference <b>between them</b> is with regard to whether one mourn <b>with him</b> only <b>in the</b> same <b>house.</b> According to the Rabbis, one is required to mourn with his relative only while he is with him in the same house, whereas according to Rabbi Akiva, he is required to observe mourning even when he is not with him. This is <b>like what Rav said to his son, Ḥiyya, and</b> it is <b>similarly</b> like what <b>Rav Huna said to his son Rabba,</b> when the latter’s wife was in mourning: <b>In her presence practice mourning,</b> but <b>out of her presence do not practice mourning.</b>
It is related that <b>the son of Mar Ukva’s father-in-law,</b> i.e., Mar Ukva’s brother-in-law, <b>died,</b> and Mar Ukva <b>thought to sit</b> in mourning <b>over him for</b> the <b>seven- and thirty-</b>day periods of mourning. <b>Rav Huna went into his</b> house, <b>found him</b> observing the rites of mourning, and <b>said to him: Do you desire to eat mourners’ food [<i>tzudaniyyata</i>]?</b> The Sages <b>said</b> that one should observe mourning <b>in honor of his wife only</b> when she is in mourning over the death of <b>his father-in-law or his mother-in-law.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One whose father-in-law or mother-in-law died may not force his wife to paint [<i>koḥelet</i>]</b> her eyelids <b>or put rouge [<i>pokeset</i></b>] on her face while she is in mourning. <b>Rather, he should overturn his bed, and observe</b> the rites of <b>mourning with her. And similarly, when her father-in-law or mother-in-law dies, she may not paint</b> her eyelids <b>or put rouge</b> on her face. <b>Rather, she should overturn her bed and practice</b> the rites of <b>mourning with him.</b>
<b>And it was taught in another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>Even though</b> the Sages <b>said that</b> a husband <b>may not force his wife to paint</b> her eyelids <b>or put rouge</b> on her face when she is in mourning, i.e., that she may not treat her mourning lightly, <b>actually, they said</b> that <b>she may pour his cup</b> of wine, <b>make his bed, and wash his face, hands, and feet,</b> as these activities are expressions of affection between husband and wife rather than unnecessary adornment or a belittlement of the mourning. These two <i>baraitot</i> <b>contradict each other:</b> The first <i>baraita</i> indicates that the husband must practice the rites of mourning together with his wife, whereas the second one bars him only from forcing her to treat her own mourning lightly.
<b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from this</b> as follows: <b>Here,</b> where one is required to observe the <i>halakhot</i> of mourning together with his wife, the <i>baraita</i> is referring to the death of <b>his father-in-law or mother-in-law. There,</b> where one is not required to mourn, the <i>baraita</i> is referring to the death of his wife’s <b>other relatives</b> for whom she is required to mourn. The Gemara summarizes: <b>Conclude from this</b> that this is the case. <b>This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The Sages <b>said</b> that one is required to observe the rites of mourning <b>in honor of his wife only</b> when she is in mourning over <b>his father-in-law or his mother-in-law.</b>
It was further related that <b>the son of Ameimar’s son died,</b> and Ameimar <b>rent</b> his garment <b>over him. His son came</b> before him, and <b>he rent</b> his garments again <b>in the presence of his</b> son, as an expression of empathy with his son’s pain and grief. Later, <b>he remembered</b> that when he rent his garments in his son’s presence <b>he rent</b> them while <b>sitting,</b> and therefore <b>he stood up</b> and <b>rent</b> his garment again while <b>standing.</b>
With regard to this issue, the Gemara reports that <b>Rav Ashi said to Ameimar: From where do we</b> derive that <b>rending</b> must be done while <b>standing?</b> He responded: <b>As it is written</b> about the deaths of Job’s sons: <b>“Then Job arose, and rent his coat”</b> (Job 1:20).

Daf 21a

Rav Ashi challenged him: <b>However, if</b> that is <b>so,</b> one should be required to stand while performing the ritual through which the brother-in-law frees the <i>yevama</i> of her levirate bonds [<i>ḥalitza</i>], as the verse states: <b>“And if he stands and he says: I do not wish to take her”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:8). <b>Here</b> must he <b>also</b> stand for the ceremony? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <i>ḥalitza</i> may be performed <b>whether</b> the man is <b>sitting, or standing, or reclining?</b>
<b>He said to him: There,</b> in the case of <i>ḥalitza</i>, <b>it is not written: And he will stand and he will say,</b> which would imply an obligation to stand; whereas <b>here,</b> in the case of rending, <b>it is written: “Then</b> Job <b>arose, and rent</b> his coat” (Job 1:20).
<b>Rami bar Ḥama said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that rending must be</b> performed while <b>standing? As it is stated: “Then Job arose, and rent</b> his coat” (Job 1:20). The Gemara asks: <b>Perhaps he did something extra</b> beyond what is required, and actually there is no obligation to stand; <b>as, if you do not say</b> that he did more than what was required of him, then how do you explain the continuation of the verse: <b>“And he shaved his head”</b> (Job 1:20)? Is every mourner required to act in <b>this</b> manner <b>also</b> and shave his head?
The Gemara concludes: <b>Rather,</b> this source must be rejected, and instead the <i>halakha</i> is derived <b>from here,</b> the verse that describes David’s mourning over his son: <b>“Then the king arose, and rent his garments”</b> (II Samuel 13:31). The Gemara asks: But <b>perhaps he</b> too <b>did something extra</b> beyond what is required, and he was not actually obligated to stand; <b>as, if you do not say</b> that he did more than what was required of him, then how do you explain the continuation of the verse: <b>“And he lay on the earth”</b> (II Samuel 13:31)? Is every mourner required to act in <b>this</b> manner <b>also?</b>
<b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If a mourner <b>sat on a bed, on a chair, on a large mortar [<i>udyanei</i>], or on the ground, he does not fulfill his obligation through any of them. And Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> This is <b>because he did not fulfill</b> his obligation to <b>overturn his bed,</b> even if he lay on the ground. This indicates that there is no requirement whatsoever to lie on the ground.
Ameimar <b>said to</b> Rav Ashi: The verse does not mean that David actually lay on the ground. Rather, it was <b>as if</b> he were <b>on the ground,</b> since he overturned his bed and brought it closer to the ground. If this is the case, then Ameimar acted correctly, and a mourner should rend his garments while standing.
§ <b>The Sages taught: These are the activities that a mourner is prohibited from</b> engaging <b>in: He is prohibited from working, and from bathing, and from anointing</b> himself with oil, <b>and from engaging in sexual relations, and from wearing shoes. And he is prohibited from reading in the Torah, and in the Prophets, and in the Writings, and from studying in the Mishna, in the midrash, and in the <i>halakhot</i>, and in the Talmud, and in the <i>aggadot</i>. But if the public needs him</b> to teach them these things, <b>he need not refrain</b> from doing so. <b>There was an incident that the son of Rabbi Yosei died in Tzippori, and</b> Rabbi Yosei <b>entered the study hall and expounded</b> there <b>for the entire day.</b>
It was related that <b>a calamity,</b> i.e., a death in the family, once <b>befell Rabba bar bar Ḥana,</b> and <b>he thought not to go out to</b> deliver <b>his exposition. Rabbi Ḥanina said to him:</b> Is it not taught that <b>if the public needs him</b> to teach them these things, <b>one need not refrain</b> from doing so? <b>He</b> then <b>thought to place an interpreter alongside him,</b> who would sound his words to the public, as was normally done for such an exposition. <b>Rav said to him: It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> similarly: <b>Provided that he does not place the disseminator</b> alongside him.
The Gemara asks: <b>But how,</b> then, should he <b>act</b> so that he can be heard? The Gemara answers: It is <b>like that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: There was <b>an incident and the son of Rabbi Yehuda bar Ilai died, and</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>entered the study hall. And Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya</b> entered after him and <b>sat by his side.</b> Rabbi Yehuda bar Ilai then <b>whispered</b> his lecture <b>to Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya, and Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya</b> whispered it <b>to the disseminator, and the disseminator sounded to the public</b> what had been told to him. In this way, it became known that Rabbi Yehuda bar Ilai was in mourning.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: For <b>the first three days, a mourner is prohibited from donning phylacteries. From the third</b> day <b>and on, and including the third</b> day, <b>he is permitted to don phylacteries. And if new faces,</b> i.e., people who had not yet come to console him, <b>come, he need not remove</b> them, although these people may falsely conclude that he had also donned phylacteries during the first two days of his mourning; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. Rabbi Yehoshua says: For the first two days, a mourner is prohibited from donning</b> phylacteries. <b>From the second</b> day, <b>and including the second</b> day, <b>he is permitted to wear</b> phylacteries. <b>But if new faces come in</b> to console him, <b>he must remove</b> his phylacteries.
<b>Rav Mattana said: What is the reason of Rabbi Eliezer? As it is written: “And the days of weeping and mourning for Moses ended”</b> (Deuteronomy 34:8). The plural term “days” implies a minimum of two, and it indicates that the main portion of the mourning period is the first two full days. <b>Rav Eina said: What is the reason of Rabbi Yehoshua? As it is written:</b> “And I will make it as the mourning for an only son, <b>and its end as a bitter day”</b> (Amos 8:10), i.e., a single “day.”
The Gemara asks: <b>But also</b> for <b>Rabbi Yehoshua, isn’t it written</b> in the Torah: <b>“And the days</b> of weeping and mourning for Moses <b>ended,”</b> thereby implying that this period lasts for two days? The Gemara answers: He could have <b>said to you</b> that <b>Moses was different, because the mourning for him was more intense,</b> and the people mourned for him longer than usual. The Gemara asks: <b>But also</b> for <b>Rabbi Eliezer, isn’t it written</b> in the Torah: <b>“And its end as a bitter day”?</b> The Gemara answers: He could have said to you that <b>the main bitterness is</b> only <b>one day,</b> but the severity of the mourning lasts for two days.
<b>Ulla said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer with regard to</b> the <b>removal</b> of phylacteries. A mourner need not remove them when new people come in to console him. <b>And the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua with regard to the donning</b> of phylacteries, and so a mourner may already don phylacteries on the second day.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the scholars: <b>According</b> to <b>Ulla,</b> who rules in accordance with Rabbi Yehoshua that a mourner may don phylacteries <b>on</b> his second <b>day</b> of mourning, is the mourner required to <b>remove</b> them if new people arrive on that day, <b>or</b> is he <b>not</b> required to <b>remove</b> them?
The Gemara answers: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> what <b>Ulla said</b> explicitly: <b>One removes</b> his phylacteries when new people come to console him, <b>and he dons</b> them again when they leave, <b>even</b> if he must don and remove them <b>a hundred times. This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Yehuda ben Teima says: He removes</b> his phylacteries <b>and dons</b> them again, <b>even</b> if he must do so <b>a hundred times.</b>
<b>Rava said: Once he dons</b> his phylacteries, <b>he does not remove</b> them <b>again.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But wasn’t it Rava</b> himself who <b>said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the <i>tanna</i></b> of <b>our</b> mishna, who <b>said</b> that the most serious period of mourning is <b>three</b> days? How then does he rule in accordance with the <i>baraita</i> that a mourner may don phylacteries during these days?

Daf 21b

The Gemara answers: <b>A mitzva is different,</b> and since it is a mitzva to put on phylacteries, the mourner is required to do so, even during the first three days.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: <b>During the first three days</b> after his bereavement, <b>a mourner is prohibited from working, even if he is a poor person who is supported by charity. From this</b> point <b>forward, he may do</b> work <b>privately in his</b> own <b>home</b> if he needs to do so. <b>And</b> similarly <b>a woman may spin</b> thread <b>on a spindle in her</b> own <b>home</b> when she is mourning.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: <b>A mourner during the first three days</b> after his bereavement <b>may not go to</b> another <b>mourner’s house</b> to console him. <b>From this</b> point <b>forward, he may go, but he may not sit among the consolers, but rather in the place of those being consoled,</b> i.e., with the mourners in that house.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in yet another <i>baraita</i>: <b>A mourner, during the first three days</b> after his bereavement, <b>is prohibited from extending greetings</b> to others. <b>From the third</b> day <b>to the seventh</b> day, <b>he may respond</b> when other people address him, <b>but he may not extend greetings</b> to them. <b>From this</b> point <b>forward, he may extend greetings and respond in his</b> usual <b>manner.</b>
The Gemara asks: Is he really <b>prohibited</b> from extending <b>greetings during the first three</b> days of mourning? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: There was <b>an incident and the sons of Rabbi Akiva died, and all the Jews entered to eulogize them with a great eulogy.</b>
<b>When they were about to take leave, Rabbi Akiva stood on a large bench and said: Our brothers, the house of Israel, listen! Even had my two sons been bridegrooms, I would have been consoled on account of the honor you have shown</b> them. <b>If you came</b> to console <b>for the sake of Akiva, there are many Akivas in the marketplace</b> whom the Jews do all not come to console. <b>Rather,</b> certainly <b>this is what you said</b> to yourselves: <b>“The Torah of his God is in his heart”</b> (Psalms 37:31), and you wished to show your respect for the Torah. <b>All the more so is your reward doubled,</b> for you have consoled mourners and shown respect for the Torah. <b>Return</b> now <b>to your homes in peace.</b> This indicates that a mourner may greet other people even on the first day of his mourning.
The Gemara answers: Showing <b>respect for the public is different.</b> Rabbi Akiva was permitted to greet them to demonstrate courtesy and respect.
§ It was taught in the aforementioned <i>baraita</i>: <b>From the third</b> day <b>to the seventh</b> day, <b>he may respond</b> when other people address him, <b>but he may not extend greetings</b> to them. <b>From this</b> point <b>forward, he may extend greetings and respond in his</b> usual <b>manner.</b>
<b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from another <i>baraita</i> that states: <b>One who finds another in mourning during the</b> first <b>thirty days</b> of that person’s bereavement <b>may</b> still <b>speak words of consolation to him, but he should not extend greetings toward him.</b> If he finds him <b>after thirty days, he may extend greetings toward him, but he should not speak words of consolation to him,</b> so as not to remind him of his pain.
If it was the mourner’s <b>wife</b> who <b>died and he married another woman</b> within thirty days of his first wife’s death, <b>one may not enter his house to speak words of consolation with him,</b> so as not to offend his new wife. If, however, <b>he finds him</b> alone <b>in the marketplace, he may speak to him with gentle words and in a serious manner.</b> This indicates that the prohibition against extending greetings lasts for thirty days and not just seven.
<b>Rav Idi bar Avin said:</b> The two <i>baraitot</i> refer to different situations: The first <i>baraita</i> is referring to the mourner <b>himself, who may extend greetings [<i>shalom</i>] to others</b> after the completion of his seven days of mourning, <b>as the others are at peace [<i>shalom</i>].</b> The second <i>baraita</i>, which speaks of a prohibition that lasts thirty days, is referring to <b>other people, who may not extend greetings to him, as he,</b> the mourner, <b>is not at peace.</b>
The Gemara challenges: <b>But from</b> the fact <b>that it teaches</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that after the third day of his bereavement, the mourner may <b>respond</b> when other people address him, <b>by inference</b> others <b>may extend greetings to him.</b> The Gemara answers: This is referring to a case where people <b>did not know</b> that he was in mourning and unknowingly extended greetings to him.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> if the <i>baraita</i> speaks of a case where the people who greeted him were unaware that he was in mourning, then <b>there also,</b> during the first three days of his bereavement, he should be permitted to respond when other people address him. The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> during the first three days, <b>he must inform them</b> that he is in mourning <b>and not respond</b> to their greetings. <b>Here,</b> after the first three days, <b>he need not inform them</b> about his bereavement, but rather he may respond to their greetings.
<b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from another <i>baraita</i> that states: <b>One who finds another in mourning during</b> his <b>twelve months</b> of bereavement <b>may</b> still <b>speak words of consolation to him, but he should not extend greetings toward him.</b> If he finds him <b>after twelve months, he may extend greetings toward him, and he should not speak words of consolation to him. He may, however, speak to him indirectly,</b> i.e., he may say to him: May you be consoled, without mentioning the name of the deceased.
<b>Rabbi Meir said: One who finds another in mourning after twelve months and speaks to him words of consolation, to what may</b> this situation <b>be likened? To a person who broke his leg and it healed,</b> and afterward a physician <b>found him and said to him: Come to me, for I will break it</b> a second time <b>and</b> then <b>I will heal it, so that you may know how good my medicines are</b> and how well they work. One who consoles his friend after so much time has passed acts in a similar fashion, stirring up an old wound and then trying to heal it. In any event, it appears that one must not extend greetings to a mourner during the entire twelve-month mourning period.
The Gemara answers: <b>This is not difficult. This</b> <i>baraita</i>, which says that one must not extend greetings to a mourner for twelve months, is referring to one who lost <b>his father or mother. That</b> <i>baraita</i>, which teaches that a mourner may be greeted after seven days, is referring to one who is in mourning over <b>other relatives.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>There too,</b> in the case of other relatives after thirty days, <b>let him speak words of consolation to him indirectly.</b> Why does the <i>baraita</i> say that after thirty days he should not speak words of consolation to him at all? The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so. And what</b> is meant by the words: <b>He should not speak words of consolation to him?</b> This means that he may not console him <b>in his</b> usual <b>manner, but he may speak to him indirectly.</b>
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: If <b>a mourner comes</b> to the house of mourning <b>from a nearby place during the first three days</b> of mourning, <b>he counts</b> his days of mourning <b>with</b> the other mourners from the time of the burial and completes his mourning with them. This is the case even if he may end up observing mourning for only five or six days. But if <b>he came from a distant place, he counts on his own</b> seven complete days from the time that he was informed of his relative’s death. <b>From this</b> point <b>forward,</b> i.e., after the first three days of mourning, <b>even if he came from a nearby place, he counts</b> seven days <b>on his own. Rabbi Shimon says: Even</b> if <b>he came on the seventh day from a nearby place, he counts</b> and completes the seven-day period of mourning <b>with</b> the other mourners.
<b>The Master said,</b> citing the <i>baraita</i>: If <b>a mourner comes</b> to the house of mourning <b>from a nearby place during the first three days</b> of mourning, <b>he counts</b> his days of mourning <b>with</b> the other mourners and completes his mourning with them. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said that Rabbi Yoḥanan said: This is</b> in a case <b>where the principal</b> member <b>of the household is in the house,</b> and therefore all the other family members follow his mourning. If, however, it is the principal member of the family who comes home, he certainly does not follow the other members, but rather he counts seven days on his own.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the scholars:

Daf 22a

If <b>the principal</b> member <b>of the household went</b> with the remains of the deceased <b>to the cemetery</b> and did not return for several days, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? If another mourner came to the house of mourning during his absence, does he follow the principal member of the household and count from the time of the burial, or does he count from the time that the funeral procession departed, like the other members of the household?
The Gemara answers: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear that which Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said that Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Even</b> if the <b>principal</b> member <b>of the household went to the cemetery,</b> the mourner who comes home during his absence <b>counts with them,</b> i.e., the other members of the household who did not go to the cemetery. The Gemara asks: Does <b>he</b> really <b>count</b> and complete his mourning <b>with them? But isn’t it taught</b> otherwise in a <i>baraita</i>, that <b>he counts</b> seven days <b>on his own?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>This is not difficult. This</b> statement, that he counts with them, is referring to a case <b>where</b> the principal member of the household <b>came</b> home <b>within three</b> days. <b>And the other</b> ruling, that he counts on his own, is referring to a case <b>where he did not come</b> home <b>within three</b> days. This conclusion is <b>similar to</b> what <b>Rav said to the sons of Hatzleponi</b> when they were in mourning: <b>Those who come</b> home <b>within three</b> days <b>should count with you;</b> whereas <b>those who do not come</b> home <b>within three</b> days <b>should count on their own.</b>
<b>Rava said to the people of Meḥoza:</b> Those of <b>you who do not follow the coffin</b> all the way to the place of interment <b>should begin counting</b> your days of mourning <b>from when you turn your faces from the city gates</b> to return home. Since the dead were commonly transported long distances and buried far away, most of the mourners did not accompany their deceased relatives to the actual site of burial.
§ The Gemara cites the <i>baraita</i> taught above: <b>Rabbi Shimon says: Even</b> if one of the mourners <b>came on the seventh day from a nearby place, he counts with</b> the other mourners and completes the seven-day period of mourning with them. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Gamda said that Rabbi Yosei ben Shaul said that Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said: And this is</b> the <i>halakha</i>, provided that <b>he came and found consolers</b> still present <b>in the house.</b>
§ <b>Rav Anan asks:</b> If the consolers <b>had</b> already <b>stirred</b> themselves <b>to stand up</b> and leave but <b>did not</b> yet actually <b>stand up</b> and leave, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is it considered as if the consolers have already left or not? This question was not answered, and the dilemma <b>stands</b> unresolved.
The Gemara relates that <b>a colleague of Rabbi Abba bar Ḥiyya learned</b> the following principle as a tradition <b>from Rabbi Abba.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And who was</b> this colleague? It was <b>Rabbi Zeira. And some say</b> a different version of this tradition: <b>A colleague of Rabbi Zeira</b> learned a tradition <b>from Rabbi Zeira.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And who is</b> this colleague? It was <b>Rabbi Abba, son of Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel with regard to</b> an animal that is unsuitable for human consumption because of severe organic disease or congenital defect, making it <b>a <i>tereifa</i>, and the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon with regard to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>mourning.</b>
The Gemara explains: The <i>halakha</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon with regard to mourning, concerning that which we</b> just <b>said</b> about a mourner who arrives on the seventh day. The <i>halakha</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel with regard to</b> defects in animals that render them <b><i>tereifa</i></b> concerning <b>that which we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the animal’s <b>intestines became perforated, but</b> the hole <b>was stopped up by mucus,</b> so that nothing comes out of this hole, the animal is <b>fit.</b> Unlike an ordinary hole in the intestines, this hole does not render the animal a <i>tereifa</i> and make it unfit for consumption. This is <b>the statement of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is this <b>mucus? Rav Kahana said: The fat [<i>shirka</i>] of the intestines that comes out under pressure. Someone</b> whose name was not given <b>said: May I merit to go up</b> to Eretz Yisrael <b>and learn this <i>halakha</i> from the mouth of its author.</b>
<b>When he went up</b> from Babylonia to Eretz Yisrael, <b>he found Rabbi Abba, son of Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba,</b> and <b>said to him: Did the Master say</b> that <b>the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel with regard to</b> defects that render the animal <b>a <i>tereifa</i>? He said to him: I said</b> just the opposite, namely, that <b>the <i>halakha</i> is not</b> in accordance with Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, and therefore such a hole in the animal’s intestine makes it unfit for eating.
He asked him again: <b>What</b> about the other ruling reported in your name, that the <i>halakha</i> <b>is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon with regard to mourning?</b> Is this accurate? <b>He said to him:</b> This issue is <b>subject to dispute, as it was stated: Rav Ḥisda said: The <i>halakha</i></b> is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Shimon, <b>and similarly Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It is the <b><i>halakha</i>.</b> But <b>Rav Naḥman said: It is not the <i>halakha</i>.</b>
The Gemara concludes: <b>The <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel with regard to</b> the aforementioned issue of <b><i>tereifa</i>, and the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with Rabbi Shimon with regard to mourning, as Shmuel stated</b> a principle: <b>The <i>halakha</i> follows the opinion of the</b> more <b>lenient</b> authority <b>in</b> matters relating to <b>mourning.</b>
§ It was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, it <b>is praiseworthy</b> for <b>one</b> to be <b>quick</b> in taking <b>the bier</b> out for burial. But <b>in</b> the case of <b>one’s father or mother,</b> acting in this manner <b>is condemnable,</b> as one should draw out the period of acute mourning for his parent. If, however, <b>it was Friday or the eve of a Festival,</b> then <b>one is praiseworthy</b> for expediting his parent’s burial because <b>he does this only out of respect for his father or mother,</b> as he does not want them to remain unburied for the duration of Shabbat or the Festival.
<b>With regard to all</b> other <b>dead,</b> if the mourner <b>wishes</b> he may <b>reduce his business</b> due to mourning. If, however, <b>he wishes</b> not to do so, he <b>need not</b>

Daf 22b

<b>reduce</b> it. <b>In</b> the case of <b>his father or mother,</b> he must always <b>reduce</b> his business.
<b>With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, if the mourner <b>wishes, he may remove</b> his garment from one of his shoulders, and if <b>he wishes</b> not to remove it, <b>he need not remove</b> it. However, <b>in</b> the case of <b>his father or mother, he</b> must always <b>remove</b> his garment from one of his shoulders.
There was <b>an incident when the father of a leading authority of</b> his <b>generation died, and</b> the authority <b>wished to remove</b> his garment from one shoulder. <b>Another leading authority of the generation</b> also <b>wished to remove</b> his own garment together <b>with him,</b> in order to join him in his mourning, <b>but</b> due to this the first person <b>refrained and did not remove</b> his garment, so that his colleague would not remove his garment as well.
<b>Abaye said: The leading authority of the generation</b> mentioned here is <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, and <b>the leading authority of the generation</b> who was <b>with him</b> was <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa. And some say: The leading authority of the generation</b> was <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa,</b> and <b>the leading authority of the generation</b> who was <b>with him</b> was <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara examines this issue: <b>Granted, according to the one who said</b> that <b>the leading authority of the generation</b> who was <b>with him was Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>this is</b> the reason that <b>he refrained and did not remove</b> his garment from his shoulder. That is to say, Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa refrained from doing so because he did not wish to cause the <i>Nasi</i> to remove his own garment.
<b>But according to the one who said</b> that it is <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa</b> who was the leader of the generation with him, <b>why did</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>refrain and not remove</b> his garment from his shoulder? <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel,</b> the father of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, was also <b>the <i>Nasi</i>, and everyone is required to remove</b> his garment from his shoulder for him, as was the accepted practice. Therefore, Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa would also have been required to bare his shoulder. Why, then, did Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi prevent him from doing so? The Gemara concludes: Indeed <b>this</b> is <b>difficult.</b>
§ The Gemara returns to the continuation of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>With regard to all deceased</b> relatives except for parents, <b>one may cut his hair after thirty days. In</b> the case of <b>one’s father or mother,</b> one may not cut his hair <b>until his colleagues have rebuked him</b> for his hair being too long. <b>With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, <b>he may enter a place</b> where <b>a joyous</b> celebration is taking place <b>after thirty days; in</b> the case of <b>his father or mother,</b> he may enter such a place only <b>after twelve months.</b>
<b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana said:</b> The ruling that a mourner may enter a house of joy after thirty days applies specifically <b>to a joyous social gathering,</b> that is to say, to the joyous meals that a group of friends would eat together, each taking a turn hosting. But this ruling does not apply to a large joyous occasion, such as a wedding feast. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i> which adds: <b>And</b> also <b>for joyous social gatherings, thirty days.</b> This implies that when the <i>baraita</i> speaks of joyous celebrations without further specification, it is not referring to joyous social gatherings, but even to weddings and other joyous occasions. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>it</b> is <b>difficult.</b>
<b>Ameimar taught</b> the previous discussion <b>as follows: Rabba bar bar Ḥana said</b> an alternative version of the discussion: <b>For a joyous social gathering one is permitted to enter immediately.</b> The Gemara poses a question: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>For joyous celebrations and for joyous social gatherings,</b> one must wait <b>thirty</b> days?
The Gemara answers: <b>This is not difficult. This</b> ruling, of the <i>baraita</i>, is referring <b>to an initial</b> gathering, when the mourner is the first in the group of friends to host. The <i>baraita</i> teaches that in such a situation the mourner is required to wait thirty days before doing so. <b>That</b> ruling, of Rabba bar bar Ḥana, is referring <b>to a reciprocal</b> gathering. The mourner’s friends have already hosted these gatherings, and now it is his turn to host. Since he is required to host such a gathering for his colleagues, he need not postpone it. Rather, he may host the group immediately.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, <b>one rends</b> his garment the length <b>of a handbreadth,</b> and that suffices. <b>In</b> the case of <b>his father or mother,</b> he must rend his garment <b>until he reveals his heart. Rabbi Abbahu said: What is the verse</b> that teaches that the rent must be a handbreadth? <b>“And David took hold of his clothes and rent them”</b> (II Samuel 1:11), <b>and taking hold cannot</b> be done for a garment <b>less than a handbreadth.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> teaches further: <b>With regard to all</b> other deceased relatives, <b>even if he is wearing ten garments,</b> one on top of the other, <b>he rends only</b> his <b>outer</b> garment. But <b>in</b> the case of <b>his father or mother, he must rend them all.</b> Failure to rend <b>his undergarment,</b> however, <b>does not invalidate</b> the fulfillment of the mitzva.
<b>Both a man and a woman</b> are required to rend their garments. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: A woman</b> first <b>rends</b> her <b>inner</b> garment <b>and turns</b> it <b>around,</b> so that the tear is <b>on her back. And</b> only <b>afterward</b> does <b>she rend</b> her <b>outer</b> garment, so that she does not expose her chest.
<b>With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, if <b>one wishes he may rip apart</b> his garment <b>on the hem,</b> rather than merely expanding the neck hole, so that the tear stands out distinctly from the opening of the garment. If he <b>wishes</b> not to do this, <b>he does not rip apart</b> the hem in this manner. That is to say, one may simply enlarge the neck hole, although rending a garment in this way makes the tear less prominent. <b>In</b> the case of <b>one’s father or mother,</b> however, <b>he must</b> always <b>rip apart</b> the hem.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Any rending that does not rip apart</b> his garment <b>on the hem</b> of the garment <b>is nothing other than a frivolous rent</b> of no significance, as it must be evident that one has rent his garment in mourning and that the rent is not merely an imperfection in the garment. <b>Rabbi Abbahu said: What is the reason</b> for <b>Rabbi Yehuda’s</b> opinion? <b>As it is written: “And he took hold of his own clothes and he rent them in two pieces”</b> (II Kings 2:12). <b>From that which is stated: “And he rent,” do I not know that</b> he rent them <b>in two? Rather,</b> these words teach <b>that</b> the rent clothes must <b>appear as if they were torn into two</b> pieces, i.e., the tear must be obvious and visible.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, <b>one may tack</b> the tear with rough stitches <b>after seven days, and one may join</b> the edges more carefully <b>after thirty</b> days. But <b>in</b> the case of <b>one’s father or mother, he may tack</b> the tear only <b>after thirty days, and he may never</b> again <b>join</b> the edges more carefully. <b>A woman,</b> however, <b>may tack</b> the tear <b>immediately, due to her honor,</b> for it would be dishonorable for her to be seen with torn garments.
<b>When Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, <b>he said that Rabbi Yoḥanan said: With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, <b>if one wishes, he may rend</b> his garment <b>with</b> his <b>hand;</b> and if <b>he wishes, he may rend</b> it <b>with a utensil</b> in a way that will preserve it. But <b>in</b> the case of <b>his father or mother,</b> he must rend his garment <b>with his hand</b> in a manner that will utterly ruin it.
<b>And Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said that Rabbi Yoḥanan said: With regard to all</b> other <b>deceased</b> relatives, one rends his garment <b>on the inside,</b> meaning, he rends his inner garment and not necessarily his outermost garment. <b>In</b> the case of <b>one’s father or mother,</b> however, <b>he must rend</b> the garment <b>on the outside,</b> i.e., the outermost garment. <b>Rav Ḥisda said: And likewise, over a <i>Nasi</i>,</b> one is required to rend his garment as he does over his father.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i> in which it was taught: The <i>halakhot</i> of rending for the death of other people referred to in the <i>baraita</i>, e.g., a <i>Nasi</i>, a president of the court, or one’s teacher, <b>were likened to</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of rending for <b>one’s father or mother only with regard to</b> the issue of carefully <b>rejoining</b> the edges of the rent, as in all of these cases it is prohibited to mend one’s garment with precise stitches.
<b>What, is</b> this <i>baraita</i> <b>not</b> also referring <b>even</b> to one who rends his garment <b>for the <i>Nasi</i>?</b> The Gemara rejects this: <b>No,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to the other people, <b>aside</b> from one who rends his garment for the <i>Nasi</i>, as rending for the <i>Nasi</i> is the same as rending for one’s father with regard to all aspects of the rending.
It was related that the <b><i>Nasi</i> died,</b> and <b>Rav Ḥisda said to Rav Ḥanan bar Rava: Turn the mortar over and stand on it, and show</b> the <b>rent to everyone.</b> Everyone will then rend his garment in this manner, as everyone is required to rend his garment over the death of the <i>Nasi</i>.
§ It was further taught: <b>For</b> mourning <b>a Sage, one removes</b> his garment <b>from the right</b> shoulder. <b>For</b> the <b>president of the court</b> he removes his garment <b>from the left</b> shoulder. <b>For</b> the <b><i>Nasi</i></b> he removes his garment <b>from here and from here,</b> from both shoulders.
<b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: When <b>a Sage dies, his study hall ceases</b> its regular study as a sign of mourning over him. When <b>the president of the court dies, all of the study halls in his city cease</b> their regular study, and everyone <b>enters the synagogue and changes their places</b> there as a sign of mourning over him. <b>Those who</b> ordinarily <b>sit in the north</b> should <b>sit in the south, and those who</b> ordinarily <b>sit in the south</b> should <b>sit in the north.</b> When <b>a <i>Nasi</i> dies, all study halls cease</b> their regular study. On Shabbat, <b>the members of the synagogue enter the synagogue</b> for public Torah reading, which requires a congregation of ten,

Daf 23a

<b>and seven</b> people <b>read</b> from the Torah. <b>And</b> then <b>they leave</b> and pray on their own. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Korḥa says:</b> It is <b>not that they stroll</b> afterward <b>in the marketplace,</b> but <b>rather they sit</b> at home <b>in silent</b> mourning.
<b>And</b> furthermore, <b>one may not speak</b> about <b><i>halakha</i> or <i>aggada</i> in a house of mourning,</b> as this is an activity that brings people joy. <b>It was said of Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel that he would speak about <i>halakha</i> and <i>aggada</i> in a house of mourning.</b>
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i>: During the <b>first week</b> after his bereavement, <b>the mourner may not go out of the opening of his house.</b> During the <b>second</b> week, <b>he may go out, but he may not sit in his</b> usual <b>place</b> in the synagogue. During the <b>third</b> week, <b>he may sit in his</b> usual <b>place but he may not speak.</b> During the <b>fourth</b> week, <b>he is like any</b> other <b>person.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: They did not need to say</b> that <b>during the first week</b> the mourner <b>may not go out of the opening of his house.</b> This teaches us nothing new, <b>as</b> at that time <b>everyone goes into his house to console him. Rather,</b> during the <b>second</b> week, he may <b>not go out of the opening of his house.</b> During the <b>third</b> week, <b>he may go out, but he may not sit in his</b> usual <b>place</b> in the synagogue. During the <b>fourth</b> week, <b>he may sit in his</b> usual <b>place, but he may not speak.</b> During the <b>fifth</b> week, <b>he is like any</b> other <b>person.</b>
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> another <i>baraita</i>: During <b>the entire thirty-day</b> period of mourning, it is prohibited <b>to marry.</b> If <b>one’s wife died, it is prohibited to marry another wife until three Festivals pass</b> since her death. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> Until the <b>first and second</b> Festivals have passed, <b>he is prohibited</b> from marrying; before the <b>third</b> Festival, however, he is <b>permitted</b> to do so.
<b>And if he does not have children, he is permitted to marry</b> another wife <b>immediately due to</b> the need to not <b>neglect</b> the mitzva to <b>be fruitful and multiply.</b> Since he has not yet fulfilled the mitzva of procreation, he is still required to marry a wife. Any delay might result in a lost opportunity for marriage. Similarly, if his wife died and <b>left him young children, he is permitted to marry</b> another wife <b>immediately, so that</b> she might <b>take care of them.</b>
There was <b>an incident when the wife of Yosef the Priest died, and he said to her sister at the cemetery</b> immediately after the funeral: <b>Go and care for your sister’s children.</b> In other words, he alluded that he wished to marry her immediately. <b>But even though</b> he married her immediately, <b>he did not engage in sexual relations with her for a long time</b> afterward. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the term: <b>A long time? Rav Pappa said: After thirty days.</b>
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> yet another <i>baraita</i>: During <b>the entire thirty-day</b> period of mourning, it is prohibited <b>to</b> wear <b>ironed</b> garments, <b>whether they are new garments or old garments taken out of the press,</b> as ironed garments appear to be new. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi disagrees and <b>says:</b> The Sages <b>prohibited</b> wearing <b>only new garments. Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, says: They prohibited</b> wearing <b>only new white garments.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Abaye went out</b> while he was in mourning <b>in an old white garment, in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rava,</b> on the other hand, <b>went out in a new, red Roman cloak,</b> as he acted <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon.</b>
It was taught in the mishna: This is <b>because</b> the Sages <b>said</b> that <b>Shabbat counts</b> as one of the days of mourning, <b>but it does not interrupt</b> the mourning period, which continues after Shabbat. It is stated that with regard to mourning on Shabbat, there is a difference in practice between <b>the residents of Judea and the residents of the Galilee. These say:</b>

Daf 23b

<b>There is</b> some <b>mourning on Shabbat,</b> i.e., with regard to mourning rites that can be observed privately in the mourner’s home and will go unnoticed by other people; <b>whereas those say: There is no mourning on Shabbat</b> at all.
The Gemara explains: <b>The ones who said</b> that <b>there is</b> some <b>mourning on Shabbat</b> rely on <b>that</b> which is <b>taught,</b> that Shabbat <b>counts</b> as one of the days of mourning, implying that some degree of mourning applies on that day. <b>The ones who said</b> that <b>there is no mourning on Shabbat</b> at all base this on <b>that</b> which is <b>taught</b> that Shabbat <b>does not interrupt</b> the mourning period.
The latter argue as follows: <b>If it should enter your mind</b> to say that <b>there is</b> some <b>mourning on Shabbat,</b> there is a difficulty, for <b>now</b> that it has been stated that actual <b>mourning applies</b> on Shabbat, <b>is</b> it <b>necessary</b> to teach us that this day does not <b>interrupt</b> the mourning period? Rather, the conclusion must be that there is no mourning on Shabbat whatsoever.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in the mishna that Shabbat <b>counts</b> as one of the days of mourning, implying that it is just like the other days of mourning, and at least some mourning rites are practiced on it? The Gemara answers: <b>Since</b> the mishna <b>wished to teach</b> in <b>the latter clause</b> that the days of a Festival <b>do not count</b> toward the requisite days of mourning, <b>it taught</b> also <b>in the first clause</b> that Shabbat <b>counts</b> as one of the days of mourning, although no mourning rites are practiced on it.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the ones who said</b> that <b>there is</b> some <b>mourning on Shabbat, isn’t</b> it <b>taught</b> in the mishna that <b>it does not interrupt</b> the mourning period, which would have been unnecessary to say if the mourning rites are practiced on it? The Gemara answers: This was not necessary for itself, but <b>due to</b> the fact that the mishna <b>wished to teach</b> in <b>the latter clause</b> that the Festivals <b>interrupt</b> the mourning, <b>it taught</b> also <b>in the first clause</b> that Shabbat <b>does not interrupt</b> it.
The Gemara asks: <b>Let us say</b> that this is <b>parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i></b> with regard to whether or not some mourning rites are observed even on Shabbat, for it was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One whose deceased</b> relative <b>is laid out before him eats in another room.</b> If <b>he does not have another room, he eats in the house of a friend. If he does not have a friend’s house</b> available, <b>he makes a partition ten handbreadths</b> high between him and the deceased, so that he may eat. If <b>he does not have material with which to make a partition, he averts his face</b> from the dead <b>and eats.</b>
<b>And</b> in any case, <b>he does not recline while he eats,</b> as reclining is characteristic of a festive meal; <b>and he neither eats meat nor drinks wine; and he does not recite a blessing</b> before eating to exempt others from their obligation; <b>and he does not</b> recite the formula to <b>invite</b> the participants in the meal to join together in the Grace after Meals, <b>and they do not recite a blessing over him nor do others invite him</b> to join in the Grace after Meals, as he cannot be a member of the three required to recite the formula. <b>And he is exempt from the recitation of <i>Shema</i>, and from</b> the <i>Amida</i> <b>prayer, and from</b> donning <b>phylacteries, and from</b> performing <b>all</b> of <b>the mitzvot mentioned in the Torah.</b>
<b>But on Shabbat he reclines</b> at the meal, as per his custom, <b>and eats; and he eats meat and drinks wine; and he recites blessings</b> to exempt others from their obligation; <b>and</b> he recites the formula to <b>invite</b> the participants in the meal to join together in the Grace after Meals, <b>and others may recite blessings on his behalf and invite</b> him to join in the Grace after Meals. <b>And he is</b> also <b>obligated in the recitation of <i>Shema</i>, and in</b> the <i>Amida</i> <b>prayer, and in</b> the mitzva of <b>phylacteries, and in all the mitzvot mentioned in the Torah. Rabban Gamliel says: Since he is obligated</b> to fulfill <b>these</b> mitzvot associated with Shabbat, <b>he is obligated</b> to fulfill <b>all</b> of the mitzvot on Shabbat.
Rabban Gamliel’s statement is vague. The following clarifies it: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The practical difference <b>between them,</b> the opinion of the anonymous first <i>tanna</i> and Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion, is with regard to <b>sexual relations.</b> According to Rabban Gamliel, the acute mourner is obligated in the mitzva to engage in marital intercourse with his wife on Shabbat, just as he is obligated in all the other mitzvot.
The Gemara asks: <b>What, is it not with regard to this</b> issue that <b>they disagree:</b> Is it not <b>that</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the anonymous first <i>tanna</i>, <b>holds</b> that <b>there is</b> some <b>mourning on Shabbat</b> with regard to private issues, and therefore the mourner does not engage in sexual relations; <b>and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabban Gamliel, <b>holds</b> that <b>there is no mourning on Shabbat</b> at all?
The Gemara rejects this argument: <b>From where</b> do you reach this conclusion? <b>Perhaps the first <i>tanna</i></b> is <b>saying</b> that it is prohibited for the grieving relative to engage in sexual relations in the case dealt with <b>only there, because his deceased</b> relative <b>is laid out before him</b> and has not yet been buried. <b>But here,</b> with regard to the period of mourning, when <b>his dead</b> has been buried and <b>is no</b> longer <b>laid out before him,</b> sexual relations are <b>not</b> prohibited.
<b>And</b> alternatively, perhaps <b>Rabban Gamliel is saying</b> that the grieving relative is permitted to engage in sexual relations <b>only there, where</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>mourning have not</b> yet <b>taken effect,</b> as mourning begins only after the burial. <b>But here, where</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>mourning</b> have already <b>taken effect,</b> he may <b>also</b> prohibit sexual relations.

Daf 24a

<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan asked of Shmuel: Is there</b> some <b>mourning on Shabbat or is there no mourning on Shabbat</b> at all? <b>He said to him: There is no mourning on Shabbat</b> at all.
<b>The Sages sat before Rav Pappa and said in the name of Shmuel: A mourner who engaged in sexual relations during his days of mourning is liable</b> to receive <b>the death penalty</b> at the hand of Heaven. <b>Rav Pappa said to them: It was said</b> that sexual relations are <b>prohibited,</b> but not that the offender is guilty of a capital crime. <b>And</b> the ruling <b>was said in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> and not in the name of Shmuel.
<b>And if you heard</b> a statement on this issue <b>in the name of Shmuel,</b> then <b>you heard as follows: Rav Taḥlifa bar Avimi said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: A mourner who did not let</b> his hair <b>grow wild and did not rend</b> his garments <b>is liable</b> to receive <b>the death penalty</b> at the hand of Heaven, <b>as it is stated</b> following the deaths of Nadab and Abihu concerning the surviving sons of Aaron: <b>“Let not the hair of your heads go loose, neither rend your clothes, that you not die”</b> (Leviticus 10:6). They were instructed not to mourn, so as not to interfere with the dedication of the Tabernacle. From here it may be deduced <b>that</b> any <b>other</b> mourner <b>who did not let</b> his hair <b>grow wild or rend</b> his clothes <b>is liable</b> to receive <b>the death penalty.</b>
<b>Rafram bar Pappa said:</b> A Sage <b>taught in <i>Evel Rabbati</i>,</b> a separate tractate devoted to the <i>halakhot</i> of mourning: <b>A mourner is prohibited from engaging in sexual relations during his days of mourning.</b> There was <b>an incident with one who engaged in sexual relations during his days of mourning and</b> he was punished, for after his death <b>pigs dragged away his corpse.</b>
<b>Shmuel</b> offered a mnemonic device with regard to the observance of mourning rites on Shabbat, and <b>said: <i>Peh</i>, <i>ḥet</i>, <i>zayin</i> are obligatory.</b> These letters combine to create a mnemonic standing for: Uncovering the head [<i>periat rosh</i>], reversing the torn garment [<i>ḥazarat kera</i>], and standing the bed upright [<i>zekifat hamitta</i>]. However, <b><i>nun</i>, <i>tav</i>, <i>reish</i>,</b> which stand for: Wearing shoes [<i>ne’ilat hasandal</i>], marital relations [<i>tashmish hamitta</i>], and hand washing [<i>reḥitzat yadayim</i>], <b>are optional.</b>
This is explained: <b>Uncovering the head,</b> i.e., removing the head covering usually worn by a mourner; <b>reversing the tear to the back,</b> so that it will not be visible; and <b>standing the bed upright</b> in the usual position, so that it is not overturned, are all <b>obligatory</b> for the mourner to uphold in honor of Shabbat. However, <b>wearing shoes, engaging in sexual relations,</b> and <b>washing one’s hands and feet in hot water in the evening are optional</b> activities on Shabbat. If a mourner wishes to refrain from them he may do so. <b>And Rav said: Uncovering the head is also an optional</b> activity.
The Gemara asks: <b>According to Shmuel, what is different about wearing shoes</b> that it is <b>optional?</b> His reasoning presumably is that <b>not everyone wears shoes</b> at all times, and therefore not wearing shoes will not be perceived by others as a sign of mourning. If so, the same thing may be said about <b>uncovering the head also,</b> i.e., <b>not everyone uncovers his head</b> at all times, as some people always walk around with their heads wrapped. This, too, should not be seen as a clear display of mourning, and therefore uncovering one’s head should also be treated as optional.
The Gemara answers: <b>Shmuel</b> conforms <b>to his</b> standard line of <b>reasoning,</b> for <b>Shmuel said: Any rending</b> of garments <b>that is not</b> done <b>at the time of</b> the <b>most intense</b> grief, i.e., the time of death, <b>is not</b> considered proper <b>rending</b> of garments. <b>And any wrapping</b> of the head <b>that is not</b> done <b>in the manner of the wrapping of the Ishmaelites,</b> who wrap their heads on all sides, <b>is not</b> considered proper <b>wrapping.</b> Since Shmuel requires that a mourner wrap his head in this special manner, one who acts in this way on Shabbat openly displays his mourning, as people do not ordinarily wrap their heads in this manner. What is this wrapping? <b>Rav Naḥman demonstrated</b> the procedure by covering up his face to the <b>sides of the beard,</b> leaving only the center of his face exposed.
<b>Rabbi Ya’akov said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: They taught</b> that a mourner is required to remove his head covering on Shabbat <b>only when he has no shoes on his feet,</b> so that he not appear too obviously to be in mourning. <b>But if he has shoes on his feet,</b> then <b>his shoes prove</b> that <b>he</b> is not observing the rites of mourning on Shabbat.
The Gemara returns to Shmuel’s statement that <b>any rending</b> of garments <b>that is not</b> done <b>at the time of most intense</b> grief and pain <b>is not</b> considered proper <b>rending</b> of garments. The Gemara poses a question: <b>But</b> when <b>they said to Shmuel</b> that <b>Rav had passed away, he rent twelve garments on account of him, and said: The man of whom I was in fear,</b> owing to his great learning, <b>has gone</b> and died. Similarly, when <b>they told Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina had passed away, he rent thirteen</b> expensive <b>wool [<i>mileta</i>] garments [<i>itztelei</i>] on account of him, and said: The man of whom I was in fear has gone.</b> When each of these Sages received the news that their distinguished colleague had died, it was no longer the time of the most intense grief, i.e., the time of death, and yet they rent their garments.
The Gemara answers: <b>The Sages are different. Since their teachings are mentioned all the time,</b> every time they are mentioned <b>is like the time of most intense</b> grief, as the pain over their death is once again renewed.
<b>Ravin bar Adda said to Rava: Your student, Rav Amram, said</b> that <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> as follows: For <b>all seven days</b> of mourning, <b>a mourner</b> must keep <b>the tear</b> in his garment <b>before him</b> so that it will be seen. <b>And if he comes to change</b> clothing, <b>he changes and rends</b> the new garment. <b>On Shabbat,</b> he turns <b>the tear to his back, and if he comes to change</b> clothing, <b>he changes but does not tear</b> the second garment. From here it seems that there is an obligation to rend garments during the entire week of mourning, not only at the most intense moment of grief.
The Gemara answers: <b>When that</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is taught,</b> it is referring to the specific case of rending one’s garment <b>in honor of his father or mother.</b> Owing to the special honor due to one’s parents, it is inappropriate for the child to wear a garment that is not torn. Consequently, if he changes clothing, he must tear the new garment, even if it is no longer the most intense time of grief.
The Gemara asks: <b>Those</b> additional <b>tears</b> that are made in honor of one’s father or mother, <b>may they be</b> properly <b>mended</b> at the end of the son’s period of mourning, <b>or may they not be</b> properly <b>mended,</b> like the tear made for one’s father or mother at the most intense moment of grief? The Gemara answers: <b>They disagree about</b> this issue, <b>the father of Rav Oshaya and bar Kappara. One said: They may not be mended, and one said: They may be mended.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>Conclude that</b> it was <b>Rav Oshaya’s father</b> who <b>said</b> that such rents <b>may not be</b> properly <b>mended, for Rav Oshaya said:</b> Such rents <b>may not be</b> properly <b>mended. From whom did he hear</b> this <i>halakha</i>? Was it <b>not from his father?</b> The Gemara rejects this argument: <b>No,</b> this cannot be offered as a proof, as perhaps <b>he heard it from bar Kappara,</b> who was <b>his teacher.</b>
§ <b>Rava said: A mourner may walk about his house in</b> a torn <b>robe [<i>unkali</i>]</b> on Shabbat, since he is not doing so in public. It was related that <b>Abaye found Rav Yosef walking back and forth in his house</b> on Shabbat when he was in mourning <b>with a scarf spread over his head</b> in the manner of mourning. <b>He said to him: Master, do you not maintain</b> that <b>there is no mourning on Shabbat? He said to him: This is what Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Matters that are in private apply,</b> and so when one is in his house he must observe the rites of mourning even on Shabbat.
§ The mishna taught: <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: Since the Temple was destroyed, <i>Shavuot</i> is like Shabbat</b> with regard to mourning. Rabban Gamliel says: Even Rosh HaShana and Yom Kippur are considered like Festivals. The Gemara comments that <b>Rav Giddel bar Menashya said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Gamliel.</b>
<b>And some teach this</b> ruling <b>of Rav Giddel bar Menashya</b> as referring <b>to the following</b> case: It is taught in a <i>baraita</i>: Within the first <b>thirty days</b> after birth, <b>an infant</b> that dies <b>is taken out</b> for burial <b>in</b> one’s <b>bosom,</b> that is to say, he is carried to his grave in one’s arms, not in a coffin. <b>And he is buried by one woman and two men,</b> there being no need for a quorum of ten men. <b>But</b> he should <b>not</b> be taken out <b>by one man and two women,</b> because it is prohibited for one man to seclude himself with two women.

Daf 24b

<b>Abba Shaul says:</b> The infant may be taken out <b>even by one man and two women,</b> for there is no concern with regard to seclusion in a time of mourning. <b>And for</b> such an infant, people <b>do not stand in a line</b> to offer their condolences to the mourners, as is ordinarily done after a burial; <b>nor do</b> others <b>recite over him the mourners’ blessing,</b> which is recited in the courtyard of the graveyard after the burial; <b>nor</b> is the usual formula for the <b>consolation of mourners</b> recited during the seven days of mourning.
<b>A thirty-day-old</b> infant that dies <b>is taken out</b> for burial <b>in a coffin [<i>deluskema</i>]. Rabbi Yehuda says: Not in</b> a small <b>coffin that is carried on</b> one’s <b>shoulder, but rather</b> in a coffin that is carried <b>in the arms</b> of two people. <b>And for</b> such an infant, people <b>stand in a line</b> to offer their condolences to the mourners. <b>And</b> others <b>recite the mourners’ blessing</b> at the cemetery. <b>And</b> people recite <b>the consolation of mourners</b> during the week of mourning.
<b>A twelve-month-old</b> infant <b>is taken out</b> for burial <b>on a bier,</b> just as an adult is. <b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> This <i>halakha</i> applies if the infant that dies <b>is one year old and his limbs are like</b> those of <b>a two year old,</b> so that he looks older, or if <b>he is two years old and his limbs are like</b> those of <b>a one-year-old.</b> Only then <b>he is taken out on a bier.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: For one that is taken out on a bier, the public should grieve [<i>matzhivin</i>]. For one that is not taken out on a bier, the public need not grieve. Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says:</b> The <i>halakha</i> is as follows: If the infant was <b>known to the public,</b> because he regularly left the house and many people knew him, <b>the public must occupy themselves with him</b> and participate in his burial. If <b>he was not known to the public, the public need not occupy themselves with him.</b>
<b>And what is the</b> status of deceased infants <b>with regard to eulogy? Rabbi Meir said in the name of Rabbi Yishmael:</b> The children of <b>the poor</b> are eulogized from <b>the age of three,</b> whereas the children of <b>the wealthy</b> are eulogized from <b>the age of five.</b> This is because a child is the sole source of joy for the poor, and so the pain and grief of the poor over the death of a child is greater than that of the wealthy. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said in the name of</b> Rabbi Yishmael: The children of <b>the poor</b> are eulogized from <b>the age of five,</b> whereas the children of <b>the wealthy</b> are eulogized from <b>the age of six. And the children of the elderly are</b> treated <b>like the children of the poor,</b> for the death of a child is particularly painful for an older person.
<b>Rav Giddel bar Menashya said</b> that <b>Rav said: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who stated it in the name of Rabbi Yishmael.</b>
<b>Rabbi Anani bar Sason taught at the entrance to the house of the <i>Nasi</i>:</b> If one suffered the loss of a close relative shortly before the festival of <i>Shavuot</i>, his <b>one day</b> of mourning <b>before <i>Shavuot</i> and <i>Shavuot</i></b> itself together count as <b>fourteen</b> days. The day before the Festival counts as seven full days of mourning because the Festival interrupts the seven-day period of mourning, and the Festival itself is counted as a seven-day Festival. Even though it is a one-day Festival, it is counted as seven days because it has the same <i>halakha</i> as the other Festivals. After <i>Shavuot</i>, then, only sixteen days are left to complete the thirty-day mourning period. <b>Rabbi Ami heard</b> this <b>and became angry</b> with him. <b>He said: Is that to say</b> that it is <b>his own</b> opinion? <b>It is</b> the opinion <b>that Rabbi Elazar said</b> that <b>Rabbi Oshaya</b> said.
Similarly <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa taught in the pavilion [<i>kila</i>] of the Exilarch:</b> If one’s relative died shortly before the festival of <i>Shavuot</i>, his <b>one day</b> of mourning <b>before <i>Shavuot</i> and <i>Shavuot</i></b> itself count as <b>fourteen</b> days. <b>Rav Sheshet heard and became angry.</b> He <b>said: Is that to say</b> that it is <b>his own</b> opinion? <b>It is</b> the opinion <b>that Rabbi Elazar said</b> that <b>Rabbi Oshaya</b> said.
In both of these cases, a Sage presented an opinion as their own, without attributing it to the original Sage who stated it, <b>for Rabbi Elazar said</b> that <b>Rabbi Oshaya said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that the <i>Shavuot</i></b> offerings <b>have redress all seven</b> subsequent <b>days?</b> That is to say, if someone failed to bring the Festival offering on the Festival itself, he has six more days to bring it. <b>As it is stated:</b> “Three times a year shall all your males appear before the Lord your God in the place that He shall choose; <b>on the festival of Passover, and on the festival of <i>Shavuot</i>,</b> and on the festival of <i>Sukkot</i>” (Deuteronomy 16:16). <b>Just as</b> the offerings of <b>the festival of Passover have redress all seven</b> days, as Passover is seven days long, <b>so too</b> the offerings of <b>the festival of <i>Shavuot</i> have redress all seven</b> days, during the week following <i>Shavuot</i>. From this we learn that <i>Shavuot</i> is treated like a seven-day Festival for all halakhic purposes.
<b>Rav Pappa granted permission to Rav Avya the Elder</b> to speak <b>and he taught</b> the following in public: If one’s relative died shortly before Rosh HaShana, his <b>one day</b> of mourning <b>before Rosh HaShana and Rosh HaShana</b> itself together count as <b>fourteen</b> days, because Rosh HaShana is treated like a seven-day Festival. <b>Ravina said: Therefore,</b> if one’s relative died shortly before <i>Sukkot</i>, his <b>one day</b> of mourning <b>before the Festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i>, <b>and the Festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i> itself, which is seven days, <b>and its Eighth</b> day of Assembly, which is considered to be a separate Festival, count as <b>twenty-one</b> days, and he must observe only nine more days of mourning to complete the thirty-day period of mourning.
It was related that <b>Ravina happened</b> to come <b>to</b> the city of <b>Sura on the Euphrates</b> River. <b>Rav Ḥaviva from Sura on the Euphrates said to Ravina:</b> Did the <b>Master say</b> that <b>one day</b> of mourning <b>before Rosh HaShana and Rosh HaShana</b> itself count <b>as fourteen</b> days? <b>He said to him: I said that it stands to reason</b> that this is so <b>according to Rabban Gamliel,</b> who maintains that Rosh HaShana is treated like a pilgrim Festival. But I did not rule in accordance with this opinion.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> Mourners <b>do not rend</b> their garments during the intermediate days of a Festival <b>and do not remove</b> their garments from their shoulders. <b>And</b> others <b>do not provide them with a meal [<i>mavrin</i>]</b> after the burial, <b>except for close relatives of the deceased. And</b> the consolers <b>provide the</b> first <b>meal</b> after the burial <b>only</b> while the mourner is sitting <b>on an upright bed,</b> and not on one that is overturned.

Daf 25a

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that only the relatives of the deceased rend their clothes. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> is this the case <b>even</b> if the deceased was a Torah <b>Sage? But isn’t it taught</b> otherwise in a <i>baraita</i>: When a Torah <b>scholar dies, everyone is his relative.</b>
The Gemara clarifies: <b>Does it enter your mind</b> to say that <b>everyone is his relative? Rather,</b> this <i>baraita</i> should be understood as follows: <b>Everyone is</b> considered to be <b>like his relative</b> in the sense that <b>everyone rends his</b> garment in anguish <b>over him, and everyone bares</b> his shoulder <b>over him</b> in mourning, <b>and everyone eats the mourner’s meal over him in the public square</b> as mourners do. The death of a Torah scholar is a personal loss for every Jew. So why is the mishna limited to only relatives? The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> it is <b>necessary</b> for the mishna to teach this <i>halakha</i> in a case <b>where</b> the deceased <b>is not a</b> Torah <b>scholar.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And if</b> the deceased <b>was an upright person</b> who feared Heaven and performed good deeds, then aren’t all those present at his death <b>obligated to rend</b> their garments over his death? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>For what</b> reason <b>do a person’s sons and daughters die when they are young?</b> They die <b>so that he will cry and mourn</b> over the death of <b>an upright person.</b>
The Gemara questions the formulation: They die so that <b>he will cry and mourn? Is security,</b> i.e., his children, <b>taken from him</b> in advance to ensure that in the future he will mourn over the death of an upright person? <b>Rather</b> the <i>baraita</i> means as follows: His children died <b>because he did not cry or mourn over an upright person</b> who died. <b>As</b> with regard to <b>anyone who cries and mourns over an upright person</b> who died, <b>they forgive him</b> for <b>all his transgressions because of the honor he accorded</b> to the deceased. If this is the case, one also rends his clothes over an upright person. The Gemara answers: Rather, the mishna is referring only to one <b>who</b> was <b>not an upright person.</b>
The Gemara challenges: But <b>if one was standing there at the time of the soul’s departure,</b> i.e., at the time of death, he <b>is</b> also <b>obligated</b> to rend his clothes. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: One who stands over the deceased at the time of the soul’s departure is obligated to rend</b> his clothes. <b>To what may this be likened? To a Torah scroll that is burned,</b> for which anyone present <b>is obligated to rend</b> his clothes.
The Gemara answers: The mishna must be referring to a person <b>who was not standing there at the time of the soul’s departure</b> but who heard that someone who is not a close relative died, and the deceased was neither a Torah scholar nor an upright person.
§ The Gemara relates that <b>when Rav Safra passed away the</b> other <b>Sages did not rend</b> their garments <b>over him. They said: We did not learn from him,</b> as he did not disseminate his Torah knowledge to the public. <b>Abaye</b> berated them and <b>said to them: Is it taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i>: If one’s <b>teacher died? It is taught:</b> If a Torah <b>scholar died,</b> and Rav Safra was certainly a Torah scholar. <b>And furthermore, every day his teachings are in our mouths in the study hall,</b> so that even if we did not learn directly from him, we should still be considered his students.
The other Sages <b>thought that what was</b> done <b>was</b> done, and it was now too late for them to rend their garments. <b>Abaye said to them: We learned:</b> With regard to a Torah <b>scholar, as long as they are engaged in eulogizing</b> him, then people <b>are obligated to rend</b> their garments, even after the time of his death. <b>They</b> then <b>thought to rend</b> their garments <b>immediately. Abaye said to them: It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A</b> Torah <b>scholar’s honor is at</b> the time of <b>his eulogy,</b> and so you should wait until the time of the eulogy before rending your garments.
§ The Gemara relates another incident: <b>When Rav Huna died they thought to place a Torah scroll on his bier,</b> as was commonly done after the death of a Torah scholar, as if to say that the deceased fulfilled everything written in the scroll. <b>Rav Ḥisda said to them:</b> This is <b>a practice that he did not hold with during his lifetime; now should we stand up and do</b> it <b>for him</b> when he is dead? <b>As Rav Taḥlifa said: I</b> myself <b>saw Rav Huna, who wished to sit on his bed, and there was a Torah scroll placed on it. And he turned a jug over and placed the Torah scroll on it</b> so that he could then sit on the bed. <b>Apparently he holds</b> that <b>it is prohibited to sit on a bed upon which a Torah scroll lies.</b> Therefore, it would be inappropriate to lay a Torah scroll next to his body after he died.
When they tried to remove his corpse from his house for the burial, <b>the bier would not</b> fit <b>through the</b> narrow <b>door. They</b> then <b>thought to lower</b> the bier <b>from the roof. Rav Ḥisda said to them: This I learned from him,</b> Rav Huna himself: <b>A scholar’s honor</b> is for him to be taken out <b>through the</b> main <b>opening,</b> and not in any other manner.
<b>They</b> then <b>thought to move him from</b> his <b>bier to</b> a narrower <b>bier</b> so that it would fit through the door. But <b>Rav Ḥisda said to them: I learned from him,</b> Rav Huna himself, <b>as follows: A scholar’s honor is</b> for him to be taken out <b>on the first bier. As Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: From where</b> is it derived that <b>a scholar’s honor is</b> for him to be taken out <b>on the first bier? As it is stated: “And they set the Ark of God upon a new cart”</b> (II Samuel 6:3). When taking the Ark to Jerusalem, King David had it placed back on the cart upon which it had been returned by the Philistines, and a Torah scholar is considered to be similar to the Ark of the Covenant. When they saw that there was nothing else that they could do, <b>they broke the doorway and took him out</b> through it.
<b>Rabbi Abba opened</b> his eulogy for <b>him: Our Rabbi was worthy that the Divine Presence should rest upon him, except</b> for the fact <b>that Babylonia caused it</b> not to rest. In other words, it was only because he lived in Babylonia and not in Eretz Yisrael that the Divine Presence did not rest upon him.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Ḥisda raised an objection</b> against this, <b>and some say</b> that it was <b>Rav Ḥanan bar Ḥisda:</b> Is it not stated: <b>“The word of the Lord came [<i>hayo haya</i>] to Ezekiel the priest, son of Buzi, in the land of the Chaldeans”</b> (Ezekiel 1:3), thereby implying that a prophet can prophesy outside of Eretz Yisrael?
<b>His father tapped him with his sandal</b> on his foot, thereby hinting to him that he should be quiet. <b>He said to him: Have I not told you not to trouble everyone</b> with questions in the middle of a eulogy? The Gemara answers the question: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the doubling of the word <b>“came [<i>hayo haya</i>]”?</b> It implies <b>that it had already come</b> before, i.e., that Ezekiel had already begun to prophesy in Eretz Yisrael, and his prophecy in Babylonia was merely a continuation of that prophecy.
§ The Gemara relates that <b>when they took</b> Rav Huna <b>there,</b> to Eretz Yisrael, for burial <b>they said to Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi: Rav Huna has come,</b> and they misunderstood and thought that he was still alive. <b>They said: When we were there,</b> in Babylonia, we <b>did not have strength to lift our heads before him. Now</b> that <b>we have come here, has he come after us?</b>
<b>They said to them: His coffin has come. Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi went out</b> to meet his funeral procession. <b>Rabbi Ila and Rabbi Ḥanina did not go out. Some say</b> that <b>Rabbi Ila went out,</b> but <b>Rabbi Ḥanina did not go out.</b>
The Gemara asks: Those who <b>went out, what is</b> the <b>reason</b> that they went? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: When <b>a coffin is passing from place to place,</b> the people <b>stand in a line</b> to show respect <b>for</b> the deceased, <b>and they recite the mourners’ blessing and the consolation of the mourners over it.</b> Those <b>who did not go out, what is the reason</b> that they did not? <b>As it is taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: When a <b>coffin is passing from place to place, they do not stand in a line</b> to show respect <b>for</b> the deceased, <b>and they do not recite the mourners’ blessing or</b> the <b>consolation of the mourners for him.</b>
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>these</b> two tannaitic statements <b>contradict each other.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>It is not difficult: Here,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where the skeleton</b> of the deceased <b>is</b> still <b>intact,</b> and the mourning practices must be observed. <b>And there</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where the skeleton</b> of the deceased <b>is no longer intact,</b> and it is not necessary to observe the customs of mourning. <b>And Rav Huna’s skeleton was</b> still <b>intact.</b> The reason that the <b>one</b> Sage <b>did not go out</b> was that <b>they did not confirm for him</b> that the skeleton was still intact.
The Sages of Eretz Yisrael <b>said: Where shall we bury him?</b> They concluded: <b>Rav Huna disseminated Torah to</b> the people of <b>Israel, and</b> similarly <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya disseminated Torah to</b> the people of <b>Israel;</b> therefore, it is appropriate to bury Rav Huna next to Rabbi Ḥiyya.
They asked: <b>Who will take him in</b> to Rabbi Ḥiyya’s burial cave, as few are fit to enter it? <b>Rav Ḥagga said to them: I will take him into</b> the cave, <b>for I presented my studies</b> before him <b>when I was</b> just <b>eighteen, never having experienced a seminal emission.
